Meanings of the Ba in Basmala and Derivation of Ism
Explains the meanings of the ba in basmala and the derivation of 'ism'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible meanings of the letter ba in the basmala and the debate over the derivation of the word 'ism'.

Core Idea
A single letter can carry deep theological meaning.

This passage from the Sharh of Jawharat al-Tawhid explores the linguistic and theological dimensions of the basmala. The letter 'ba' is not merely grammatical; it carries layers of meaning about how we relate to Allah through His name. The author also delves into the origin of the word 'ism' itself, showing how grammar connects to creed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow. Notice the structure: first the ba, then the ism.
The Arabic contains footnote markers which are references.
2
Identify the two meanings of ba
Find the two possibilities: accompaniment (musahaba) and seeking help (isti'ana). Understand why accompaniment is considered preferable.
The author cites adab (propriety) as the reason for preference.
3
Grasp the allusive meaning
The allusive (ishari) interpretation connects the ba to divine attributes of perfection and transcendence.
This shows how grammar can hint at theology.
4
Understand the etymology of ism
Compare the Basran and Kufan views: from sumuww (height) or from wasama (mark).
The passage ends mid-sentence; we only have the beginning of the Kufan view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن الباء في البسملة إما للمصاحبة على وجه التبرك، أو للاستعانة كذلك (۳) ، ولا مانع من الاستعانة باسمه تعالى كما يستعان بذاته، والأولى جعلها للمصاحبة ؛ لأن جعلها للاستعانة فيه إساءة أدب؛ لأنَّ باء الاستعانة تدخل على الآلة، فيلزم عليها جعل اسم الله مقصوداً لغيره لا لذاته إلا أن يقال: إن من جعلها للاستعانة نظر إلى جهة أخرى ؛ وهي أن الفعل المشروع فيه لا يتم على الوجه الأكمل إلا باسمه تعالى، لكن قد يقال : مظنّة الإساءة ما زالت موجودة (۳) (2) ومعناها الإشاري بي كان ما كان وبي يكون ما يكون، وحينئذ يكون في الباء إشارة إلى جميع العقائد؛ لأن المراد بي وجد ما وجد، وبي يوجد ما يوجد، ولا يكون كذلك إلا من اتَّصف بصفات الكمال وتنزّه عن صفات النقصان(٥)، كما ذكره بعض أئمة التفسير ) والاسم مشتق عند البصريين من السُّمُو ؛ وهو العلو؛ لأنه يعلو مسماه، وعند الكوفيين من (وَسَمَ) بصيغة الماضي؛ أي: (عَلَمَ) بصيغة الماضي أيضاً (۷) ؛ لأن-
Then know that the ba in basmala is either for accompaniment (musahaba) for blessing, or for seeking help (isti‘ana) as well (3). There is no preventing seeking help by His name, just as one seeks help by His essence. The preferable is to make it for accompaniment, because making it for seeking help involves ill-literary (isa'at adab), because the ba of seeking help enters upon the instrument, which necessarily makes the name of God intended for something other than itself. Unless it is said that the one who makes it for seeking help considers another aspect: that the prescribed act is not completed in the most perfect way except by His name. But it may be said: the likelihood of ill-literary still exists (3). And its allusive meaning (ishari) is: by it (bi) was what was, and by it is what will be. Then in the ba there is an indication of all beliefs, because the meaning is: by it existed what existed, and by it exists what exists. And such is only one who is characterized by attributes of perfection and free from attributes of imperfection (5), as mentioned by some exegetes. And the word 'ism' according to the Basrans is derived from sumuww, which is highness, because it rises above its named object; and according to the Kufans from wasama (past tense), i.e. 'alama (past tense) (7); because...
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage discusses the meaning of the letter ba in basmala and the etymology of the word 'ism'. First, two possible meanings of the ba are presented: accompaniment (musahaba) for blessing, or seeking help (isti‘ana). Both are permissible, but the preferable (awla) is accompaniment, because seeking help uses the ba of instrumentality, which could imply that God's name is used as a means, potentially implying ill-literary toward God. A counterargument is that seeking help by the name is like seeking help by the essence, but the author notes that the suspicion of ill-literary remains. Then the passage mentions an allusive (ishari) interpretation: the ba signifies that all existence is through God and will be through God, indicating divine attributes of perfection and transcendence above imperfection. Finally, the etymology of 'ism' is given: Basrans derive it from sumuww (highness), Kufans from wasama (to mark/sign). The text breaks off mid-sentence at 'because'.

Key Terms
الباء للمصاحبة
الباء للاستعانة
التفسير الإشاري
الاسم
السمو
وَسَمَ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that seeking help by God's name is always the appropriate meaning of the ba in basmala.
Correction: The passage explains that accompaniment is preferable because seeking help can imply a lack of respect, treating God's name as an instrument.

Checkpoint
Why is making the ba in basmala for companionship (musahaba) considered preferable over seeking help (isti‘ana)?
A) Because seeking help is forbidden
B) Because seeking help implies using God's name as an instrument, which is ill-literary
C) Because companionship is the only valid meaning
D) Because the allusive meaning contradicts seeking help

Key Takeaway
The letter ba in basmala is preferably understood as accompaniment to maintain divine propriety, and the word ism derives either from 'highness' or 'mark'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Bismillah' before an action, do you mean that you are using God's name as a tool to get what you want, or are you acknowledging that you are accompanying God by His permission? Which meaning truly reflects your attitude in that moment?
?
The allusive meaning of the ba reminds you that everything exists through God. Do you live as though your own existence depends on Him every moment, or do you act independently? What specific thing have you done today that reveals your true belief?
?
The Basrans say 'ism' comes from 'highness'—the name rises above its object. Do you treat God's names as if they are above your own desires and purposes, or do you drag them down to serve your own goals? When you invoked His name today, was it to elevate your act or to lower His name to your level?

Derivation of the Word 'Ism' (Basran and Kufi Views)
Commentator explains the Basran and Kufi derivations of the word 'ism'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two linguistic derivations of the word 'ism' (name) according to the Basran and Kufi schools, and why the Kufi derivation is specifically from a verb.

Core Idea
The word 'ism' is either from 'highness' or 'marking' — both reveal something about naming.

Before diving into theological arguments, it helps to know the linguistic roots of key terms. Scholars have debated whether the word 'ism' (name) comes from the root meaning 'highness' (sumuww) or 'marking' (wasm). This passage presents both views and critiques a common simplification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Get a feel for the two views.
2
Identify the two derivations
Find the Basran derivation (from sumuww) and the Kufi derivation (from wasama). Notice how each school justifies its choice.
The Basrans focus on the name's elevation over the named; the Kufis insist on derivation from verbs.
3
Understand the Kufi principle
The Kufis derive all nouns from verbs. That's why they say 'ism' comes from the past tense verb wasama (he marked), not from the noun wasm (mark).
This principle is key to their entire theory of derivation.
4
Note the criticism
The commentator says some scholars' statement that it's from wasm (mark) is 'too lenient' because it overlooks the verb-root requirement.
5
Summarize the meaning
Conclude: 'ism' essentially means 'that which indicates the named entity'.
Both derivations ultimately agree on the function.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاسم مشتق عند البصريين من السُّمُو؛ وهو العلو؛ لأنه يعلو مسماه، وعند الكوفيين من (وَسَمَ) بصيغة الماضي؛ أي: (عَلَمَ) بصيغة الماضي أيضاً؛ لأن الاشتقاق عندهم من الأفعال، فقولُ بعض العلماء: (وعند الكوفيين من الوسم بمعنى العلامة) فيه تسمح، ومعناه: ما دل على مسمى.
And the word 'ism' is derived according to the Basrans from sumuww, which is highness, because it rises above its named entity. According to the Kufis, it is derived from wasama in the past tense, meaning 'alama' (he marked) also in the past tense, because derivation for them is from verbs. So the statement of some scholars: 'And according to the Kufis, from wasm meaning mark' is too lenient. And its meaning: that which indicates the named entity.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage defines the linguistic derivation of the word 'ism' (name). The commentator presents two views: the Basran school derives it from sumuww (highness) because the name is elevated above the named object. The Kufi school derives it from wasm (marking) because derivation in their view is from verbs. The author criticizes some scholars who say it is from wasm meaning 'mark' as being too lenient. He concludes that the essential meaning of 'ism' is that which indicates the named entity.

Key Terms
الاسم
السمو
الوسم
البصريون
الكوفيون
الاشتقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think derivation of 'ism' is from a noun root (like wasm) for the Kufis.
Correction: The Kufis insist that derivation must be from a verb; thus 'ism' is from the verb wasama, not the noun wasm.

Checkpoint
From what do the Basrans derive the word 'ism'?
A) From wasm meaning mark.
B) From sumuww meaning highness.
C) From 'alama meaning knowledge.
D) From samaa meaning sky.

Key Takeaway
The word 'ism' is derived either from highness (Basran) or from a verb meaning to mark (Kufi), and its essential meaning is that which indicates a named entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by His names, do you think of them as reflecting His highness above all perfection (Basran view) or as marks that distinguish His attributes from creation (Kufi view)? How does your perspective affect the reverence in your du'a?
?
The commentator corrects a lenient claim about the Kufi derivation. Are there areas in your understanding of Allah's names where you have accepted an oversimplified or lenient view without verifying? What will you do today to correct one such understanding?
?
If every name you use for someone or something carries an intrinsic meaning from its root, have you ever considered that your own name and the names you give to others shape how you see them? How can this awareness change the way you speak about people and about Allah?

Distinction Between Ism, Musamma, and Tasmiya
Clarification of the grammatical distinction between ism and musamma and reconciliation of two opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between a name (ism) and its referent (musamma) and how the two apparently conflicting views on their identity can be reconciled.

Core Idea
Ism differs from musamma unless ism means the referent.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid tackles a subtle but important distinction between the word we use (ism) and the thing it refers to (musamma). While grammar treats them as separate, some theologians treat them as identical. The commentator explains how both views can be reconciled depending on what we mean by 'name.' Let's read closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Note the key terms: الاسم, المسمى, المدلول.
The passage is short but dense. Take your time.
2
Identify the two opposing views
The commentator first presents the grammatical definition that implies ism and musamma are distinct. Then he mentions that if ism means the referent, they are the same. Notice the phrase 'نعم إن أريد به المدلول' which signals the reconciliation.
3
Reflect on the reconciliation
Understand that the reconciliation hinges on the meaning of 'ism' itself. When ism is taken as the word, it is distinct; when taken as the referent, it is identical. This resolves the apparent disagreement between scholars.
The commentator affirms the distinctness as the verified position but allows for the alternative interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم: (كلمةٌ دَلَّتْ على معنى في نفسها .... إلى آخره، فهو اصطلاح نحوي، وعُلمَ من التعريف المذكور أن الاسم غير المسمَّى، وهو التحقيق؛ نعم إن أريد به المدلول، كان عين المسمى، وبهذا يُجمع بين القولين.
As for their statement: 'A word that indicates a meaning in itself... etc.' that is a grammatical terminology. And it is known from the aforementioned definition that the name (ism) is other than the named (musamma), and this is the verified position. Yes, if by the name the referent is intended, then it is the same as the named, and by this the two opinions are reconciled.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage clarifies the distinction between ism (the word) and musamma (the named entity). The commentator notes that the grammatical definition of ism as a word indicating a meaning in itself implies that the word is not identical to its referent. However, if by ism one means the referent itself (the meaning), then they are the same. This reconciles seemingly conflicting views: some scholars say ism is identical to musamma, others say it is distinct. The commentator affirms the distinctness as the verified position, but allows reconciliation by noting the two possible meanings of 'ism'.

Key Terms
الاسم
المسمى
التسمية
المدلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that the word (ism) is always the same as the thing it refers to (musamma).
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the word is distinct from its referent unless by 'ism' we mean the referent itself. In normal usage, they are different.

Checkpoint
How can the two divergent opinions on ism and musamma be reconciled?
A) They cannot be reconciled.
B) If ism means the word, it is different; if it means the referent, it is the same.
C) Both say ism equals musamma.
D) The Kufis and Basrans agree.

Key Takeaway
The name (ism) and the named (musamma) are distinct unless 'ism' is taken to mean the referent itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by His names, do you ever stop to consider that the name you utter is not the same as His infinite reality? What would change in your worship if you truly internalized that the 'ism' (name) is distinct from the 'musamma' (the Named)?
?
Think of a situation where you mistook a label or description for the full truth of a person or concept. How did that limited understanding affect your judgment? What will you do to move from knowing the name to truly knowing the named?
?
The passage shows that scholars reconciled two opinions by clarifying the intended meaning of a single word. Where in your own life have you clung to one understanding of an Islamic term without considering its possible shades of meaning? Will you now seek to understand the deeper intention behind the words you use?

Linguistic Definition of Hamd and Its Four Categories
Definition of hamd with examples and four categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic definition of hamd, its two types based on blessings, and the four categories of praise (eternal and contingent).

Core Idea
Hamd is verbal praise of the good—whether or not it is for a blessing—and spans both eternal and created praise.

In this passage, the commentator defines hamd linguistically and gives examples to distinguish praise in return for a blessing from praise without such context. He then uses the term 'kalam' to ensure the definition covers both God's eternal praise and human praise, leading to four categories. This opening is foundational for understanding the terminology used throughout the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation together
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms like الحمد, الثناء, الكلام.
2
Distinguish the two examples
Identify the two examples given: one for praise in return for a blessing (Zayd's generosity) and one without (Zayd's prayer). Understand how they illustrate the definition.
The author clarifies that hamd can be both for blessings and for other voluntary good actions.
3
Understand the four categories
Note why the author uses 'kalam' instead of 'lisan' to include eternal praise. Then list the four categories: eternal for eternal, eternal for contingent, contingent for eternal, contingent for contingent.
This categorization is key for later discussions on divine praise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحمدُ لغةٌ : الثناء بالكلام على الجميل الاختياري على جهة التبجيل والتعظيم، سواء كان في مقابلة نعمة أم لا، فمثال الأول ما إذا أكرمك زيد، فقلت: زيد كريم؛ فإنه في مقابلة نعمة؛ ومثال الثاني ما إذا وجدت زيداً يصلي صلاةً تامَّةٌ، فقلت: زيد رجلٌ صالح ; فإنه ليس في مقابلة نعمة . والثناء بتقديم المثلثة على النون : هو الإتيان بما يدلُّ على التعظيم، وقيل: هو الذكرُ بخير، وضدُّهُ : النثاء بتقديم النون على المثلثة. وإنما عبرنا بالكلام كما عبّرَ به بعضُ المحققين ليشمل التعريف حينئذ الحَمْدَ القديم؛ وهو حمد الله نفسه بنفسه، وحمده لأنبيائه وأوليائه وأصفيائه، والحمد الحادث ; وهو حمدنا الله تعالى، وحمد بعضنا البعض، فدخلَتْ أقسام الحمد الأربعة؛ وهي : حمد قديم لقديم، وحمد قديم لحادث، وحمد حادث لقديم، وحمد حادث لحادث (۱)؛ وأما تعبير بعضهم باللسان، فيلزم عليه ألا يكون التعريف شاملاً للقديم، إلا أن يُراد باللسان الكلام على سبيل المجاز المرسل؛ من إطلاق السبب وهو وإرادة المسبَّبِ وهو الكلام، ولا يرد أن التعاريف تُصانُ عن المجاز؛ لأن محل ذلك ما لم يكن المجاز مشهوراً كما هنا .
And 'al-hamd' linguistically is: verbal praise of a voluntary beautiful action in a manner of veneration and glorification, whether it is in return for a blessing or not. An example of the first is if Zayd honors you, you say: Zayd is generous; then it is in return for a blessing. An example of the second is if you find Zayd performing a perfect prayer, you say: Zayd is a righteous man; then it is not in return for a blessing. And 'al-thana' (with the occurrence of the letter like) preceding the noon: is to produce what indicates glorification, and it is said: it is mentioning with goodness, and its opposite is 'al-natha' (with the noon preceding the like). And we only used 'kalam' (speech) as some investigators expressed it, to include in the definition then the eternal praise: which is God's praise of Himself, and His praise of His prophets, saints, and chosen ones; and the contingent praise: which is our praise of God, and our praise of each other. So the four categories of praise enter: eternal praise for eternal, eternal praise for contingent, contingent praise for eternal, and contingent praise for contingent. As for the expression of some by 'lisan' (tongue), it would follow that the definition does not include the eternal, unless 'lisan' is intended to mean speech by way of metonymy (majaz mursal), by applying the cause (tongue) and intending the effect (speech), and it is not objected that definitions are free from metaphor, because that applies only where the metaphor is not well-known as here.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage provides the linguistic definition of 'hamd'. The author defines it as verbal praise (thana' bi al-kalam) of a voluntary beautiful action in a way that shows veneration and glorification, whether or not it is in response to a blessing. He gives examples to illustrate both cases. He then clarifies the meaning of 'thana' (praise) and its opposite 'natha' (blame). Next, he justifies using the word 'kalam' in the definition instead of 'lisan' (tongue) to include the eternal praise of God and the contingent praise, which yields four categories of hamd: eternal to eternal, eternal to contingent, contingent to eternal, and contingent to contingent. He addresses a potential objection that definitions should be free from metaphor, noting that well-known metaphors are acceptable.

Key Terms
الحمد
الثناء
الكلام
اللسان
المجاز المرسل
الأقسام الأربعة
القديم
الحادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hamd is only given in return for a blessing.
Correction: The passage defines hamd as praise of a voluntary beautiful action whether or not it is in return for a blessing, as shown by the example of praising someone for their prayer without any personal benefit.

Checkpoint
Why does the author use 'kalam' in the definition of hamd instead of 'lisan'?
A) 'Lisan' is a more accurate term
B) To include eternal praise, since 'lisan' would exclude it except metaphorically
C) Because 'kalam' is the only word for speech
D) To make the definition longer

Key Takeaway
Hamd is verbal praise of voluntary good actions with veneration, and it can be eternal or contingent, producing four categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise someone for a good deed, do you consciously recognize that your praise is a form of hamd that can be either for a blessing or not? How does this affect your sincerity?
?
If Allah praises His own prophets and saints, what does that tell you about the importance of praising others with veneration? Is your praise of others free from envy or self-interest?
?
Since hamd can be eternal (God's praise of Himself), do you ever feel that your own praise of Allah is lacking in the veneration it deserves? What would it take for you to increase the quality of your hamd?

The Voluntary Condition and Pillars of Hamd
The author explains the condition 'ikhtiyari' and lists the five pillars of hamd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition 'ikhtiyari' in the definition of hamd, the distinction between hamd and madh, and the five pillars of hamd.

Core Idea
Hamd is praise for voluntary beauty; it has five pillars.

This passage deepens our understanding of hamd by examining the condition 'ikhtiyari' and excluding involuntary praise. The author also clarifies the relationship between hamd and madh, and then enumerates the five essential components of hamd. Let's explore these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition 'ikhtiyari'
Read the passage and note that hamd is only for voluntary beauty. Involuntary beauty receives madh, not hamd.
The example of the pearl illustrates this.
2
Distinguish hamd from madh
Notice the author's discussion of al-Zamakhshari's view and the clarification that hamd implies volition.
3
Learn the five pillars
Identify the five pillars listed: al-hamid, al-mahmud, al-mahmud bihi, al-mahmud 'alayh, and al-sigha.
4
Examine the two attributes
Understand how the praised-with and praised-for attributes can be identical in essence but differ in consideration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولنا على الجميل (الاختياري) أي: لأجل الجميل الاختياري، ولو كان جميلاً في اعتقاد المحمود بزعم الحامد، وإن لم يكن جميلاً شرعاً؛ كنهب الأموال (۲)، وخرج بقيد (الاختياري) الاضطراري؛ فإن الثناء عليه يسمى مدحاً لا حمداً ، تقول : مدحت اللؤلؤةً على حسنها، دون حمدتُها، وقال الزمخشري: (الحمدُ والمدح أخوان بمعنى: أنهما مترادفان. والاختياري إنما هو قيد في المحمود عليه لا في المحمود به، فقد يكون المحمود عليه اختياريًّا، والمحمود به اضطراريًا، كما إذا أكرمك زيد ; فقلت : زيد حسن (ع) وأركان الحمد خمسة : حامد، ومحمود، ومحمود به، ومحمود عليه، وصيغة. ثم اعلم أن المحمود به والمحمود عليه قد يتحدان ذاتاً ويختلفان اعتباراً؛ كما إذا أكرمك زيدٌ ; فقلت : زيد كريم; فإن الكرم من حيث كونه باعثاً على الحمد يقال له : محمود عليه، ومن حيث كونُهُ مدلول الصيغة يقال له : محمود به، وقد يختلفان ذاتا واعتبارا.
And our saying 'of the voluntary beautiful' i.e., because of the voluntary beautiful, even if it is beautiful in the belief of the praised according to the claim of the praiser, even if it is not legally beautiful, like the plunder of wealth. And by the restriction 'voluntary' the involuntary is excluded; for praise of it is called 'madh' not 'hamd'. You say: I praised the pearl for its beauty, but I do not say: I praised (hamd) it. And al-Zamakhshari said: 'Hamd and madh are two brothers', meaning they are synonyms. And the voluntary is a restriction on the praised-for attribute not on the praised-with attribute. So there may be the praised-for attribute voluntary and the praised-with attribute involuntary, as when Zayd honors you and you say: Zayd is good. And the pillars of hamd are five: the praiser, the praised, that with which praise is given, that for which praise is given, and the form. Then know that that with which praise is given and that for which praise is given may be identical in essence but differ in consideration, as when Zayd honors you and you say: Zayd is generous; then generosity, from the aspect of being the motive for praise, is called 'praised-for attribute', and from the aspect of being the meaning of the form, it is called 'praised-with attribute', and they may differ in essence and consideration.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage continues the explanation of the definition of 'hamd'. The author focuses on the phrase 'of the voluntary beautiful' (ala al-jamil al-ikhtiyari). He explains that the beauty may be according to the belief of the one who praises, even if it is not legally beautiful, like the plunder of wealth by thieves. He emphasizes that the restriction 'voluntary' excludes involuntary actions; praising such actions is called 'madh' (eulogy) not 'hamd'. He mentions al-Zamakhshari's statement that hamd and madh are synonyms, but the author implies a distinction. Then he clarifies that the voluntary condition applies to the attribute for which praise is given (al-mahmud 'alayh) not to the attribute by which praise is given (al-mahmud bihi). He gives an example where the motive is voluntary but the expression is involuntary. After that, he enumerates the five pillars of hamd: the praiser, the praised, the attribute by which praise is given, the attribute for which praise is given, and the verbal form. He then explains the possible identity or difference between the praised-with and praised-for attributes, providing an example with generosity and form.

Key Terms
الجميل الاختياري
الاضطراري
المدح
أركان الحمد
الحامد
المحمود
المحمود به
المحمود عليه
الصيغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the voluntary condition applies to the act of praising itself.
Correction: The author clarifies that it applies to the attribute for which praise is given (the motive), not the act of praising.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the five pillars of hamd?
A) Praiser, praised, praise, reward, intention
B) Praiser, praised, that with which praise is given, that for which praise is given, and the form
C) Praiser, praised, blessing, beauty, speech
D) Heart, tongue, action, intention, sincerity

Key Takeaway
Hamd is praise for voluntary beauty, has five pillars, and the motive attribute must be voluntary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'alhamdulillah', do you truly mean it as praise for Allah's voluntary, perfect attributes, or has it become a mindless habit? This lesson demands that you examine your hamd: is it based on understanding His voluntary beauty, or is it mere madh? Commit today to pause before each hamd and reflect on the voluntary kindness you are acknowledging.
?
The passage says hamd can be for what is beautiful in your belief, even if not legally beautiful. Are there things you call Allah 'good' that you haven't verified from revelation? Are you praising Him for what you assume is good, risking a false hamd? Let this lesson press you to align your praise only with His revealed attributes of perfection.
?
The distinction between hamd and madh reveals that Allah's praise must be for His voluntary actions. Will you stop praising Him for involuntary things like tests or hardships as if He did them without wisdom? This week, challenge yourself: when difficulty comes, consciously praise Him for His voluntary mercy, not for the trial itself—because hamd is only for the voluntary beautiful.

The Five Pillars of Hamd and the Distinction Between Instrument and Object of Praise
The author enumerates the five pillars of hamd and explains the identity and difference of two of them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five essential components of any act of praise (hamd) and how the instrument of praise (mahmud bihi) and the object of praise (mahmud 'alayhi) can be identical or distinct in essence and consideration.

Core Idea
Praise has five pillars; the cause and content of praise can be the same or different.

To properly understand the theology of praise (hamd) in Islamic thought, one must first recognize its constituent parts. The author lays out the five pillars that every act of praise contains, and then clarifies a potential confusion between two of them—the instrument by which praise is expressed and the object that incites praise. This lesson will walk you through these concepts with clear examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the five pillars
The passage lists five pillars: praiser, praised, instrument of praise, object of praise, and formula. Make sure you can name each one in Arabic and English.
3
Understand the two scenarios
The passage explains two cases: when the instrument and object are identical in essence but differ in consideration, and when they differ entirely. Pay attention to the examples given.
The first example uses generosity, the second uses knowledge.
4
Apply the distinction
Think about how this distinction applies when praising Allah. What is the cause of praise? What is the attribute used in praise?
This will help you connect the concept to actual theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأركان الحمد خمسة : حامد، ومحمود، ومحمود به، ومحمود عليه، وصيغة. ثم اعلم أن المحمود به والمحمود عليه قد يتحدان ذاتاً ويختلفان اعتباراً؛ كما إذا أكرمك زيدٌ ; فقلت : زيد كريم؛ فإن الكرم من حيث كونه باعثاً على الحمد يقال له : محمود عليه، ومن حيث كونُهُ مدلول الصيغة يقال له : محمود به، وقد يختلفان ذاتاً واعتباراً؛ كما إذا أكرمك زيد ; فقلت زيد عالم فإن المحمود عليه هو الكرم، والمحمود به هو العلم.
The pillars of hamd are five: the praiser, the praised, the instrument of praise, the object of praise, and the formula. Then know that the instrument of praise and the object of praise may unite in essence but differ in consideration; as when Zaid honors you, and you say 'Zaid is generous' — for generosity, in terms of being a cause for praise, is called the object of praise (mahmud 'alayhi), and in terms of being the denotation of the formula, it is called the instrument of praise (mahmud bihi). And they may differ in essence and consideration; as when Zaid honors you, and you say 'Zaid is knowledgeable' — then the object of praise is generosity, and the instrument of praise is knowledge.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and clarification. The author lists the five essential components (arkan) of any act of praise: the praiser (hamid), the praised (mahmud), the instrument of praise (mahmud bihi) — the attribute or phrase used, the object of praise (mahmud 'alayhi) — the reason or cause for praise, and the formula (sigha) — the wording. Then he focuses on the relationship between the mahmud bihi and the mahmud 'alayhi. They can be identical in essence but differ in consideration, as in the example where generosity is both the cause (mahmud 'alayhi) because it prompted the praise, and the instrument (mahmud bihi) because it is the content of the praise formula. Alternatively, they can differ in both essence and consideration, as when the cause is generosity (the voluntary act of Zaid) but the instrument is knowledge (an attribute of Zaid, which is not the direct cause).

Key Terms
أركان (arkan)
حامد (hamid)
محمود (mahmud)
محمود به (mahmud bihi)
محمود عليه (mahmud 'alayhi)
صيغة (sigha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the instrument and cause of praise are always the same.
Correction: The author shows they can differ: in the second example, generosity is the cause but knowledge is the instrument.

Checkpoint
In the example 'Zaid is generous' when Zaid has honored you, what is the mahmud 'alayhi?
A) Generosity
B) Knowledge
C) Zaid
D) The formula

Key Takeaway
Praise has five essential pillars; the reason for praise (mahmud 'alayhi) and the attribute used in praise (mahmud bihi) may coincide or diverge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise Allah, do you consider the true reason (mahmud 'alayhi) behind your praise? Or do you simply repeat formulas without connecting them to His actions or attributes that prompted that praise?
?
Think of a recent time you praised someone. Did the attribute you used (mahmud bihi) genuinely align with the reason (mahmud 'alayhi) you praised them? Or were you just using a nice phrase? How can you ensure your praises are truthful and from the heart?
?
The passage distinguishes between cause and instrument of praise. In your relationship with Allah, are you aware that His attributes are both the cause and the content of your praise? Does this awareness change how you say 'Alhamdulillah'?

Nine Meanings of 'Al-Hamdu Lillah'
Enumeration of possible meanings of 'al' and 'lam' in 'al-hamdu lillah'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nine possible interpretations of the phrase 'al-hamdu lillah' based on the meanings of 'al' and 'lam', and why one combination is impossible.

Core Idea
Three meanings of 'al' times three of 'lam' gives nine possibilities, but one is ruled out by logic.

The opening phrase of the Fatiha, 'al-hamdu lillah', is not just a statement of praise but a phrase rich with interpretive possibilities. Classical scholars analyzed the definite article 'al' and the prepositional 'lam' to uncover multiple layers of meaning. This passage from Sanusi's commentary presents a precise enumeration of those possibilities and identifies one combination that cannot be valid.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Get a sense of the author's methodical approach.
Pay attention to the terms used for each meaning.
2
Identify the three meanings of 'al'
The passage lists three possibilities for 'al' in 'al-hamdu': total inclusion, genus, and previous covenant. Understand each one.
These are technical terms from usul al-fiqh and Arabic grammar.
3
Identify the three meanings of 'lam'
Similarly, the 'lam' in 'lillah' can mean entitlement, exclusivity, or ownership. Grasp the distinctions.
These meanings reflect theological nuances about to whom praise belongs.
4
Understand the nine combinations and the impossible one
Multiply the three by three to get nine combinations. Note which one is rejected: 'lam' for ownership with 'al' for previous covenant.
The impossibility is due to logical contradiction: ownership implies generality, while previous covenant implies specificity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن (أل) في الحمد إما للاستغراق، أو للجنس، أو للعهد، واللام : في (الله) إما للاستحقاق، أو للاختصاص، أو للملك ; فيتحصَّلُ من ذلك احتمالات تسعة قائمة من ضرب ثلاثة في ثلاثة، يمتنع منها جعل اللام للملك مع جعل (أل) للعهد.
Then know that 'al' in 'al-hamdu' is either for total inclusion, or for the genus, or for a previous covenant; and the 'lam' in 'lillah' is either for entitlement, or for exclusivity, or for ownership. So from that come nine possibilities arising from multiplying three by three, of which it is impossible to make the 'lam' for ownership while making 'al' for a previous covenant.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage analyzes the grammatical and theological possibilities in the phrase 'al-hamdu lillah' (Praise be to Allah). The author states that the definite article 'al' in 'al-hamdu' can have three meanings: (1) الاستغراق (total inclusion, i.e., all praise), (2) الجنس (the genus, i.e., praise as a concept), or (3) العهد (previous covenant, i.e., a specific praise known from context). The lam in 'lillah' can have three meanings: (1) الاستحقاق (entitlement, i.e., praise is deserved by Allah), (2) الاختصاص (exclusivity, i.e., praise is exclusively for Allah), or (3) الملك (ownership, i.e., praise belongs to Allah). Multiplying the three possibilities of 'al' by the three of 'lam' gives nine combinations. One combination is logically impossible: making the lam for ownership while making al for a previous covenant, because if praise is owned by Allah, it cannot be a specific praise known from some previous context (it would be general). The author does not state the remaining eight but implies they are possible.

Key Terms
أل
لام
استغراق
جنس
عهد
استحقاق
اختصاص
ملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'al-hamdu lillah' has only one fixed meaning.
Correction: The classical scholars identified nine possible interpretations based on the grammatical meanings of 'al' and 'lam', with only one combination ruled out.

Checkpoint
Which combination of 'al' and 'lam' is considered impossible?
A) Al for genus and lam for entitlement
B) Al for total inclusion and lam for exclusivity
C) Al for previous covenant and lam for ownership
D) Al for genus and lam for ownership

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'al-hamdu lillah' admits eight valid interpretations from three meanings of 'al' and three of 'lam', with one combination logically excluded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'al-hamdu lillah', which of the eight valid interpretations resonates most with your current spiritual state? Are you praising Him out of a sense of ownership, entitlement, or exclusivity? Challenge yourself to incorporate all eight meanings into your daily praise.
?
If the one impossible combination teaches us that Allah's ownership cannot be confined to a specific covenant, how does this expand your understanding of His lordship? Do you ever limit your praise to certain times or places, forgetting that He owns all praise at all times?
?
Consider your last utterance of 'al-hamdu lillah'. Did you mean it as a general, all-encompassing praise, or was it limited to a specific blessing? How can you deepen your intention to include the full scope of meanings?

The Meaning of 'Salam' as Greeting
Commentator defines 'salam' as greeting and rejects security interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator insists that 'salam' in the phrase 'Salam Allah' means a greeting of honor rather than security, and how this relates to the Prophet's status and fear of Allah.

Core Idea
‘Salam’ to the Prophet is a supreme greeting, not a promise of safety.

In this commentary on the poem 'Jawharat al-Tawhid', the author explains the meaning of 'Salam Allah' when addressing the Prophet. He argues that it is not merely a wish for security, but a greeting that befits Allah's majesty and the Prophet's lofty rank. This distinction highlights the proper respect due to the Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the Arabic terms like 'السلام' and 'التحية'.
2
Understand the definition of 'salam'
The commentator defines 'salam' as a supreme greeting (تحية) that befits Allah and is specific to Him. This greeting is greatest because the Prophet is the greatest creation.
3
Grasp why security interpretation is rejected
The commentator rejects interpreting 'salam' as security because it might imply the Prophet could be in a state of fear. The Prophet's fear is only of awe and veneration, not of harm.
This clarifies the respectful meaning of 'salam'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعنى (سلام الله أي تحيَّتُه اللائقة به و بحسب ما عنده تعالى، كما تشعر به إضافته له تعالى، فالمطلوب تحيةٌ عظمى بلغت الدرجة القصوى، فتكون أعظم التحيات؛ لأنه أعظم المخلوقات؛ والمراد بالتحية في حقه صلى الله عليه وسلم كما أفاده السنوسي في شرح الجزائرية»: أن يُسمِعَهُ كلامَهُ القديم الدال على رفعة مقامِهِ العظيم ولم يرتض بعضُهم تفسير (السلام) بالأمن وإن ذكره السنوسي وغيره (۳) ؛ لأنه ربما أشعرَ بمَظنَّة الخوف مع أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم - بل وأتباعه - لا خوف عليهم، نعم يخاف صلى الله عليه وسلم خوف مهابة وإجلال، ولذلك قال : «إنِّي لأخوفُكُمْ مِنَ اللهِ (٤) .
And the meaning of 'peace of Allah' is His greeting befitting Him and according to what He possesses, as indicated by its attribution to Him. So what is sought is a supreme greeting that has reached the highest degree, thus it is the greatest of greetings because he is the greatest of creations. And what is meant by greeting regarding him, peace be upon him, as al-Sanusi stated in his commentary on al-Jaza'iriyya, is that He should make him hear His eternal speech indicating the loftiness of his great station. And some did not approve of interpreting 'peace' as security, although al-Sanusi and others mentioned it, because it might imply fear, while the Prophet, peace be upon him, and even his followers, have no fear upon them. Yes, the Prophet fears fear of awe and veneration, and therefore he said: 'Indeed, I am the most fearful of you of Allah.'
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a definition of 'سلام الله' in the poem. The commentator explains that 'salam' here means a greeting (تحية) that befits Allah and is specific to Him. This greeting is supreme, reaching the highest degree, and is the greatest of greetings because the Prophet is the greatest of creation. The commentator then specifies what this greeting entails according to al-Sanusi: that Allah makes the Prophet hear His eternal speech, which indicates the loftiness of his station. The commentator then rejects the interpretation of 'salam' as security (أمن), despite its mention by al-Sanusi and others. The reason is that security might imply the possibility of fear, whereas the Prophet and his followers are secure from fear. However, the Prophet does have fear of Allah, but it is a fear of awe and veneration (خوف مهابة وإجلال), not fear of harm or punishment. The commentator quotes the Prophet's own statement: 'إني لأخوفكم من الله' to emphasize this point.

Key Terms
السلام
التحية
الأمن
مهابة
إجلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'salam' in this context means security from punishment or harm.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that 'salam' is a greeting of honor, not a grant of safety. The Prophet has no fear of harm, only fear of Allah's awe.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject interpreting 'salam' as 'security (أمن)'?
A) Because the Prophet does not need security
B) Because it might imply the Prophet could be in a state of fear
C) Because al-Sanusi did not approve of it
D) Because security is not a greeting

Key Takeaway
The 'salam' for the Prophet is a greatest greeting, not a grant of immunity, and his fear is one of awe, not insecurity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'السلام عليك أيها النبي' in your prayers, do you feel you are offering a mere wish for his safety, or do you sense that you are acknowledging his supreme rank and asking Allah to honor him? Which understanding moves you to send more blessings upon him?
?
The Prophet said, 'إني لأخوفكم من الله'. Is your fear of Allah one of awe and reverence, or is it a fear of punishment? How does this lesson challenge your perception of fear in your relationship with Allah?
?

The Meaning of Allah's Salah: Mercy with Glorification
Commentator defines 'salah' and discusses two interpretations: mercy with glorification vs absolute mercy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of 'salah' when attributed to Allah and why the preferred view includes glorification.

Core Idea
Allah's salah is mercy with glorification, not mere mercy.

In this passage, the commentator explains a subtle but important point about the word 'salah' when used for Allah. Is it simply mercy, or is it more? The answer affects how we understand a key Quranic verse. Let's dive in.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the pronunciation note and the two interpretations.
Pay attention to the phrase 'اللغة القليلة' which sets up the discussion.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The commentator presents two meanings: 1) mercy with glorification (preferred) and 2) absolute mercy. Note the reasoning for each.
The preferred view is linked to the context of the poem and the pronoun reference.
3
Understand the Quranic implication
See how the difference affects the interpretation of the conjunction in Quran 2:157. The first view makes it 'general to specific', the second makes it 'explanatory'.
This shows how theological nuance influences Quranic exegesis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (مَعْ صَلَاتِهِ) بإسكان العين هنا على اللغة القليلة لأجل الوزن، وإن كان الأفصح فتحها . ومعنى (صلاته) رحمته المقرونة بالتعظيم كما تشعر به الإضافة إلى ضميره تعالى، وهذا هو اللائق بالمقام، وقيل هي مطلق الرحمة؛ سواء قرنت بالتعظيم أم لا، لكن هذا بيان للصلاة في حد ذاتها بقطع النظر عن المقام، وينبني على هذا الخلافِ العطفُ في قوله تعالى : (أُولَٰئِكَ عَلَيْهِمْ صَلَوَاتٌ مِّن رَّبِّهِمْ وَرَحْمَةٌ) [البقرة : ١٥٧]؛ فعلى الأول : يكون من عطف العام على الخاص، وعلى الثاني : من عطف التفسير .
His statement: 'with His prayer' with the 'ayn in sukoon here in the less common dialect for the sake of meter, even though the more eloquent is to open it. And the meaning of 'His prayer' is His mercy accompanied by glorification, as indicated by the attribution to His pronoun, and this is befitting the context. And it is said it is absolute mercy, whether accompanied by glorification or not, but this is an explanation of prayer in itself, regardless of context. And upon this difference is based the conjunction in His statement: 'Those, upon them are prayers from their Lord and mercy' [al-Baqara 2:157]; thus, according to the first view, it is conjunction of the general to the specific; and according to the second, it is conjunction of explanation.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological explanation of the word 'مع صلاته' (with His prayer) in the poem. The commentator first addresses the pronunciation of 'مع' (with), noting that it is pronounced with sukun on the 'ayn for meter, although the more common and eloquent pronunciation is with fatha. Then he explains the meaning of 'صلاته' (His prayer). The preferred interpretation is that it refers to Allah's mercy accompanied by glorification (التعظيم), as indicated by the pronoun 'his' referring to Allah. This is appropriate to the context. An alternative view is that it is absolute mercy, regardless of glorification. The commentator notes that this second view explains the word 'صلاة' in itself, apart from the context. This difference has implications for understanding the Quranic verse al-Baqara 2:157. According to the first view (mercy with glorification), the word 'صلوات' (prayers) is specific, and 'رحمة' (mercy) is general, so the conjunction is of the general to the specific. According to the second view (absolute mercy), 'صلوات' and 'رحمة' have the same meaning, so the conjunction is explanatory.

Key Terms
الصلاة
الرحمة
التعظيم
اللغة القليلة
عطف العام على الخاص
عطف التفسير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'salah' from Allah is simply mercy without any additional connotation.
Correction: The preferred interpretation is that it is mercy accompanied by glorification, as the context and pronoun indicate.

Checkpoint
According to the preferred interpretation, what does 'salah' from Allah mean?
A) Absolute mercy
B) Mercy accompanied by glorification
C) Mercy without glorification
D) Prayer recited by angels

Key Takeaway
Allah's 'salah' is mercy accompanied by glorification, which distinguishes it from mere mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience Allah's mercy in your life—whether through relief from hardship or daily blessings—do you also feel a corresponding sense of awe and glorification? How often do you stop to combine gratitude with reverence?
?
If Allah's salah upon you includes glorification, then receiving His mercy is not a casual affair. How does that change the way you respond to His blessings? Will you begin to consciously add glorification to your thanks?
?
The passage shows that a small shift in understanding a word can change the meaning of a Quranic verse. Are you willing to examine your own assumptions about Allah's attributes, even if it means adjusting long-held interpretations? What concrete step will you take to seek deeper understanding?

Meanings of Salah and the Homonym Debate
Commentator defines 'salah' from various subjects and analyzes it as homonym vs unified meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different meanings of 'salah' depending on the subject and the linguistic debate over whether it is a verbal or semantic homonym.

Core Idea
Salah has one essential meaning of 'turning with mercy,' not multiple unrelated meanings.

In this passage, the commentator explores how the word 'salah' is used differently when attributed to Allah, angels, or creation. He then engages a key linguistic question: does 'salah' have multiple distinct meanings, or does it have a single core meaning that manifests differently? This lesson unpacks both views and their implications for understanding this fundamental term.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the commentator's line of reasoning.
Pay attention to how the commentator introduces the debate.
2
Identify the four subjects of salah
Note the meanings of salah from Allah, angels, others (including inanimate objects), and the concise version. Understand each attribution.
The majority view gives distinct meanings for each subject.
3
Understand the two linguistic theories
Learn the definitions of verbal homonym (مشترك لفظي) and semantic homonym (مشترك معنوي). Identify which view corresponds to which.
The commentator explains that the first view (distinct meanings) leads to verbal homonym, while Ibn Hisham's unified view leads to semantic homonym.
4
See why the commentator prefers semantic homonym
The commentator states that the verified view is semantic homonym. Reflect on the implications for understanding 'salah' across contexts.
This conclusion unifies the meaning while acknowledging contextual nuances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد فسَّرَ الجمهور الصلاة بأنها من الله الرحمة ومن الملائكة الاستغفار، ومن غيرهم ولو حجراً وشجراً ومدراً النضرَّعُ والدُّعاءُ، فقد ورد أنها صَلَّتْ عليه كما رواه الحلبي في السيرة (۱) ، وإن اشتهر أنها سلَّمت عليه فقط، وإن شئت قلت وهو الأخصر : هي من الله الرحمة ومن غيره الدعاء؛ وحينئذ يكون شاملاً للاستغفار وغيره؛ واختار ابن هشام في مغنيه : أنها العطف بفتح العين، وهو بالنسبة الله الرحمة ... إلى آخره (۲) ، ويترتب على هذا الخلاف أنها من قبيل المشترك اللفظي على الأول؛ وضابطه : أن يتحد اللفظ ويتعدَّدَ المعنى والوضعُ ، ومِن قبيل المشترك المعنوي على الثاني، وضابطه : أن يتَّحد كلٌّ من اللفظ والمعنى والوضع، والتحقيق: الثاني، وإن ربَّحَ بعضُهم الأول (۳) .
And the majority interpreted 'prayer' as from Allah: mercy, from angels: seeking forgiveness, and from others, even stone, tree, and clay: supplication and prayer. For it has been reported that they prayed upon him, as al-Halabi narrated in al-Sira, although it is famous that they only greeted him. And if you wish to say it more concisely: it is from Allah: mercy, and from other than Him: supplication; and then it encompasses seeking forgiveness and others. And Ibn Hisham in his Mughni chose that it is 'al-'atf' with fatha on the 'ayn, and with respect to Allah it is mercy... to the end. And upon this difference, it is of the type of verbal homonym according to the first view; its definition: that the word is one and the meaning and the [lexical] assignment are multiple. And of the type of semantic homonym according to the second view; its definition: that the word, meaning, and assignment are each one. And the verified [view] is the second, even though some preferred the first.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the various meanings of 'صلاة' (prayer) depending on the subject. The commentator begins by stating the majority interpretation: from Allah, it means mercy; from angels, it means seeking forgiveness (استغفار); from others, including inanimate objects like stones, trees, and clay, it means supplication and prayer (التضرع والدعاء). He notes a report from al-Halabi's al-Sira that these objects prayed upon the Prophet, but the famous account is that they only greeted him. He then offers a concise formulation: from Allah it is mercy, and from others it is supplication, which includes seeking forgiveness. The commentator then introduces the view of Ibn Hisham in Mughni al-Labib, who defines 'صلاة' as 'العطف' (turning/mercy) with fatha on the 'ayn, and with respect to Allah it means mercy. This leads to a linguistic analysis: are these meanings multiple unrelated meanings (مشترك لفظي) or a single meaning with different applications (مشترك معنوي)? The first view (majority interpretation of distinct meanings for each subject) makes 'صلاة' a verbal homonym (لفظي), where the word is the same but the meaning and lexical assignment differ. The second view (Ibn Hisham's unified 'عطف') makes it a semantic homonym (معنوي), where word, meaning, and assignment are one. The commentator affirms that the verified view is the second (semantic homonym), although some preferred the first.

المشترك المعنوي
العطف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'salah' has multiple unrelated meanings depending on the subject.
Correction: The verified view is that 'salah' has one essential meaning of 'turning with mercy' (al-`atf), which manifests as mercy from Allah, supplication from others, etc.

Checkpoint

A) Verbal homonym
B) Semantic homonym

D) Neither

Key Takeaway

The Use of 'Ala' in Prayers upon the Prophet
Explanation of the grammatical and semantic reason for using 'ala' in prayers upon the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical and semantic reasoning behind the use of the preposition 'ala' in prayers upon the Prophet, and how it differs from ordinary supplications.

Core Idea
The use of 'ala' signals elevation, not harm.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a subtle grammatical point: why does the phrase 'salla Allahu 'alayhi wa sallam' use the preposition 'ala' rather than the expected 'lam'? After all, 'ala' is typically used for supplications of harm. The explanation reveals a deep understanding of both syntax and semantics, showing that prayers upon the Prophet are a unique category of blessing.

Guided Path
1
Parse the phrase 'ala nabiyy'
Examine the grammatical construction of the prepositional phrase and understand its role as a predicate.
The phrase is attached to a deleted predicate of a subject.
2
Identify the grammatical issue
Notice that 'ala' is used instead of the expected 'lam' for a supplication of benefit.
This triggers the commentator's explanation.
3
Examine the commentator's reasoning
Understand the distinction between prayers (salat) and ordinary supplication (du'a), and why the rule for du'a does not apply here.
The key difference is that salat is understood only as benefit, while du'a with 'ala' implies harm.
4
Note the additional semantic nuance
Appreciate that 'ala' also conveys 'shiddat al-tamakkun' (strong establishment), befitting the Prophet's status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( عَلَى نَبِيٍّ ) أي : كائنان على نبي، فالجار والمجرور متعلق بمحذوف خبر المبتدأ، وليس من باب التنازع ; لأن بعضهم منعه في الجوامد، وإنما عدى الدعاء ب (على) مع أن الدعاء إن كان بخير تعدى باللام وإن كان بشر تعدى بـ (على)، لأن محل ذلك ما لم يكن بعنوان الصلاة والسلام; للفرق الظاهر بين (صلى وسلم عليه) و(دعا) عليه ; إذ الأول لا يفهم منه إلا المنفعة، والثاني لا يفهم منه إلا المضرَّة وأيضاً في التعبير بـ (على) إشارة إلى شدة التمكن.
His saying: 'upon a prophet (عَلَى نَبِيٍّ)' means: the prayer is upon a prophet, so the prepositional phrase is attached to a deleted predicate of the subject, and it is not from the category of competition (tanazu‘) because some prevented it in the case of agent nouns. And the du‘a (supplication) is made transitive with 'ala' even though normally a du‘a for good takes the lam and a du‘a for harm takes 'ala', because the case here is that it is not under the heading of supplication, due to the apparent difference between 'he prayed and greeted upon him (salla wa sallama ‘alayhi)' and 'he supplicated against him (da‘a ‘alayhi)'; for the former is not understood except as benefit, and the latter is not understood except as harm. And also in the expression with 'ala' there is an indication of strong establishment (shiddat al-tamakkun).
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and semantic explanation of the phrase 'عَلَى نَبِيٍّ' (upon a prophet) in the poem's line. The commentator first parses the phrase: 'على نبي' means the prayer is upon a prophet, and the prepositional phrase is attached to a deleted predicate. He then clarifies that this is not an instance of 'تنازع' (competing agents) because some grammarians disallow that with agent nouns. The main point is to explain why the word 'على' is used instead of the expected 'لام' for a prayer of benefit. Normally, in Arabic, if you pray for good for someone, you use 'لام' (e.g., رحمه الله), and if you pray for harm, you use 'على'. But here, although prayers upon the Prophet are clearly a form of supplication for good, the preposition 'على' is used. The commentator explains that this is because the phrase 'صلى الله عليه وسلم' is not considered a regular 'دعاء' (supplication). There is a clear difference between 'صلى عليه' (he prayed upon him) and 'دعا عليه' (he supplicated against him). The former can only be understood as benefit, while the latter as harm. Furthermore, using 'على' indicates 'شدة التمكن' (strong establishment or elevation), which is fitting for the Prophet's high status. Thus, the passage justifies the exceptional usage by distinguishing the specialized meaning of prayers upon the Prophet.

Key Terms
على
الصلاة
الدعاء
شدة التمكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The use of 'ala' in prayers upon the Prophet implies harm or curse.
Correction: The commentator explains that this is a special usage distinct from ordinary supplication, and 'ala' here indicates elevation and strong establishment, not harm.

Checkpoint

A) Because the Prophet is above all creation
B) Because the phrase is not considered a regular supplication but a special formula of benefit
C) Because 'على' can be used for both good and harm
D) Because it is a grammatical mistake that poets allowed

Key Takeaway
The exceptional use of 'ala' in prayers upon the Prophet carries the meaning of elevation and is distinct from ordinary supplication.

Linguistic Meanings of ad-Dīn
The author lists the linguistic meanings of 'dīn'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple linguistic meanings of the Arabic word ad-Dīn beyond its common translation as 'religion'.

Core Idea
The word 'dīn' carries fundamental meanings: obedience, worship, recompense, and reckoning.

Before discussing the technical definition of 'dīn' in Islamic theology, the author first clarifies its linguistic roots. This lesson explores the various connotations of 'dīn' in the Arabic language, setting the stage for a deeper theological understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic phrase listing the linguistic meanings. Try to recognize the words for obedience, worship, recompense, and reckoning.
2
Identify each meaning
List the four meanings mentioned. Reflect on how each relates to the concept of religion.
Note that 'dīn' is not limited to 'religion' alone.
3
Connect to terminological definition
Consider how these linguistic meanings inform the later technical definition in Islamic terminology.
For example, obedience and worship are central to the practice of the religion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (الدِّينُ) يطلق لغةً على عدة معانٍ ؛ منها الطاعة والعبادة والجزاء والحساب، ولهم فيه اصطلاحاً تعريفان :
أحدُهُما مختصر : وهو ما شرعَهُ اللهُ تعالى على لسان نبيه من الأحكام، ويسمى ديناً لأننا ندين له وننقاد، ويسمّى أيضاً ملَّةً من حيث إن الملك يُمليه على الرسول، وهو يُمليه علينا ويسمى شرعاً وشريعة من حيث إن الله شرعَه لنا؛ أي: بيَّنه لنا على لسان النبي ؛ فالله هو الشارع حقيقة، والنبي شارع مجاز (٦).
His statement: 'ad-Dīn (religion)' is used linguistically for several meanings: among them obedience (ṭāʿa), worship (ʿibāda), recompense (jazāʾ), and reckoning (ḥisāb). In their terminology, there are two definitions of it: One of them is brief: it is what Allah Almighty has legislated (sharaʿa) through the tongue of the Prophet of rulings (aḥkām). It is called 'dīn' because we serve Him and submit to Him (nadinū lahu wa nangādu). It is also called 'milla' from the sense that the King dictates it to the messenger, and he dictates it to us. And it is called 'sharʿ' and 'sharīʿa' from the sense that Allah has legislated it (sharaʿa) for us, i.e., He explained it to us through the tongue of the Prophet. So Allah is the true lawgiver (al-shāriʿ ḥaqīqa), and the Prophet is a metaphorical lawgiver (shāriʿ majāzan).
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and terminological definition of the word 'ad-Dīn' (religion). First, the author mentions its linguistic meanings: it can refer to obedience, worship, recompense, and reckoning. Then he turns to its technical definition in Islamic terminology, presenting two definitions. The first is brief: it is what Allah has legislated through the Prophet – that is, the body of rulings (aḥkām) revealed through divine legislation. This is called 'dīn' because it involves service and submission to God. The author then relates this term to other related terms: 'milla' comes from the idea that the King (Allah) dictates to the messenger, who in turn dictates to the people; 'sharʿ' and 'sharīʿa' come from the root 'sharʿ' meaning 'to legislate' or 'make plain'. Finally, a crucial theological distinction is drawn: Allah is the true lawgiver (al-shāriʿ ḥaqīqa) because He is the source of legislation, while the Prophet is a lawgiver in a metaphorical sense (majāz), because he conveys and explains the divine law, but does not create it. This distinction is important in Islamic theology to protect divine sovereignty in lawgiving.

Key Terms
الدين
لغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a linguistic meaning of 'ad-Dīn' mentioned in the passage?
A) Obedience
B) Worship
C) Faith
D) Reckoning

Key Takeaway
The Arabic word 'dīn' has several linguistic meanings including obedience, worship, recompense, and reckoning.

Terminological Definition of ad-Dīn and Related Terms
The author gives the brief terminological definition of 'dīn' and explains its relation to milla, sharʿ, and sharīʿa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the brief terminological definition of ad-Dīn as divine legislation, and the meanings of related terms milla, sharʿ, sharīʿa, and the distinction between Allah and the Prophet as lawgivers.

Ad-Dīn in Islamic terminology is what Allah legislated through the Prophet; Allah is the true lawgiver.

Having understood the linguistic range of 'dīn', the author now turns to its technical meaning in Islamic theology. He presents a concise definition and then clarifies related terms that are often used interchangeably, while also drawing a critical distinction about the source of religious law.

This passage is a lexical and terminological definition of the word 'ad-Dīn' (religion). Distinction Between Haqq and Sidq
Definitions of truth (haqq) and truthfulness (sidq) with distinction in attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical distinction between haqq (truth) and sidq (truthfulness) in terms of attribution of correspondence.

Core Idea
Haqq and sidq differ in which side the correspondence is attributed to.

In Islamic theology, precise terminology matters. This lesson explores the subtle difference between two words often translated as 'truth'—haqq and sidq. The passage from Sanusi explains their definitions and the reasoning behind their distinct usages. Pay attention to how correspondence is attributed to reality in haqq and to the judgment in sidq.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get an overview. Notice the definitions and the key terms.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two definitions
Locate the definitions of haqq and sidq and their opposites (batil and kadhib). Understand that both involve correspondence between judgment and reality.
3
Focus on the attribution of correspondence
The author explains that in haqq, correspondence is attributed to reality (al-waqi'), while in sidq it is attributed to the judgment. Reflect on the reason given: haqq derives from 'fixed' and the fixed is reality.
This is the core distinction.
4
Consider the alternative view
The passage mentions that some scholars consider haqq and sidq to be identical. Note their reasoning: both mean correspondence of the report to reality, with reality defined as Allah's knowledge or the Preserved Tablet.
This shows a range of opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنهم فسروا الحقِّ بأنه الحكمُ الذي طابقه الواقع، وضده الباطل، وفسروا الصدق بأنه الحكمُ الذي طابق الواقع، وضده الكذبُ، فأسندوا المطابقة في تفسير الحقِّ إلى الواقع، وفي تفسير الصدق إلى الحكم وذلك أن المطابقة وإن كانت مفاعلة من الجانبين إلا أنه لما كان الحقُّ مأخوذاً من حَقَّ الشيءُ: إذا ثبت، والثابت إنما هو الواقع، ناسب أن تنسب المطابقة في جانب الحقِّ إلى الواقع، بخلافه في الصدق .
واختار بعض المحققين أن الحق والصدق شيء واحد، وهو مطابقة الخبر للواقع ؛ لأن الواقع شيء ثابت في نفسه يقاس عليه غيرُه ، والمراد بالواقع : علم الله تعالى، وقيل: اللوح المحفوظ، وقيل غير ذلك.
And know that they interpreted 'truth' (haqq) as the judgment to which reality corresponds, and its opposite is falsehood (batil); and they interpreted 'truthfulness' (sidq) as the judgment that corresponds to reality, and its opposite is lying (kadhib). So they attributed the correspondence in the interpretation of haqq to reality, and in the interpretation of sidq to the judgment. That is because correspondence, although it is from both sides, yet since haqq is derived from 'haqqa al-shay'u' (the thing became fixed), and the fixed is only reality, it is fitting that correspondence is attributed in the case of haqq to reality, unlike in the case of sidq. And some verifiers (muhaqqiqun) chose that haqq and sidq are one thing, which is the correspondence of the report to reality; because reality is something fixed in itself against which other things are measured. And the intended by 'reality' is the knowledge of Allah, or it is said: the Preserved Tablet (al-lawh al-mahfuz), or other than that.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'haqq' (truth) and 'sidq' (truthfulness) and clarifies the difference between them. The commentators define haqq as 'the judgment to which reality corresponds', meaning the judgment is in accordance with reality; its opposite is falsehood (batil). They define sidq as 'the judgment that corresponds to reality', meaning the judgment agrees with reality; its opposite is lying (kadhib). The difference lies in the attribution of correspondence: in haqq, the correspondence is attributed to reality (i.e., reality matches the judgment), while in sidq, it is attributed to the judgment (i.e., the judgment matches reality). This is because haqq is derived from 'thabit' (fixed), and the fixed is reality, so correspondence appropriately relates to reality. Some scholars, however, hold that haqq and sidq are identical, both meaning correspondence of the report to reality, and they consider reality to be Allah's knowledge or the Preserved Tablet. This classification is typical in kalam discourse about epistemology and truth conditions.

Key Terms
حق
صدق
مطابقة
واقع
باطل
كذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Haqq and sidq are exact synonyms with no difference.
Correction: Classical scholars distinguish them by which side the correspondence is attributed to: in haqq, reality matches the judgment; in sidq, the judgment matches reality.

Checkpoint
In the classical definition, how is haqq different from sidq?
A) Haqq is subjective, sidq is objective
B) In haqq, correspondence is attributed to reality; in sidq, to the judgment
C) Haqq applies only to divine, sidq to human
D) They are identical

Key Takeaway
The distinction between haqq and sidq lies in the direction of correspondence attribution—haqq emphasizes reality matching the judgment, while sidq emphasizes the judgment matching reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Grammatical Cases of the Name 'Muhammad'
Explanation of three possible grammatical cases for 'Muhammad' with objections and answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three permissible grammatical cases for the name 'Muhammad' in the line and how the commentator defends each, including responses to objections.

Core Idea
The name 'Muhammad' can be read in three cases, each grammatically valid, with the nominative preferred for honor.

In this passage, the commentator analyzes a single word from the poem and shows remarkable grammatical depth. While the meter requires dropping the nunation, three different i‘rab readings are possible, each with its own justification and scholarly defense. This lesson explores those readings and the objections raised against some of them, revealing how grammar serves theological reverence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the Arabic text carefully, then the translation. Notice the concise style and the mention of objections and answers.
The passage assumes knowledge of Arabic grammar terms like raf‘, nasb, and jarr.
2
Identify the three grammatical cases
Find where the commentator lists the three possibilities: nominative, accusative, genitive. Note which one is preferred and why.
The nominative is preferred for its honorific implication.
3
Examine the objections and answers for the genitive
The genitive has two sub-possibilities: badal and ‘aṭf bayān. Each receives an objection and a counterargument. Follow the logical flow.
The answers appeal to general rules or particular scholarly opinions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (مُحَمَّدِ) بحذف تنوينه للوزن كتسكين باء (العاقب)، ويجوز في اللفظ الشريف أوجه الإعراب الثلاثة : الرفع على أنه خبر لمبتدأ محذوف؛ أي: هو محمد، وهذا هو الأولى من جهة التعظيم ؛ ليكون الاسم الشريف مرفوعاً ،وعمدة، كما أن مدلوله مرفوع الرتبة وعمدة الخلق. والنصب على أنه مفعول لفعل محذوف والتقدير أعني : محمداً أو نحو ذلك، لكن النصب لا يساعده الرسم إلا على طريقة من يرسم المنصوب بصورة المرفوع والمجرور. والجرُّ على أنه بدل أو عطف بيان، لكن يرد على أنه بدل : أن القاعدة أن المبدل منه في نية الطرح والرمي، فيقتضي جعله بدلاً : أن وصف النبوة في نية الطرح والرمي أنه مقصود ! ويُجاب عنه بأن القاعدة أغلبية، أو أن ذلك بالنظر لعمل العامل. ويرد على أنه عطف بيان أنه يشترط أن يكون عطف البيان موافقاً للمتبوع تعريفاً وتنكيراً! ويُجاب عنه بأنه جري على رأي الزمخشري القائل بعدم اشتراط ذلك.
His saying: (Muhammad) with the deletion of its nunation for the meter, like making the ba of (al-ʻāqib) sukun. And the three grammatical cases are permissible for the noble word: The nominative as a predicate of a deleted subject, i.e., 'He is Muhammad', and this is preferable from the perspective of honor, so that the noble name is nominative and a predicate, just as its referent is elevated in rank and the pivot of creation. The accusative as a direct object of a deleted verb, the estimation being 'I mean Muhammad' or similar, but the accusative is not supported by the orthography except according to the method of one who writes the accusative in the form of the nominative and genitive. The genitive as a substitution or explanatory conjunction, but it is objected that as a substitution, the rule is that the substituted is intended to be discarded, so making it a substitution would imply that the description of prophethood is intended to be discarded, while it is intended! And it is answered that the rule is general, or that is with regard to the operation of the operator. And it is objected that as an explanatory conjunction, it requires that the explanatory conjunction agree with the followed in definiteness and indefiniteness! And it is answered that this follows the opinion of al-Zamakhshari who holds that this condition is not required.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical analysis. The commentator explains that the word 'Muhammad' in the line is written without nunation due to meter. He then presents three possible grammatical cases: (1) nominative, as a predicate of a deleted subject (i.e., 'He is Muhammad'), which is preferred because it honors the name; (2) accusative, as a direct object of a deleted verb like 'I mean', though the spelling does not normally indicate accusative; (3) genitive, as a substitution (badal) or explanatory conjunction (ʻaṭf bayān). He then raises objections to each of the genitive possibilities: for substitution, the rule is that the substituted is intended to be dismissed, but prophethood is not dismissed; this is answered by noting that the rule is not absolute, or the dismissal pertains to the operator, not the word. For explanatory conjunction, it requires agreement in definiteness, but 'Muhammad' is definite and the followed is perhaps indefinite; this is answered by citing al-Zamakhshari who does not require that condition.

الزمخشري

Correction: The commentator demonstrates that all three cases (nominative, accusative, genitive) are permissible, each with its own justification and responses to objections.

Key Takeaway
The name 'Muhammad' in the line can be read in nominative, accusative, or genitive, each supported by grammatical reasoning, with the nominative preferred for its honorific sense.
