Resolving Repetition with Taǧrīd
Commentator answers an objection about repetition using taǧrīd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator uses the rhetorical device of taǧrīd to resolve an apparent textual repetition in the poem.

Core Idea
Taǧrīd: an elegant solution to an apparent repetition in praise of the Prophet.

In the course of explaining the poem, the commentator faces a clever objection: if 'al-`Āqib' already means the final seal, saying 'to the messengers of his Lord' seems redundant. But the commentator shows that this is not a flaw—it is an opportunity to highlight a subtle Arabic rhetorical device called taǧrīd. Let's see how the objection is raised and how it is answered.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a pronunciation note, an objection, and an answer.
Pay attention to the objection's phrasing: 'if you say...'
2
Identify the objection
The objector argues that 'al-`Āqib' means 'the seal of the messengers', so repeating 'the messengers of his Lord' creates redundancy.
This is a common type of literary criticism.
3
Understand the answer: taǧrīd
The commentator replies by invoking taǧrīd: 'al-`Āqib' is taken in an abstract sense as just 'the seal', without specifying of whom. The phrase 'li-rusuli rabbih' then supplies that specification, avoiding repetition.
Taǧrīd is a rhetorical figure where a term is stripped of its specific reference.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical device
Consider how taǧrīd enriches meaning: it allows a concise yet layered praise, first stating the quality (seal) and then clarifying the scope (messengers).
This device is common in Arabic poetry and prose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لِرُسُلِ) بسكون السين للوزن، وإن جاز في غير ما هنا الضم أيضاً . فإن قلت: حاصل معنى العاقب أنه الخاتم للرسل، وحينئذ يلزم التكرار مع قول المصنف : الرسل ربِّه ! قلت : يندفع ذلك بارتكاب التجريد بأن يُراد بـ (العاقب) الخاتم فقط .
His saying: (لِرُسُلِ) with sukūn on the sīn for meter, and it is also permissible elsewhere to have ḍamma. If you say: the sum meaning of al-`Āqib is that he is the seal of the messengers, and then repetition is necessary with the author's saying: 'the messengers of his Lord'! I say: that is repelled by resorting to taǧrīd, in that what is meant by al-`Āqib is only 'the seal'.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. First, the commentator notes the pronunciation of the word 'li-rusuli' with a sukūn on the sīn for metrical flow in the poem, though a ḍamma is also possible elsewhere. Then an objector raises a problem: if 'al-`Āqib' means the seal of the messengers, then the phrase 'li-rusuli rabbih' (to the messengers of his Lord) would be redundant because it repeats 'messengers'. The answer is to use the rhetorical principle of taǧrīd (abstraction): the term 'al-`Āqib' is taken in an abstract sense, meaning simply 'the seal' without specifying 'of the messengers'. Thus, the phrase 'li-rusuli rabbih' provides the specific reference to messengers, and no repetition occurs.

Key Terms
التجريد
العاقب
الخاتم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The apparent repetition is a genuine flaw in the poem.
Correction: The repetition is only apparent; taǧrīd shows it is a deliberate rhetorical enhancement.

Checkpoint

B) By resorting to taǧrīd, taking al-`Aqib as simply the seal
C) By ignoring the objection
D) By changing the text

Key Takeaway
Taǧrīd allows a term to be used abstractly, removing apparent redundancy and enriching meaning.

Resolving Repetition with Tajrid
Commentator resolves repetition objection and notes pronunciation for meter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how commentators use tajrid (abstraction) to resolve apparent redundancy in theological descriptions.

Core Idea
Abstraction removes redundancy without changing meaning.

In theological texts, precise wording can sometimes appear redundant. Commentators have developed tools like tajrid to show that no repetition actually occurs. This passage demonstrates how a small grammatical adjustment clarifies the meaning and preserves the eloquence of the original.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the structure: an imagined objection (فإن قلت...) followed by the answer (قلت...).
2
Understand the problem
Why would saying 'al-Aqib' and 'al-rusul' together cause repetition? Because if al-Aqib means 'seal of the messengers', then saying 'al-rusul' again would be redundant.
The commentator explains this clearly.
3
Learn the solution: tajrid
Tajrid means abstraction. Here it means taking 'al-Aqib' as just 'the seal' without the implied object, so no repetition.
This is a common rhetorical device.
4
Consider an alternative explanation
A footnote suggests taking 'al-Aqib' in its literal sense 'the one who comes after', which also avoids redundancy.
This shows scholarly flexibility.
5
Note the pronunciation rule
The word 'rusul' is pronounced with sukoon on the sin for meter, even though normally it could have damma.
Meter affects pronunciation in poetry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: حاصل معنى العاقب أنه الخاتم للرسل، وحينئذ يلزم التكرار مع قول المصنف : الرسل ربِّه ؛ لأن التقدير : الخاتم للرسل الرسل ربه ! قلت : يندفع ذلك بارتكاب التجريد بأن يُراد بـ (العاقب) الخاتم فقط . قوله : ( يدفع ذلك ... إلى آخره) أولى منه حمل العاقب على معناه اللغوي؛ وهو الآتي في العقب. انتهى. الأجهوري. قوله : (لِرُسُلِ) بسكون السين للوزن، وإن جاز في غير ما هنا الضم أيضاً .
For if you say: The essential meaning of al-Aqib is that he is the seal of the messengers, and then repetition would occur with the author's saying 'the messengers of his Lord', because the implied meaning is 'the seal of the messengers, the messengers of his Lord'! I say: That is repelled by resorting to tajrid (abstraction), meaning that by al-Aqib is meant only 'the seal'. His saying: (That is repelled... etc.) is better than carrying al-Aqib to its linguistic meaning, which is 'the one who comes after'. End. Al-Ujhuri. His saying: (li-rusuli) is with sukoon on the sin for the meter, although in other than this place, damma is also permissible.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The commentator raises an objection: if 'al-Aqib' means the seal of the messengers, then the phrase 'the seal of the messengers, the messengers of his Lord' would be redundant. The answer uses tajrid (abstraction), meaning that 'al-Aqib' here refers only to the concept of 'seal' without implying the object. This avoids repetition. A footnote suggests an alternative: taking 'al-Aqib' in its linguistic sense of 'the one who comes after', which also avoids repetition. Finally, the commentator notes the pronunciation of 'li-rusul' with sukoon on the sin for the sake of meter.

التجريد
التقدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Grammatical Attachment of 'Wa Aalihi'
Grammatical analysis of the phrase 'wa aalihi' in the poem, focusing on its attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'wa aalihi' must be grammatically attached to 'nabi' rather than 'Muhammad' and the principle of substitution (badal) in Arabic grammar.

Core Idea
The conjunct of a substitute is also a substitute, so 'wa aalihi' cannot be attached to 'Muhammad'.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid addresses a subtle grammatical point: the attachment of the phrase 'wa aalihi' to the preceding words. The author argues that it must be coordinated with 'nabi' (prophet) and not with 'Muhammad', based on the rule of substitution. Understanding this rule clarifies the intended meaning and avoids a grammatical error.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the first part of the source passage carefully, focusing on the grammatical discussion. Pay attention to the terms 'عطف' (coordination) and 'بدل' (substitution).
2
Identify the two possible attachments
The commentary presents two possible coordinations: with 'nabi' or with 'Muhammad'. Note why attaching to 'Muhammad' is considered corrupt.
The key is that 'Muhammad' is a substitute for 'nabi'.
3
Understand the substitution rule
The rule states that what is coordinated with a substitute is also a substitute. Apply this to the example with 'Ali and the servant' to see the logical issue.
If 'servant' is coordinated with 'Ali', it would incorrectly imply the servant is an imam.
4
Review the scholar's opinion
Al-Ujhuri is cited stating that while either attachment is possible, coordinating with 'nabi' is preferable.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the determined view (المتعين) is to attach 'wa aalihi' to 'nabi' for grammatical consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وآله ... إلى آخره)؛ أي وسلام الله مع صلاته على آله ... إلى آخره، فهو معطوف على (نبي) كما هو المتعين، وأما عطفه على (محمد) فلا يخفى فساده، وإن ذكره المصنف في «شرحه» ؛ لأن محمداً) بدل من ، نبي)، والمعطوف على البدل بدل ، ولا يصح أن يكون الآلُ ومَنْ ذُكر معهم بدلاً من (نبي) (۱) فلو قلنا : جاءَ الإمام علي والخادم، ثم جعلنا (الخادم) معطوفاً على (علي) للزم أن يكون الخادم إماماً ; لأن المعطوف على البدل بدل أيضاً , اما عطفه على الإمام) فلا يضر، بل هو المتعين، وهذا ليس كقولنا : جاء الإمام أبو يوسف ومحمد، ومع هذا فقد قال العلامة عبد البر الأجهوري في فتح المجيد : (وآله): معطوف على نبي أو محمد، لكن عطفهم على نبي أولى; لأنه هو المحدّث عنه بطريق الأصالة، ومحمد بدل منه . (۲) أخرجه البخاري (٦٣٥٧) ، ومسلم (٤٠٦). (۳) في (ب): (ونحوه). وفي كلامه الصلاة على غير الأنبياء والملائكة تبعاً، وهي جائزة اتفاقاً، بل هي مطلوبة ; لقوله : «اللهم صل على محمد وعلى آلِ محمد (٢)، وللنهي عن الصلاة البتراء ; وهي التي لم يُذكر فيها الآلُ، وأما استقلالاً فقيل بأنها ممنوعة، وقيل: مكروهة، وقيل: خلاف الأولى، والأصح الكراهة وألحق أبو محمد الجويني السلام بالصلاة بالنظر للغائب، وأما المخاطب فيخاطب بالسلام عليك أو عليكم أو نحوه (۳). وأصل (آل) : أَوّل كجَمَل ; بدليل تصغيره على (أويل)، وقيل: أصله أهل; بدليل تصغيره على أهيل، وإضافته للضمير في كلام المصنف جائزة، خلافاً لمن منعها، قال عبد المطلب [من مجزوء الكامل] : وانْصُرْ عَلَى آلِ الصَّلِ بِ وَعَابِدِيهِ اليَوْمَ آلَكْ (۱) انظر الروض الأنف (۱۵۲۱) .
His saying: 'and his family ... to the end', meaning and the peace of Allah along with His blessings upon his family ... to the end. So it is coordinated with 'nabi' (prophet) as is the determined view. As for coordinating it with 'Muhammad', its corruption is not hidden, even though the author mentioned it in his commentary, because 'Muhammad' is a substitute (badal) of 'nabi', and what is coordinated with a substitute is a substitute, and it is not valid that the family (al) and those mentioned with them be a substitute for 'nabi'. (1) For if we say: 'The imam Ali and the servant came', then we make 'the servant' coordinated with 'Ali', it would necessitate that the servant is an imam, because what is coordinated with a substitute is also a substitute. But coordinating it with 'al-imam' does not harm, rather it is the determined view. And this is not like our saying: 'The imam Abu Yusuf and Muhammad came.' And with this, al-Allamah Abd al-Barr al-Ujhuri said in Fath al-Majid: 'Wa Aalihi' is coordinated with 'nabi' or 'Muhammad', but coordinating them with 'nabi' is better because he is the one being reported about as the original, and Muhammad is a substitute from it. (2) Reported by al-Bukhari (6357) and Muslim (406). (3) In (b): 'and the like.' And in his statement, prayer upon non-prophets and angels is as a follow-up (taba'an), and it is permissible by consensus, rather it is required (matlubah) because of his saying: 'O Allah, bless Muhammad and the family of Muhammad' and because of the prohibition of the 'blessing that is cut off' (al-salat al-batra') which is that in which the family is not mentioned. As for independently (istiqlalan), it is said that it is forbidden (mamnu'ah), and it is said that it is disliked (makruhah), and it is said that it is contrary to the better (khilaf al-awla), and the most correct (asahh) is dislike (karahah). Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni annexed peace (salam) to prayer with regard to the absent (al-gha'ib). As for the one addressed (al-mukhatab), he is addressed with 'peace be upon you' or similar. And the origin of 'al' is 'awwal' like 'jamal', evidenced by its diminutive 'uwail'. And it is said its origin is 'ahl' evidenced by its diminutive 'uhail'. And its addition to the pronoun in the author's speech is permissible, contrary to those who forbid it. Abd al-Muttalib said [in meter mujzoo' al-kamil]: 'And help (us) against the family of al-Sulaybi and his worshippers today, your family.' (1) See Rawd al-Anf (1521).
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and juristic analysis of the phrase 'wa aalihi' from the poem. The author begins by stating that the phrase is attached to 'nabi' (prophet) as a conjunct, and not to 'Muhammad'. He argues that considering it attached to 'Muhammad' is corrupt because 'Muhammad' is a substitute (badal) for 'nabi', and the rule is that the conjunct of a substitute is also a substitute, which would incorrectly make the family a substitute. A footnote provides an example: if we say 'the imam Ali and the servant came', making 'servant' attached to 'Ali' would imply the servant is an imam. Instead, we should attach to 'imam'. The author then cites a scholar who says it could be attached to either but attaching to 'nabi' is preferable. Next, he discusses the ruling on sending prayers upon the family. He clarifies that praying for non-prophets and angels as a follow-up to the prophet is permissible by consensus and even recommended due to explicit hadith. However, praying for them independently (without the prophet) is subject to differing opinions: forbidden, disliked, or contrary to the better, with the correct view being disliked. Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni equated sending peace (salam) with sending blessings (salat) for the absent, while the present is addressed directly. The passage then examines the linguistic origin of the word 'al': two views—it is derived from 'awwal' or from 'ahl', each evidenced by the diminutive form. The author also notes that adding it to a pronoun is permissible, contrary to some. Finally, he cites a line of poetry from Abd al-Muttalib as evidence for the use of 'al' with a pronoun.

Key Terms
وآله
عطف
بدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'wa aalihi' is automatically attached to the closest word 'Muhammad'.
Correction: The commentary shows that due to substitution rules, it must be attached to 'nabi'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is coordinating 'wa aalihi' with 'Muhammad' considered corrupt?
A) Because 'Muhammad' is not a prophet
B) Because 'Muhammad' is a substitute for 'nabi', and the conjunct of a substitute is also a substitute
C) Because the poem does not allow it
D) Because the majority of scholars reject it

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'wa aalihi' must be grammatically attached to 'nabi' due to the rule that the conjunct of a substitute is also a substitute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the salawat in your prayer, do you consciously include the family of the Prophet (peace be upon him) with the same intention as you include him? How does this lesson's grammatical precision affect the depth of your blessing?
?
This lesson shows that a simple conjunction can change the meaning of a prayer. Are there other supplications or phrases you repeat where you might be careless about wording? What will you change today to ensure your words reflect your true intent?
?
The author strongly rejects the attachment to 'Muhammad' as corrupt. Think of a time when you followed a common practice without verifying its correctness. How can you develop a habit of questioning the authenticity and precision of the religious phrases you use?

Ruling on Prayer for the Prophet's Family
Juridical ruling on sending prayers upon the Prophet's family.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rulings regarding sending blessings (salat) upon the Prophet's family, both as a follow-up and independently.

Core Idea
Prayer upon the family is required as a follow-up but disliked independently.

This section of the commentary addresses the juristic ruling on sending blessings upon the Prophet's family. The author distinguishes between praying for them as a follow-up to the Prophet (taba'an) and praying for them independently (istiqlalan). He cites a hadith and discusses the differing opinions among scholars, concluding that independent prayer is disliked.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage on the ruling of prayer. Note the key terms: 'تبعاً' (as a follow-up) and 'استقلالاً' (independently).
2
Understand the follow-up ruling
The author states that prayer upon non-prophets as a follow-up is permissible by consensus and even required due to the hadith and the prohibition of the 'cut-off prayer'.
The hadith 'O Allah, bless Muhammad and the family of Muhammad' is evidence.
3
Analyze the independent ruling
There are three opinions: forbidden, disliked, contrary to the better. The author states the most correct view is dislike (karahah).
Know the meaning of 'خلاف الأولى' (contrary to the better).
4
Note the annexation of peace by al-Juwayni
Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni equated sending peace (salam) with blessings for the absent, but for the present, direct address is used.

قوله : (وآله ... إلى آخره)؛ أي وسلام الله مع صلاته على آله ... إلى آخره، فهو معطوف على (نبي) كما هو المتعين، وأما عطفه على (محمد) فلا يخفى فساده، وإن ذكره المصنف في «شرحه» ؛ لأن محمداً) بدل من ، نبي)، والمعطوف على البدل بدل ، ولا يصح أن يكون الآلُ ومَنْ ذُكر معهم بدلاً من (نبي) (۱) فلو قلنا : جاءَ الإمام علي والخادم، ثم جعلنا (الخادم) معطوفاً على (علي) للزم أن يكون الخادم إماماً ; لأن المعطوف على البدل بدل أيضاً , اما عطفه على الإمام) فلا يضر، بل هو المتعين، وهذا ليس كقولنا : جاء الإمام أبو يوسف ومحمد، ومع هذا فقد قال العلامة عبد البر الأجهوري في فتح المجيد : (وآله): معطوف على نبي أو محمد، لكن عطفهم على نبي أولى; لأنه هو المحدّث عنه بطريق الأصالة، ومحمد بدل منه . (۲) أخرجه البخاري (٦٣٥٧) ، ومسلم (٤٠٦). (۳) في (ب): (ونحوه). وفي كلامه الصلاة على غير الأنبياء والملائكة تبعاً، وهي جائزة اتفاقاً، بل هي مطلوبة ; لقوله : «اللهم صل على محمد وعلى آلِ محمد (٢)، وللنهي عن الصلاة البتراء ; وهي التي لم يُذكر فيها الآلُ، وأما استقلالاً فقيل بأنها ممنوعة، وقيل: مكروهة، وقيل: خلاف الأولى، والأصح الكراهة وألحق أبو محمد الجويني السلام بالصلاة بالنظر للغائب، وأما المخاطب فيخاطب بالسلام عليك أو عليكم أو نحوه (۳). وأصل (آل) : أَوّل كجَمَل ; بدليل تصغيره على (أويل)، وقيل: أصله أهل; بدليل تصغيره على أهيل، وإضافته للضمير في كلام المصنف جائزة، خلافاً لمن منعها، قال عبد المطلب [من مجزوء الكامل] : وانْصُرْ عَلَى آلِ الصَّلِ بِ وَعَابِدِيهِ اليَوْمَ آلَكْ (۱) انظر الروض الأنف (۱۵۲۱) .
His saying: 'and his family ... to the end', meaning and the peace of Allah along with His blessings upon his family ... to the end. Linguistic Origin of the Word 'Al' (آل)
Linguistic analysis of the word 'آل' and its grammatical usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two proposed origins of the Arabic word 'آل' (family) and the grammatical rule regarding its attachment to pronouns.

Core Idea
The word 'آل' is derived from either 'أَوّل' or 'أهل', evidenced by its diminutive forms.

This passage delves into the linguistic etymology of the word 'آل' (family), presenting two scholarly views: it is derived from 'أَوّل' or from 'أهل'. The author provides evidence from the diminutive forms and also addresses the permissibility of attaching 'آل' to pronouns, citing a line of poetry from Abd al-Muttalib as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the last part of the source passage about the origin of 'آل'. Note the two proposed origins and the diminutive evidence.
2
Understand the two origins
The first origin is 'أَوّل' (like 'جمل'), evidenced by the diminutive 'أويل'. The second origin is 'أهل', evidenced by the diminutive 'أهيل'.
Know that the diminutive (تصغير) is a common linguistic tool to identify the root.
3
Learn the rule about pronoun attachment
The author states that adding 'آل' to a pronoun is permissible, contrary to those who forbid it. He cites a line of poetry as evidence.
The line from Abd al-Muttalib shows 'آلَك' (your family).
4
Analyze the poetic evidence
The poetry example demonstrates the use of 'آل' with a possessive pronoun, supporting the permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Āl in the Context of Supplication
Definition of 'āl' in the supplication context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in the context of supplication, the term 'āl' refers to every believer, including sinners.

Core Idea
In supplication, 'āl' includes all believers, especially sinners.

The word 'āl' (family) has different meanings depending on the context. In this lesson, we focus on its meaning in supplication, where it is understood broadly to include every believer, even those who are sinful.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the relevant sentence from the source: 'ففي مقام الدعاء كما هنا : كلُّ مؤمن ولو عاصياً ; لأنَّ العاصي أشدُّ احتياجاً للدعاء من غيره'.
2
Identify the context and definition
Note that the context is supplication (du'a). The definition given is 'every believer, even if sinful'.
This is the broadest meaning among the three contexts.
3
Understand the reason
The phrase 'because the sinner is more in need of supplication than others' provides the rationale: those in greater need are included.
This highlights a principle of inclusion based on need.
4
Contrast with other contexts
Keep in mind that this meaning applies only in supplication; other contexts give different definitions.
This prepares you for the next lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۲) رواه الطبراني في «الأوسط» (۳۳۳۲)، والبيهقي في السنن الكبرى، (۸۳/۲)، والقشيري في رسالته» (۲۲۷/۱). And know that the word 'al' has meanings according to contexts (maqamat), and perhaps they are made into statements (aqwal) but it is not good. In the context of supplication, as here: every believer even if sinful, because the sinner is more in need of supplication than others. In the context of praise: every pious believer, taken from what is reported: 'The family of Muhammad is every pious person', although it is weak. As for 'I am the grandfather of every pious person', it is not reported. (3) Sibawayh said in al-Kitab (3/624): 'Fa'l is not among what the singular is broken upon for the plural.' And al-Akhfash went that it is a broken plural, not a collective noun. Note: Often books of language use the term 'plural' and do not mean broken plural, but rather the meaning of plural, as indicated by al-Allamah Abd al-Hadi al-Abyari in al-Mawakib al-Aliyyah.

Commentary
This passage classifies the meanings of the word 'al' according to three different contexts. First, in the context of supplication (as in the poem being commented on), 'al' refers to every believer, even if sinful, because sinners are most in need of prayers. Second, in the context of praise, it refers to every pious believer, based on a weak hadith that says 'The family of Muhammad is every pious person.' The author notes that the claim 'I am the grandfather of every pious person' is not reported. Third, in the context of zakat (obligatory charity), the meaning of 'al' is restricted to specific blood relatives. The author explains the views of the four schools: Shafi'is include Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib; Malikis (and Hanbalis) only Banu Hashim; Hanafis specify five families: Ali, Ja'far, Aqil, Abbas, and al-Harith. The footnotes provide hadith sources and a grammatical note about the word 'sahb' (companions) which is actually a prelude to the next lesson. The passage is structured as an enumeration of contexts (supplication, praise, zakat) with each context's definition.

Āl in the Context of Praise
Definition of 'āl' in the praise context with hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in the context of praise, 'āl' is restricted to pious believers, based on a weak hadith.

Core Idea
In praise, 'āl' refers to pious believers only.

Moving to the context of praise (madḥ), the meaning of 'āl' narrows. This lesson explains the definition and the evidence used, noting its weakness.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the sentence: 'وفي مقام المدح: كلُّ مؤمن تقي أخذاً مما ورد: «آل محمد كل تقي» (٢) وإن كان ضعيفاً، وأما أنا جد كل تقي)، فلم يرد'
2
Identify the definition
Note that in praise, 'āl' means 'every pious believer' (kullu mu'min taqiyy).
This is narrower than the supplication context.
3
Examine the evidence
The author cites a hadith: 'The family of Muhammad is every pious person.' However, he notes it is weak (ḍa'īf).
Also note that a stronger claim ('I am the grandfather of every pious person') is not reported.
4
Consider implications
Think about why the author includes a weak hadith: it shows the scholarly effort to define terms, even when evidence is not strong.
This context is used in praise, not legal rulings.

Āl in the Context of Zakat: School Opinions
Enumeration of school views on 'āl' in zakat context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different legal definitions of 'āl' in the context of zakat according to the four Sunni schools.

Core Idea
In zakat, 'āl' is defined differently by each school.

In the context of zakat (obligatory charity), the term 'āl' takes on specific legal definitions that vary among the Islamic schools of law. This lesson presents the views of the Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanafi, and Hanbali schools.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read: 'وفي مقام الزكاة: بنو هاشم وبنو المطلب عندنا معاشر الشافعية وبنو هاشم فقط عند السادة المالكية كالحنابلة، وخصَّت الحنفيةُ فِرَقاً خمسة آل علي وآل جعفر وآل عقيل، وآل العباس وآل الحارث.'
2
Identify the Shafi'i view
The Shafi'is include both Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib.
The author identifies with the Shafi'i school ('indanā').
3
Identify the Maliki (and Hanbali) view
The Malikis (and similarly Hanbalis) restrict it to Banu Hashim only.
This is narrower than the Shafi'i view.
4
Identify the Hanafi view
The Hanafis specify five families: Ali, Ja'far, 'Aqil, al-'Abbas, and al-Harith.
This is a more detailed list.
5
Compare and contrast
Notice the differences: Shafi'is broader, Malikis/Hanbalis narrower, Hanafis enumerate specific branches.
This reflects different interpretative principles.

The Context-Dependent Meanings of Āl
Classifies meanings of آل in supplication, praise, and zakāt with school differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the word 'āl' takes different meanings depending on the context of supplication, praise, and zakāt, and how Islamic legal schools differ in its application.

Core Idea
'Āl' is not fixed—it adapts to supplication, praise, and zakāt.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawḥīd clarifies that the word 'āl' (family) does not have a single definition. Instead, its meaning shifts based on the context—whether one is praying, praising, or discussing zakāt eligibility. The author also notes differences among the Islamic legal schools regarding who qualifies as 'āl' for zakāt.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and the literal translation. Notice the linguistic notes and the poetry example before the main classification.
The poetry is cited as evidence for adding 'āl' to a pronoun.
2
Understand the three contexts
The passage lists three maqāms: supplication, praise, and zakāt. For each, note the scope of 'āl' and the reasoning given.
3
Compare legal school positions on zakāt
In the zakāt context, the Shāfiʿī, Mālikī/Ḥanbalī, and Ḥanafī positions differ. Identify the groups included by each school.
The Ḥanafīs specify five sub-families.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider why the author says 'perhaps they are made into opinions, but that is not good'—meaning the meanings are context-based, not arbitrary opinions.
This highlights that legal rulings depend on context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تصغيره على أهيل، وإضافته للضمير في كلام المصنف جائزة، خلافاً لمن منعها، قال عبد المطلب [من مجزوء الكامل] : (٢) وَانْصُرْ عَلَى آلِ الصَّلي بِ وَعَابِدِيهِ اليَوْمَ آلَكْ (١) واعلم أن الآلَ له معان باعتبار المقامات وربما جعلت أقوالاً وليس بحسن ففي مقام الدعاء كما هنا : كلُّ مؤمن ولو عاصياً ؛ لأنَّ العاصي أشدُّ احتياجاً للدعاء من غيره، وفي مقام المدح: كلُّ مؤمن تقي أخذاً مما ورد: «آل محمد كل تقي» (٢) وإن كان ضعيفاً، وأما أنا جد كل تقي)، فلم يرد، وفي مقام الزكاة: بنو هاشم وبنو المطلب عندنا معاشر الشافعية وبنو هاشم فقط عند السادة المالكية كالحنابلة، وخصَّت الحنفيةُ فِرَقاً خمسة آل علي وآل جعفر وآل عقيل، وآل العباس (٣) لفظه وآل الحارث .
Its diminutive is 'أهيل', and its addition to a pronoun in the author's speech is permissible, contrary to the one who forbade it. 'Abd al-Muttalib said [from the mutaqārib meter]: 'And grant victory over the family of al-Ṣalīb and its worshippers today, Your family.' Know that 'āl' has meanings according to contexts, and perhaps they are made into opinions, but that is not good. In the context of supplication, as here: every believer, even if disobedient, because the disobedient is more in need of supplication than others. In the context of praise: every pious believer, taken from what was narrated: 'The family of Muḥammad is every pious person'—even though it is weak—and as for 'I am the grandfather of every pious person', it was not narrated. In the context of zakāt: the Banū Hāshim and Banū al-Muṭṭalib among us, the Shāfiʿīs, and only Banū Hāshim among the Mālikīs and Ḥanbalīs. The Ḥanafīs specified five groups: the family of ʿAlī, the family of Jaʿfar, the family of ʿAqīl, the family of al-ʿAbbās, and the family of al-Ḥārith.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage classifies the meanings of the word 'āl' (آل) according to different contexts. It begins with a linguistic note that the diminutive of 'āl' is 'uhayl' and that adding it to a pronoun (e.g., 'ālahu') is permissible, contrary to some grammarians. Then it cites a line of poetry by ʿAbd al-Muṭṭalib as an example. The main classification follows: (1) In the context of supplication, 'āl' includes every believer, even sinners, because they are in greater need of prayer. (2) In the context of praise, it includes only pious believers, based on a weak hadith that says 'the family of Muḥammad is every pious person.' (3) In the context of zakāt, the scope differs by school: Shāfiʿīs include Banū Hāshim and Banū al-Muṭṭalib; Mālikīs and Ḥanbalīs restrict it to Banū Hāshim; Ḥanafīs list five specific families (ʿAlī, Jaʿfar, ʿAqīl, al-ʿAbbās, al-Ḥārith). The passage thus shows that the word is not monolithic in meaning.

Key Terms
آل
مقام الدعاء
مقام المدح
مقام الزكاة
بنو هاشم
بنو المطلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think 'āl' always refers to the Prophet's blood relatives.
Correction: Its meaning changes based on context: in supplication it includes all believers, in praise only the pious, and in zakāt it varies by school.

Checkpoint
In the context of supplication, who is included in the meaning of 'āl' according to this passage?
A) Only pious believers
B) Every believer, even sinners
C) Only Banū Hāshim
D) The Prophet's immediate family only

Key Takeaway
The word 'āl' has different meanings in supplication, praise, and zakāt, reflecting the principle that language and law must be interpreted contextually.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the phrase 'Āl Muḥammad', do you automatically think only of blood relatives? How would your view of the Muslim community change if you saw every pious believer as truly part of his family? Will you start looking at fellow Muslims with that lens from now on?
?
The scholars differed on who qualifies as 'āl' for zakāt. Are there any matters of faith or practice where you are absolutely certain that your view is the only correct one, without considering the legitimate differences that exist? Will you commit to studying differing opinions with humility before dismissing them?
?
In your daily interactions, do you interpret people's words based on your own default definitions rather than pausing to ask about the context? Recall a specific misunderstanding you had recently. How might this lesson on context-dependent meanings help you avoid that in the future? What concrete step will you take to practice contextual understanding this week?

Logic and Technical Terms
beginner
6 min
The Linguistic and Technical Definition of a Companion
Defines ṣaḥābī linguistically and technically, with key conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of a companion (ṣaḥābī) according to Sunni scholarship, including its linguistic basis and the conditions for being considered a companion.

Core Idea
A companion is anyone who met the Prophet believing, even if they never saw or narrated from him.

This passage from Tuhfat al-Murīd clarifies who qualifies as a companion of the Prophet (ṣaḥābī). The author begins by explaining the linguistic nature of the word 'ṣuḥb' and then gives a technical definition that is broader than common assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic nature of 'ṣuḥb'
Notice that 'ṣuḥb' is a collective noun, not a plural of 'ṣāḥib'. This affects how we understand the term.
The singular 'ṣāḥib' exists but the collective form is used.
2
Learn the linguistic meaning
The root meaning is 'long association' (ṭālat ʿishratuka bihī). This sets the stage for the technical definition.
3
Grasp the technical definition
A companion is one who met the Prophet after his mission while believing in him, in a customary meeting place (anywhere on earth).
Meeting includes both seeing and not seeing.
4
Note what is not required
Seeing the Prophet, narrating from him, or even visually distinguishing him are not required according to the sound view.
5
Understand the condition 'died in Islam'
This condition applies to the continuation of companionship, not its origin. Apostasy after meeting does not negate the initial companionship if the person died a Muslim.
This will be expanded in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وَصَحْبِهِ) خصَّهُم مع دخولهم في الآل بالمعنى الأعم لمزيد الاهتمام والتحقيق أن ( صحباً) ليس جمعاً لـ (صاحب) بل اسم جمع وإن كان له واحد من وهو صاحب ، وهو لغةً : من طالَتْ عشرتك به ، والمراد به هنا الصحابي؛ وهو مَنْ اجتمع بنبينا مؤمناً به بعد البعثة في محلّ التعارف؛ بأن يكون على وجه الأرض وإن لم يره، أو لم يرو عنه شيئاً ، أو لم يميز على الصحيح، وأما قولهم: (ومات على الإسلام) فهو شرط لدوام الصحبة ، لا لأصلها (4) ، فإن ارتد والعياذ بالله تعالى ومات مرتدا، فليس بصحابي ؛ كعبد الله بن خَطل، وأما من عاد للإيمان كعبد الله بن أبي سرح فتعود له الصحبة، لكن مجرَّدة عن الثواب عندنا معاشر الشافعية، واشتهر أنها لا تعود عند المالكية، لكن المصرَّح به في كتبهم التردُّد، وحينئذ فلا مانع من الرجوع في ذلك لمذهب الشافعية على ما كان يرتضيه بعضُ أشياخهم عودها التسمية والكفاءة ؛ فيسمَّى صحابيا، ويكون كفؤاً لبنت الصحابي. ويدخلُ في الصحابي ابنُ أم مكتوم ونحوه من العميان، وكُنيت أمه به لکنم بصره، واسمُهُ عبد الله ؛ أحد المؤذنين له ، ويدخل عيسى والخضر وإلياس عليهم الصلاة والسلام (۳) ، وتدخل الملائكة الذين اجتمعوا به في الأرض(۳)، فعيسى عليه صلى الله عليه وسلم الصلاة والسلام آخرُ الصحابة من البشر الظاهرين، وأما الملائكة فباقون إلى النفخة، والخضرُ يموتُ عند رفع القرآن، وقيل: بل مات. والحاصل أن الخضر وإلياس حيَّان على المعتمد ، ولكن إلياس رسول بنص القرآن؛ قال تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّ إِلْيَاسَ لَمِنَ الْمُرْسَلِينَ ﴾ [الصَّافات : ۱۲۳]، وأما الخضر فقيل: ولي ، وقيل : نبي، وقيل: رسول، وخيرُ الأمور أوساطها .
His statement: 'and his company' (wa-ṣaḥbihi) singled them out even though they are included in 'āl' in the broader sense, for extra emphasis. The fact is that 'ṣuḥb' (صحبا) is not a plural of 'ṣāḥib' (صاحب) but a collective noun, even though it has a singular from the same root, which is 'ṣāḥib'. It means linguistically: one with whom you have long association. What is intended here is the 'ṣaḥābī' (companion); he is: one who met our Prophet, believing in him after his mission, in a customary meeting place, whether on the face of the earth, even if he did not see him, or did not narrate anything from him, or did not distinguish [the Prophet] according to the sound view. As for their saying: 'and died in Islam', it is a condition for the continuation of companionship, not for its origin. For if he apostatized—God forbid—and died an apostate, he is not a companion; like ʿAbd Allāh b. Khaṭal. As for one who returned to faith, like ʿAbd Allāh b. Abī Sarḥ, companionship returns to him, but devoid of reward among us, the Shāfiʿīs. It is famous that it does not return among the Mālikīs, but what is stated in their books is hesitation. Then there is no obstacle to reverting to the Shāfiʿī school for that, according to what some of their shaykhs approved: the return of the designation and suitability (kafāʾa), so he is called a companion and is suitable [as a husband] for the daughter of a companion. Included in the companion is Ibn Umm Maktūm and similar blind persons; his mother was called Umm Maktūm because of the loss (luknum) of his eyesight, and his name is ʿAbd Allāh, one of his (the Prophet's) muʾadhdhins. Also included are Jesus, al-Khiḍr, and Elias—peace be upon them—and the angels who gathered with him on earth. So Jesus—peace and blessings be upon him—is the last of the companions among visible humans. As for the angels, they remain until the Trumpet [blast]. Al-Khiḍr will die when the Qurʾān is raised, and some say he has already died. The summary is that al-Khiḍr and Elias are alive according to the relied-upon view, but Elias is a messenger by explicit Qurʾānic text; God Most High says: 'And indeed Elias is among the messengers' (Q 37:123). As for al-Khiḍr, some say he is a saint (walī), some say a prophet, some say a messenger, and the best of affairs is the middle ones.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'ṣaḥābī' (companion) from the word 'ṣaḥb' in the text. It first clarifies the linguistic nature of 'ṣuḥb': it is a collective noun, not a broken plural, though it has a singular 'ṣāḥib'. The technical definition follows: a companion is anyone who met the Prophet after his mission while believing in him, in a customary meeting place (i.e., anywhere on earth), even if the person never saw the Prophet or narrated from him, and even if they were unable to visually distinguish him. The condition 'died in Islam' applies to the continuation of companionship, not its initial existence. Apostasy before death cancels the status (e.g., ʿAbd Allāh b. Khaṭal). If the person returns to faith (e.g., ʿAbd Allāh b. Abī Sarḥ), companionship returns but without reward according to Shāfiʿīs; Mālikīs are uncertain. The passage then lists unusual inclusions: blind people (like Ibn Umm Maktūm), Jesus, al-Khiḍr, Elias, and angels who met the Prophet on earth. Jesus is declared the last companion among visible humans; angels continue until the Trumpet. The status of al-Khiḍr and Elias is discussed: both are alive according to the relied view, with Elias being a prophet (by Qurʾān), while al-Khiḍr's prophetic status is debated. The commentary thus expands the definition of 'companion' far beyond the usual understanding.

Key Terms
صحب
صاحب
صحابي
اجتماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think a companion must have seen the Prophet or narrated hadith from him.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that a companion is defined simply by meeting the Prophet as a believer, even without seeing or narrating.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT required for someone to be considered a companion (ṣaḥābī)?
A) Meeting the Prophet after his mission
B) Being a believer at the time of meeting
C) Having seen the Prophet with their own eyes
D) The meeting taking place in a customary meeting place

Key Takeaway
A companion is anyone who met the Prophet as a believer after his mission, regardless of seeing, narrating, or distinguishing him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever reduce the Companions to mere names in hadith chains, or do you see them as flesh-and-blood humans whose faith was tested and proven? What would it take for you to have that same certainty in the Prophet's message today?
?
If you were among the people who met the Prophet but never narrated a single hadith, would you still be content with your faith? Or do you measure your worth by recognition and reputation?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
5 min
Apostasy and Its Effect on Companionship
Explains the effect of apostasy on companionship with examples and madhhab differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how apostasy affects the status of companionship, including its complete negation if death occurs in apostasy, and the return of companionship upon reversion to Islam according to different schools.

Core Idea
Apostasy after companionship cancels it if death occurs in that state; returning to faith restores it but without reward.

After defining the companion, the author addresses a crucial situation: what happens if a companion apostatizes? This passage explains the ruling using examples and notes differences between the Shāfiʿī and Mālikī schools regarding the return of the companion's status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling on apostasy
If a companion apostatizes and dies an apostate, he is not a companion at all. Example: ʿAbd Allāh b. Khaṭal.
This shows the condition 'died in Islam' is for continuation.
2
Understand the case of returning to faith
If the apostate returns to Islam (like ʿAbd Allāh b. Abī Sarḥ), companionship returns, but without reward according to the Shāfiʿīs.
The reward is lost, but the title and certain rulings apply.
3
Note the Mālikī position
It is famously said that companionship does not return among Mālikīs, but their books express hesitation, allowing referral to the Shāfiʿī view.
4
Learn the practical outcome
The Shāfiʿī view that companionship returns is adopted for the sake of designation (tasmiya) and suitability (kafāʾa) in marriage.
This has legal implications for marriage to a companion's daughter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Shāfiʿī view on a companion who apostatizes and then returns to Islam?
A) He is not a companion at all
B) He regains full companionship with reward
C) He regains companionship but without reward
D) He may or may not be a companion depending on the Mālikī school

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If someone close to you abandoned Islam and later returned, would you view them as a full believer or hold their past against them? This lesson teaches that companionship can be restored (even without reward). Are you willing to restore your trust in them without demanding perfection?
?
The Shāfiʿīs distinguish between the title of companionship and the reward. Have you ever done a good deed that was outwardly correct but empty of inner faith? How can you ensure that your own actions are not merely formal but truly rewarded by Allah?
?
The Mālikī school hesitated about returning companionship. Have you ever hesitated to accept someone's repentance because you doubted its sincerity? What does this lesson teach you about giving others the benefit of the doubt, especially regarding their Islamic identity?

8 min
Unusual Inclusions: The Broad Scope of Companionship
Lists unique individuals included as companions (blind, Jesus, Khidr, Elias, angels) with details.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which unusual individuals (blind, Jesus, al-Khiḍr, Elias, angels) are included among the companions according to the broader definition, and their specific statuses.

Core Idea
Companionship includes the blind, the prophets Jesus, Khidr, Elias, and even angels who met the Prophet on earth.

Having established the definition, the author now lists several categories of beings that are included as companions, even though they may not be typically thought of as such. This passage covers the blind (like Ibn Umm Maktūm), prophets (Jesus, Khidr, Elias), and angels, along with discussions on their lifespans and statuses.

Guided Path
1
Learn about the inclusion of the blind
Blind persons like Ibn Umm Maktūm are companions even though they never saw the Prophet. His story is given.
This reinforces that seeing is not required.
2
Discover the inclusion of Jesus, al-Khiḍr, and Elias
These prophets are considered companions because they met the Prophet (peace be upon him) on earth.
Jesus is the last companion among visible humans.
3
Understand the inclusion of angels
Angels who gathered with the Prophet on earth are also companions; they remain until the Trumpet.
4
Note the status of al-Khiḍr and Elias
Both are alive according to the relied view. Elias is a messenger by Qur'ān; al-Khiḍr's prophethood is debated.
The passage ends with a preference for moderation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What is intended here is the 'ṣaḥābī' (companion); he is: one who met our Prophet, believing in him after his mission, in a customary meeting place, whether on the face of the earth, even if he did not see him, or did not narrate anything from him, or did not distinguish [the Prophet] according to the sound view. As for their saying: 'and died in Islam', it is a condition for the continuation of companionship, not for its origin. For if he apostatized—God forbid—and died an apostate, he is not a companion; like ʿAbd Allāh b. Khaṭal. As for one who returned to faith, like ʿAbd Allāh b. Abī Sarḥ, companionship returns to him, but devoid of reward among us, the Shāfiʿīs. It is famous that it does not return among the Mālikīs, but what is stated in their books is hesitation. Then there is no obstacle to reverting to the Shāfiʿī school for that, according to what some of their shaykhs approved: the return of the designation and suitability (kafāʾa), so he is called a companion and is suitable [as a husband] for the daughter of a companion. Included in the companion is Ibn Umm Maktūm and similar blind persons; his mother was called Umm Maktūm because of the loss (luknum) of his eyesight, and his name is ʿAbd Allāh, one of his (the Prophet's) muʾadhdhins. Also included are Jesus, al-Khiḍr, and Elias—peace be upon them—and the angels who gathered with him on earth. So Jesus—peace and blessings be upon him—is the last of the companions among visible humans. As for the angels, they remain until the Trumpet [blast]. Al-Khiḍr will die when the Qurʾān is raised, and some say he has already died. The summary is that al-Khiḍr and Elias are alive according to the relied-upon view, but Elias is a messenger by explicit Qurʾānic text; God Most High says: 'And indeed Elias is among the messengers' (Q 37:123). As for al-Khiḍr, some say he is a saint (walī), some say a prophet, some say a messenger, and the best of affairs is the middle ones.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'ṣaḥābī' (companion) from the word 'ṣaḥb' in the text. It first clarifies the linguistic nature of 'ṣuḥb': it is a collective noun, not a broken plural, though it has a singular 'ṣāḥib'. The technical definition follows: a companion is anyone who met the Prophet after his mission while believing in him, in a customary meeting place (i.e., anywhere on earth), even if the person never saw the Prophet or narrated from him, and even if they were unable to visually distinguish him. The condition 'died in Islam' applies to the continuation of companionship, not its initial existence. Apostasy before death cancels the status (e.g., ʿAbd Allāh b. Khaṭal). If the person returns to faith (e.g., ʿAbd Allāh b. Abī Sarḥ), companionship returns but without reward according to Shāfiʿīs; Mālikīs are uncertain. The passage then lists unusual inclusions: blind people (like Ibn Umm Maktūm), Jesus, al-Khiḍr, Elias, and angels who met the Prophet on earth. Jesus is declared the last companion among visible humans; angels continue until the Trumpet. The status of al-Khiḍr and Elias is discussed: both are alive according to the relied view, with Elias being a prophet (by Qurʾān), while al-Khiḍr's prophetic status is debated. The commentary thus expands the definition of 'companion' far beyond the usual understanding.

Key Terms

ابن أم مكتوم
عيسى
الخضر
إلياس
ملائكة

Correction: The passage includes blind persons, prophets, and angels who met the Prophet, broadening the definition.

The definition of companion is broad enough to include the blind, prophets, and angels, with specific rulings on their statuses and lifespans.

Meaning of 'Ḥizb' in the Poem
Defines 'ḥizb' linguistically and interprets its reference in the poem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two possible interpretations of the word 'ḥizb' in the poem and why the commentator prefers the broader meaning of all followers.

Core Idea
The word 'ḥizb' in the poem is best understood as all followers of the Prophet, regardless of time.

In this short passage, the commentator unpacks the word 'ḥizb' from the line 'wa-ḥizbihi'. He first gives a linguistic definition, then explores two possible meanings in context, and finally selects the more inclusive interpretation. Understanding this term helps clarify the scope of the Prophet's 'party' mentioned in the creed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the Quranic citation used to support the linguistic definition.
2
Understand the linguistic definition of 'ḥizb'
The commentator defines 'ḥizb' as a group whose affair is united, whether in good or evil. Identify the Quranic verse he cites.
This definition is neutral—it applies to both positive and negative groups.
3
Identify the two interpretations
Find the two possible meanings for 'ḥizb' in the poem: 1) the most intimate circle of the Prophet (special of the special), 2) all followers absolutely.
The first interpretation is more specific than 'companions' and 'family'.
4
Understand the preference and reasoning
The commentator prefers the second interpretation because it is more general, and the word 'āl' cannot cover all followers due to its restriction to the pious.
This shows the commentator values inclusivity in defining the Prophet's party.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَحِزْبِهِ) أي: جماعته ، والحزب الجماعة الذين أَمْرُهُم واحدٌ في خير أو شر ، ومنه : ﴿كُلُّ حِزْبٍ بِمَا لَدَيْهِمْ فَرِحُونَ﴾ [المؤمنون: ٥٣] ، والظاهر أن المراد به هنا مَنْ غلبت ملازمته له ، فهو خاص الخاص ؛ لأنهم أخصُّ من الصحب الذين هم أخص من الآل، ويحتمل أن يُراد به أتباعُهُ مطلقاً، سواءٌ كانوا في عصره أم لا، وهو أولى؛ لما فيه من التعميم، ولا يغني عنه الآلُ؛ لتخصيص بعضهم له بالأتقياء.
His statement: 'and his party' (wa-ḥizbihi) i.e., his group. 'Ḥizb' is the group whose affair is united, whether in good or evil. From it is: 'Each party rejoices in what they have' (Q 23:53). The apparent [meaning] here is the one whose constant association with him [the Prophet] predominates, so it is the special of the special, because they are more specific than the companions who themselves are more specific than the family. It is also possible that what is meant is his followers absolutely, whether they were in his era or not, and this is preferable because of the generality it entails. The word 'āl' does not suffice for this because it is restricted by some to the pious.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage interprets the word 'ḥizb' (حزب) in the line 'wa-ḥizbihi' from the poem. It defines 'ḥizb' linguistically as a group united by a common purpose, whether good or evil, citing the Quranic verse 'each party rejoices in what they have' (Q 23:53). The commentator then offers two interpretive possibilities for the poem's usage: (1) It refers to those whose constant association with the Prophet predominates — i.e., the most intimate circle, even more specific than the 'ṣaḥb' (companions), who are themselves more specific than the 'āl' (family). (2) It could refer to all his followers, regardless of time. The commentator prefers the second, because it is more general, and noting that 'āl' cannot cover this since it is sometimes restricted to the pious. Thus 'ḥizb' here is understood in its broadest possible sense.

جماعة
خاص الخاص
أتباع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'ḥizb' refers only to a partisan faction or a small elite group within the Prophet's followers.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that 'ḥizb' can mean all followers absolutely, and this is the preferable interpretation for the poem.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is more specific
B) Because it is more general and the word 'āl' does not cover all followers
C) Because it is the only meaning found in the Quran
D) Because it excludes non-pious followers

Key Takeaway
The commentator interprets 'ḥizb' in the poem as all followers of the Prophet, both contemporaries and later generations, because this meaning is more inclusive and avoids the restrictiveness of the term 'āl'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Meaning of 'Hizb' in the Poem
Defines 'Hizb' and discusses its intended meaning in the poem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'Hizb' as used in the poem and the two interpretations of its scope.

Core Idea
'Hizb' means a group with a unified purpose, and its scope can be narrow or broad.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the term 'Hizb' from the poem. He defines it lexically as a group united in a common cause, quotes a Quranic verse, and then presents two possible interpretations within the context. The first restricts it to those with constant association to the Prophet, while the second includes all followers. The preferred interpretation favors generality, encompassing all who follow the Prophet regardless of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the full meaning. Pay attention to the lexical definition and the Quranic citation.
The passage is short but packed with nuance.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The author presents two interpretations of 'Hizb' in this context. First, the apparent meaning ('الظاهر') referring to those with constant association. Second, a broader meaning ('ويحتمل') encompassing all followers absolutely. Note the reasoning and preference given.
The terms 'خاص الخاص' (special of the special), 'صحب' (companions), and 'آل' (family) are used to show degrees of specificity.
3
Reflect on the author's reasoning
Consider why the author favors the more general interpretation: it includes all followers and is not restricted to the pious like 'آل'. How does this affect the meaning of the blessing recited in the poem?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary

Knowledge and Types of Ignorance
Definition of knowledge and types of ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of knowledge and the distinction between simple and compound ignorance as presented in the classical Aqeedah tradition.

Core Idea
Knowledge is seeing things as they are; ignorance is blindness to reality or to one's own blindness.

This lesson introduces the foundational kalam term 'knowledge' (al-‘ilm) and its opposite, ignorance (al-jahl). The commentator draws on definitions from al-Rāghib and Shaykh al-Islām, then distinguishes two types of ignorance: simple absence of knowledge and the more dangerous compound ignorance where a person is unaware of their own ignorance. Understanding these terms is essential for later discussions about Allah's attributes and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of knowledge
Start by reading the Arabic passage and translation for the definition of knowledge. Notice the key phrase 'idrāk al-shay' bi-ḥaqīqatihi' (apprehension of a thing in its reality).
This definition emphasizes correspondence with reality.
2
Understand the two types of ignorance
The passage then explains simple and compound ignorance. Simple ignorance is a lack of knowledge where knowledge should exist. Compound ignorance is a mismatch plus unknowingness.
Compound ignorance is more serious because it blocks learning.
3
Reflect on the poem
The poem illustrates the tragedy of compound ignorance: being ignorant yet unaware of it. Read it and consider why the author included it.
The poem reinforces the definition in an emotional way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1). والعلم : إدراك الشيء بحقيقته كما قاله الراغب (۲) ، وهو كقول شيخ الإسلام: إدراك الشيء على ما هو به (۳)، ويطلق حقيقة عرفية على القواعد المدوّنة، وعلى الملكة التي يُقتدرُ بها على إدراكات جزئية ) ، والمراد هنا الأول؛ بدليل الحكم عليه بالتحتُم، ومقابله الجهلُ؛ وهو إما بسيط وإما مركب فالأول: عدم العلم بالشيء شأنه العلم والثاني : إدراك الشيء على خلاف ما هو عليه في الواقع، وإنما سمي مركباً لاستلزامه جهلين؛ جهله بالشيء، وجهله بأنه جاهل؛ وفي ذلك قيل : [من الطويل] جَهِلْتَ وَمَا تَدْرِي بِأَنَّكَ جَاهِلٌ وَمَنْ لِي بِأَنْ تَدْرِي بِأَنَّكَ لَا تَدْرِي ؟ (٥)
(1). And knowledge: is apprehension of a thing in its reality as al-Rāghib said, and it is like the statement of Shaykh al-Islām: apprehension of a thing as it is. And it is applied conventionally to codified rules, and to the faculty by which one is able to have particular perceptions. And what is intended here is the first meaning, as evidenced by the ruling that it is obligatory. Its opposite is ignorance: which is either simple or compound. The first: absence of knowledge concerning something that is normally the object of knowledge. The second: apprehension of a thing contrary to what it is in reality. It is called compound because it entails two ignorances: ignorance of the thing, and ignorance that one is ignorant. Concerning that it is said [from the ṭawīl meter]: 'You were ignorant and you do not know that you are ignorant, and who will give me that you know that you do not know?' (5).
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage defines knowledge (al-‘ilm) and contrasts it with ignorance (al-jahl). The commentator begins by citing two definitions: al-Rāghib al-Iṣfahānī's definition of knowledge as 'apprehension of a thing in its reality', and Shaykh al-Islām Zakarīyā al-Anṣārī's similar definition as 'apprehension of a thing as it is'. He notes that the term 'knowledge' can also be used conventionally to refer to codified bodies of knowledge or to the mental faculty enabling particular perceptions, but here the intended meaning is the first (apprehension of reality) because it is described as obligatory (muḥtam). The opposite of knowledge is ignorance, which has two types: simple ignorance and compound ignorance. Simple ignorance is the absence of knowledge regarding something that could be known. Compound ignorance is to apprehend something contrary to its true nature, called 'compound' because it entails two forms of ignorance: being ignorant of the thing itself, and being ignorant of the fact that one is ignorant. The poem cited illustrates this tragic state: one is ignorant but does not realize it, and wishes to know that he does not know.

Key Terms
العلم
الجهل البسيط
الجهل المركب
إدراك
حقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Compound ignorance is simply having wrong information, without the additional layer of being unaware of one's ignorance.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that compound ignorance requires two ignorances: ignorance of the thing and ignorance that one is ignorant. The person not only holds a false belief but is also unaware of their error.

Checkpoint
What is the defining feature of compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab) according to the passage?
A) It is the absence of all knowledge.
B) It involves two people being ignorant.
C) It entails ignorance of a thing plus ignorance that one is ignorant.
D) It is having incorrect information.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is apprehending reality as it is, while compound ignorance is the dangerous state of being wrong and unaware of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The poem mourns the state of not knowing that you do not know. Are you willing to ask others to point out your blind spots? What fear holds you back?

The Meaning of Divine Acceptance (Qabūl)
Definition of qabūl with two meanings and a clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of qabūl (acceptance) in Islamic theology: as divine reward for correct action and as contentment without objection, and why the latter is specified.

Core Idea
Acceptance is either divine reward or contentment without objection—never with displeasure.

The author defines the term qabūl, which appears in the hope statement. He gives two possible meanings: one as reward for good deeds, and another as contentment with something while refraining from objection. This nuance clarifies the nature of divine acceptance and guards against misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without parsing. Then read the English translation to grasp the full definition.
Notice the use of the particle 'قيل' indicating an alternative opinion.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage gives two definitions of qabūl: (1) reward for correct action, (2) contentment with something while refraining from objection. Mark them in the text.
3
Understand the refinement
The author explains why the second definition adds 'while refraining from objection': because contentment can coexist with objection, but true acceptance is without displeasure.
The verse from Ibn Malik supports this distinction.
4
Connect to the broader context
Consider how this definition fits the author's hope for divine acceptance. The dual meaning enriches the concept of acceptance.
This lesson is a key term clarification, not a full argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فِي القَبُولِ) أي : في حصول ،القبول فهو على تقدير مضاف، ومعنى القبول : الإثابة على العمل الصحيح، وقيل: الرضا بالشيء مع ترك الاعتراض عليه. وإنما قلنا : مع ترك الاعتراض لأن الرضا قد يكون مع اعتراض كما يقتضيه قول ابن مالك : [من الرجز] وَنَقْتَضِي رِضاً بِغَيْرِ سُخْطِ نبه على ذلك الشيخ الملوي (٢). (٢) ونقله عنه العلامة الأمير في حاشيته» (ص ٣٤).
His saying: 'in acceptance' i.e., in attaining acceptance, so it is on the assumption of an omitted noun. The meaning of acceptance: rewarding for correct action, and it is said: contentment with something while refraining from objection. We said 'while refraining from objection' because contentment may be accompanied by objection, as indicated by the saying of Ibn Malik: 'And we require satisfaction without displeasure'. The scholar al-Mallawi pointed this out. (2) Reported by al-Amir in his gloss.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'qabūl' (acceptance) in the context of the author's hope. It gives two meanings: (1) divine reward (ithāba) for correct action, and (2) contentment (riḍā) with something while refraining from objection. The author explains that the latter is specified because contentment can coexist with objection, but true acceptance is without displeasure, as cited from Ibn Malik's poetry.

Key Takeaway

Istikhdām: The Rhetorical Device of Dual Reference
The author explains the grammatical case of 'nāfi'an' and the rhetorical device of istikhdām, with a footnote giving a formal definition and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rhetorical device of istikhdām and how it resolves a grammatical objection in the poem.

Core Idea
Istikhdām allows a word to be used in one sense and its pronoun in another.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the grammatical status of 'nāfi'an' and identifies the use of istikhdām. This device explains how a single word can refer to both its literal form and its meaning, resolving an apparent contradiction. The footnote provides a richer definition and examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two grammatical issues
Look for the identification of 'nāfi'an' as a ḥāl and the mention of istikhdām in relation to 'bihā'. Notice how the author presents the objection and its resolution.
The main text is concise; the footnote expands on istikhdām.
2
Study the definition of istikhdām and the two examples
In the footnote, read al-Jurjani's definition carefully. Then examine the two poetic examples to see how the device works in practice.
The first example uses 'sky' (samā') to mean rain, then the pronoun refers to grass.
3
Connect istikhdām to the objection in the main text
How does invoking istikhdām repel the objection that benefit comes from meaning, not the word? Reflect on the dual reference of the pronoun in 'bihā'.
The pronoun first refers to the word, then to the meaning.
4
Consider the nuance about 'urjūza' vs 'jawhara'
The footnote notes that intending the word (lafẓ) is clear for 'jawhara' but not for 'urjūza'. Think about why this distinction matters.
This shows careful analysis of the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (نَافِعاً) حال من الاسم الكريم، وقوله : (بها) أي بالأرجوزة أو بالجوهرة، وفي كلامه استخدام (۳)؛ حيث أطلق الأرجوزة أو الجوهرة أولاً وأراد اللفظ (٤)، وأعاد الضمير عليها وأراد المعنى، فاندفع النظر بأن النفع بمعناها لا يلفظها الذي هو الاسم المراد فيما تقدم. واستُشكل جعل (نافعاً) حالاً من الاسم الكريم؛ بأنه يقتضي أنه لو لم يحصل نفع بهذه المقدمة، لا الله ! يرجو (۳) قال العلامة الشريف الجرجاني في تعريفاته» (ص (۲۲) (الاستخدام : هو أن يُذكر لفظ له معنيان فيراد به أحدهما، ثم يراد بالضمير الراجع إلى ذلك اللفظ معناه الآخر، أو يراد بأحد ضميريه أحد معنييه ، ثم بالآخر معناه الآخر؛ فالأول كقوله : إذا نزل السماءُ بِأرض قوم رعيناه وإن كانُوا غِضابا أراد بالسماء : الغيث، وبالضمير الراجع إليه من رعيناه النبت، والسماء يطلق عليهما، والثاني كقوله : فسقى الغضى والساكنيه وإن هُمُ شبوه بين جوانحي وضُلوعي : أراد بأحد الضميرين الراجعين إلى الغضى وهو المجرور في الساكنيه المكان، وبالآخر وهو منصوب في شبوه - النار ؛ أي: أوقدوا بين جوانحي نار الغضى، يعني نار الهوى التي تشبه نار الغضى. (٤) قوله : (وأراد اللفظ) ظاهر بالنسبة لجوهرة، وغير ظاهر بالنسبة لأرجوزة؛ لأنَّ المراد منها المعنى، حيث قال فيما تقدم : أي : منظومة من بحر الرجز ... إلى آخره . انتهى أجهوري .
His saying: 'beneficial' (nāfi'an) is a circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) from the noble name. His saying: 'with it' (bihā) i.e., with the rajaz poem or with the jawhara. In his speech there is istikhdām (usage) where he first used the word rajaz poem or jawhara intending the word (lafẓ), then he returned the pronoun to it intending the meaning, so the objection that benefit is for its meaning not its word is repelled. It was objected that making 'nāfi'an' a ḥāl from the noble name implies that if no benefit comes from this introduction, then Allah... he hopes? [text cut off] (3) Al-Sharif al-Jurjani said in his Definitions: 'Istikhdām: that a word with two meanings is mentioned, one meaning intended, then the pronoun referring to that word intends the other meaning; or one of its two pronouns intends one meaning, and the other intends the other meaning.' The first example: 'When the rain falls upon a land, we graze it even if they are angry' – he intended by 'rain' (al-samā') the rain, and by the pronoun in 'graze it' the plants, and rain (al-samā') applies to both. The second example: 'He watered the ghada trees and their inhabitants, even if they kindled it between my ribs and collarbone' – he intended by one pronoun the place, and by the other the fire. (4) His saying: 'and intended the word' is apparent regarding 'jawhara', but not apparent regarding 'urjūza' because the intended meaning of it is the rajaz poem... etc.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a rhetorical and grammatical analysis. The author identifies 'nāfi'an' as a circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) referring back to 'Allah' (the noble name). The phrase 'bihā' can refer either to the poem (urjūza) or the 'jawhara'. The author points out the usage of 'istikhdām' — a rhetorical device where a word with two meanings is used with one meaning, then the pronoun refers back to the other meaning. Here, the author first says 'with it' intending the word (lafẓ), then returns the pronoun to intend the meaning. This resolves an objection. The footnote defines istikhdām in detail with examples from poetry.

Key Terms
استخدام
حال
لفظ
معنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that when a pronoun is used, it always refers to the same aspect (word or meaning) as its antecedent.
Correction: Istikhdām shows that a pronoun can refer to a different aspect of the same word, such as shifting from the word itself to its meaning.

Checkpoint
What rhetorical device is identified in the phrase 'bihā' where the first reference is to the word and the pronoun to the meaning?
A) Kināyah
B) Istikhdām
C) Taqhāb
D) Ijāz

Key Takeaway
Istikhdām is a rhetorical device where a word used in one sense has its pronoun refer to another sense, resolving apparent contradictions.

The author used istikhdām to resolve an objection about benefit coming from meaning, not words. Reflect on a time when someone objected to your beliefs or statements. ?
The examples of istikhdām involve poetry and eloquent speech. Consider the words you use in your daily worship or conversations about faith. Are you mindful when the same word might carry different meanings for you and your listener? How might this awareness deepen your sincerity and connection with others?

The Ḥāl Muqaddara: A Circumstantial Accusative for Future Intention
Commentator argues for 'nāfi'an' as ḥāl muqaddara.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator treats the word 'nāfi'an' as a circumstantial accusative of future intention (ḥāl muqaddara) and how this resolves the objection about the timing of benefit.

Core Idea
Strong hope makes future benefit feel present, so the grammar allows a future-intended circumstantial accusative.

The commentator addresses an objection: the poet says 'hoping for benefit', but benefit comes later. He explains that the strong hope makes the benefit seem present, so using a circumstantial accusative is valid, specifically a future-intended one. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and the two types of circumstantial accusative.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
The passage begins with an implied objection. Read the Arabic and translation to see how the commentator answers it.
2
Understand the concept of ḥāl muqaddara
The footnote defines two types of circumstantial accusative. Focus on the future-intended type and its examples.
The verse 'Enter it, remaining forever' is a Quranic example.
3
Apply to the poet's line
See why 'nāfi'an' is considered future-intended and why this is acceptable despite bad manners.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب بأنه لما تقوى رجاؤه في النفع بها، صار محققاً، فكأنه موجود في سائر الأحوال، وحينئذ فلا ضرر في تقييد الرجاء بالنفع، ويصح جعله حالاً من فاعل (أرجو)، لكنه بعيد ; إذ فيه إساءة أدب ; حيث جعل نفسه نافعاً، وعلى كل فهي حال مقدرة (1) ; لأن النفع بها متأخّرُ عن زمن نُطق المصنف بذلك، لا سيما إن كانت الخطبة متقدمة على التأليف. وقوله : (مُريداً أي شخصاً مريداً ، فهو صفة لموصوف محذوف، وذلك المحذوف مفعول لقوله : (نافعاً) لأنه اسم فاعل يعمل عمل الفعل، ولفظ : (مريداً) وإن كان نكرة في سياق الإثبات المراد به كل مريد; لأن النكرة في سياق الإثبات قد تعم ; كما يدلُّ لذلك المقام والسياق والمتعلق بـ (مريداً) محذوف; أي: مريداً لها القراءة، أو حفظاً، أو غير ذلك. (1) تنقسم الحال باعتبار الزمان إلى مقارنة ومقدرة، فالمقارنة كقولك: جاء زيد ماشياً، والمقدرة وتكون في الاستقبال كقولك : مررت برجل معه صقر صائداً به غداً ; أي: مقدراً الصيد به غداً، وكقوله سبحانه: ﴿فَادْخُلُوهَا خَلِدِينَ ﴾ [الزمر : ٧٣].
And it was answered that when his hope in benefiting by it became strong, it became realized, so it is as if existing in all states, and then there is no harm in restricting hope to benefit, and it is valid to make it a circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) from the agent of 'I hope', but it is far-fetched, for it involves bad manners, since he made himself beneficial. In any case, it is a circumstantial accusative of future intention (ḥāl muqaddara) (1), because benefiting by it is delayed from the time the author uttered that, especially if the sermon preceded the composition. And his statement: 'murīdan' meaning a person who intends, so it is an attribute of an omitted described noun, and that omitted noun is the object of his statement 'nāfi'an' because it is a participle acting like a verb. The word 'murīdan' although indefinite in a positive context, what is meant is every intend-er, because the indefinite in a positive context may have generality, as the position and context indicate. The object attached to 'murīdan' is omitted, i.e., intending it for reading, or memorization, or other. (1) The circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) is divided with respect to time into concomitant (muqārina) and future-intended (muqaddara). The concomitant is like your saying: 'Zayd came walking'. The future-intended is used for the future, like your saying: 'I passed by a man who had a falcon, intending to hunt with it tomorrow', i.e., assuming his hunting with it tomorrow, and like His saying, the Exalted: 'Enter it, remaining forever' [al-Zumar: 73].
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection about the poet's use of 'nāfi'an' and 'murīdan'. The commentator explains the grammar: the circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) is used to indicate that the hope of benefit is considered realized, so it is like a present state, though the actual benefit is future. He then analyzes 'murīdan' as an adjective for an omitted noun, and as a participle. He explains that the indefinite 'murīdan' can be general. The footnote clarifies the two types of ḥāl: concomitant and future-intended, giving examples. The commentator resolves a potential contradiction by showing that the ḥāl can be future-intended, thus the poet's wording is valid.

Key Terms
الحال
الحال المقارنة
الحال المقدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The circumstantial accusative must always be simultaneous with the verb.
Correction: The grammar allows a future-intended circumstantial accusative (ḥāl muqaddara) that indicates a state expected in the future.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, which type of circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) is used for 'nāfi'an'?
A) Concomitant (muqārina)
B) Future-intended (muqaddara)
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
A circumstantial accusative can refer to a future state when the hope is so strong it is treated as present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you truly hope for something from Allah, does your hope become so real that you act as if it has already come—like the poet's ḥāl muqaddara?
?
In your daily interactions, do you ever catch yourself attributing benefit to created beings as the ultimate source, forgetting that Allah is the true benefactor?
?
Is there a specific hope you hold so strongly that you can already feel it present in your life, and what would change if you lived with that certainty?

Analysis of 'Murīdan': Adjective and Indefinite Generality
Commentator analyzes 'murīdan' as an adjective with omitted object and discusses indefinite generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical analysis of 'murīdan' as an adjective for an omitted noun, and why the indefinite in a positive context can imply generality.

Core Idea
An indefinite noun in a positive context can be general, and 'murīdan' is an adjective describing an unspecified intentional person.

After explaining the first term, the commentator turns to 'murīdan'. He shows that it is an adjective (ṣifa) for an omitted noun (the person who intends), and that this omitted noun is the object of the previous participle 'nāfi'an'. He then clarifies that the indefinite 'murīdan' in this positive context can mean 'every intender', not necessarily a specific one. This lesson breaks down that analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical role of 'murīdan'

The described noun is omitted.
2
Understand the omitted object
The omitted noun is the object of 'nāfi'an'. See how the participle works like a verb.
3
Learn the rule of indefinite in positive context
The commentator mentions that indefinite in a positive context can have generality. Consider the context and evidence.
This rule is important for understanding the meaning of the poet's line.
