The Necessary: Definition and Divisions
Sanusi defines the necessary, answers an objection, and divides it into two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the necessary (wajib) in kalam, how it is defended against an objection, and its division into necessary-by-necessity (daruri) and necessary-by-demonstration (nazari).

Core Idea
The necessary is that which cannot be non-existent, regardless of any intellect.

Before we can discuss the attributes of Allah, we need a clear understanding of the term 'necessary' (wajib). This passage by Sanusi defines it carefully, addresses a subtle objection about imagining impossibility, and then divides it into two categories. Pay close attention to how the definition is refined to avoid dependence on the intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its variations
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noticing the two ways the definition is parsed (agent vs. object).
The definition involves 'conception' (tasawwur) but is later clarified as 'assent' (tasdiq).
2
Follow the objection and answer
Notice the objection that the soul can imagine the non-existence of the necessary. Then see how the answer refines 'conception' to 'assent'.
3
Grasp the preferred definition
The author offers a better definition that does not tie the necessary to the intellect. Understand why this is preferred.
The necessary is necessary in itself.
4
Learn the two divisions
The passage ends with two types: daruri (conditional, like a body occupying space) and nazari (unconditional, like Allah's attributes).
Note that daruri is contingent on something else, while nazari is absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد عرَّفوا الواجب في هذا الفنّ بأنه : ما لا يتصور في العقل عدمه (۳)، ببناء الفعل للفاعل؛ أي ما لا يمكن بسبب العقل عدمه، أو للمفعول؛ أي: ما لا تدرك النفس بسبب العقل عدمه، لكن يرد على هذا أن النفس قد تدرك عدم الواجب؛ لأن المحال قد يُنصور (۱)؛ أي: يدرك. ويجاب بأن المراد بالتصور هنا : التصديق (۲)، والمعنى حينئذ ما لا تصدق النفس بسبب العقل بعدمِهِ. وعُلمَ من هذا : أن العقل آلةٌ في الإدراك؛ والمدرك إنما هو النفس. والأولى عدم ربط الواجب بالعقل، فيُقالُ : الواجب : هو ما لا يقبل الانتفاء؛ لأنَّ الواجب واجبٌ في نفسه وُجد عقل أو لم يوجد. والواجب قسمان : ضروري : كتحيز الجرم؛ أي: أخذه قَدْراً من الحيز، وهو المكان؛ فإنه ما دام الجرم موجوداً يجب أن يتحيّز ، فهو واجب مقيَّد بدوام الجرم (۳). ونظري : كصفاتِ الله تعالى (٤).
And they defined the necessary in this art as: that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the intellect (3), with the verb built for the agent: i.e., that whose non-existence cannot occur by reason, or for the object: i.e., that whose non-existence the soul cannot perceive by reason. But an objection arises to this: that the soul may perceive the non-existence of the necessary because the impossible may be imagined (1); i.e., perceived. And it is answered that what is meant by 'conception' here is 'assent' (2), and the meaning then is that whose non-existence the soul does not assent to by reason. And from this it is known that the intellect is an instrument in perception, and the perceiver is only the soul. And the better [view] is not to tie the necessary to the intellect, so it is said: The necessary is that which does not accept non-existence; because the necessary is necessary in itself whether an intellect exists or not. And the necessary is of two divisions: Necessary by necessity (daruri): like the occupation of space by a body; i.e., its taking up a measure of space, which is place; for as long as the body exists it must occupy space, so it is a necessary [attribute] conditional on the body's duration (3). And necessary by demonstration (nazari): like the attributes of Allah, the Exalted (4).
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents the definition and classification of the necessary (wājib) in the science of kalām. First, it gives the standard definition: that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the intellect. The definition is analyzed grammatically: if the verb 'to imagine' is built for the agent, it means that reason cannot conceive its non-existence; if built for the object, it means that the soul cannot perceive its non-existence through reason. An objection is raised: the soul can conceive the non-existence of the necessary because it can imagine impossible things (like the breaking of a necessary connection). The answer is that 'conception' here means 'assent' (taṣdīq) – i.e., the soul does not give intellectual assent to its non-existence. From this, we learn that the intellect is merely an instrument; the true perceiver is the soul. The author then proposes a better formulation: do not tie the definition to the intellect at all; instead, define the necessary as that which does not admit of non-existence, because it is necessary in itself whether or not any intellect exists. Finally, the necessary is divided into two types: (1) darūrī (necessary by necessity) – like a body occupying space: as long as the body exists, it must occupy space, so this necessity is conditional on the body's existence; (2) naẓarī (necessary by demonstration) – like the attributes of Allah, which are necessary unconditionally.

Key Terms
الواجب
التصور
التصديق
ضروري
نظري
تحيز الجرم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The necessary's definition depends on the existence of an intellect or on the ability to conceive it.
Correction: The necessary is necessary in itself; the definition should not be tied to the intellect. It is better to say: the necessary is that which does not accept non-existence.

Checkpoint
What is the preferred definition of the necessary (wajib) according to the passage?
A) That whose non-existence is not conceivable in the intellect
B) That which does not accept non-existence
C) That which exists eternally
D) That which cannot be perceived by the senses

Key Takeaway
The necessary is what must exist by itself, not depending on any intellect, and it is either conditional (like a body's occupation of space) or unconditional (like Allah's attributes).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on Allah's attributes, do you ever act as if they depend on your understanding or feelings? How does knowing that they are necessary in themselves—regardless of any intellect—change how you trust His perfection even when you cannot grasp it?
?
This lesson distinguishes between what is necessary by itself and what is conditional. In your own life, do you treat anything as absolute that is actually conditional? What would it mean to reorient your certainties toward only that which truly does not accept non-existence?
?
The author says the intellect is an instrument and the soul is the perceiver. When you doubt Allah's attributes due to intellectual confusion, do you remember that your soul must assent to the truth? How can you train your soul to affirm what the intellect knows, even when the imagination rebels?

Definition of al-alam (the world) in Kalam
Sanusi defines al-alam and dismisses narrow interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of al-alam (the world) in Islamic theology and why restrictive interpretations are rejected.

Core Idea
In kalam, the world means all created existents, nothing less.

Before we can discuss God's relationship to creation, we need a clear definition of 'the world.' The author gives a precise theological definition and warns against narrowing it without evidence. This passage establishes the scope of the created realm.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author first gives a positive definition, then excludes non-existents, and finally lists and rejects restrictive views.
The passage uses a pattern: definition → exclusion → enumeration of incorrect views.
2
Understand the core definition
Identify what is included: everything other than Allah and His attributes among existents and states. Note that 'states' refers to accidents or modes of being.
The term 'states' (الأحوال) is a technical term in Ash'ari kalam.
3
Recognize what is excluded
Non-existents (المعدومات) are not part of the world, whether they are possible (like a future child) or impossible (like a partner to God). Only actual existents count.
4
Examine the restrictive views
The passage lists five restrictive definitions some scholars proposed. Note each: 1) only living beings, 2) only humans and jinn, 3) only angels, 4) the three plus devils, 5) only people of Paradise and Hell.
The author rejects all as lacking evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن (العالم) بفتح اللام : اسمٌ لما سوى الله وصفاته من الموجودات والأحوال على القول بها، وأما المعدوماتُ فليست من العالم، سواء كانت ممكنة كولي لزيد قبل وجوده أو مستحيلةً كالشريك، وبعضُهم خص (العالم) بذي الروح، وبعضُهم خصَّهُ بالإنس والجن، وبعضُهم خصَّهُ بالملائكة، وبعضُهم خصَّهُ بالثلاثة مع الشياطين، وبعضُهم خصَّهُ بأهل الجنة والنار، لكن لا دليل على ذلك كله، ذكره المصنف في شرحه الصغير
And know that 'al-alam' (with fatḥ on the lām) is a name for what is other than Allah and His attributes among existents and states according to the opinion about them. As for non-existents, they are not part of the world, whether they are possible like a son for Zayd before his existence or impossible like a partner. And some of them restricted 'al-alam' to that which has a soul, and some of them restricted it to humans and jinn, and some of them restricted it to angels, and some of them restricted it to the three along with devils, and some of them restricted it to the people of Paradise and Hellfire, but there is no evidence for all of that. The author mentioned it in his Small Commentary.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-alam' (the world) as used in kalam. The author clarifies that the world is everything other than Allah and His attributes, including both substances and accidents (states). Non-existents are excluded, whether they are possible (like a future child) or impossible (like a partner to God). He then lists various restrictive definitions some scholars proposed—limiting the world to living beings, humans and jinn, angels, the three groups plus devils, or the people of Paradise and Hell—but rejects all of them as unsupported by evidence. The correct definition is the broad one encompassing all created beings.

Key Terms
العالم
المعدومات
الممكنات
المستحيلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People commonly think 'the world' refers only to living beings or to humans.
Correction: In kalam, al-alam includes all created existents, animate and inanimate, spiritual and material, excluding only Allah and His attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which of the following is NOT included in the definition of 'al-alam'?
A) Possible non-existents
B) Angels
C) Humans and jinn
D) The throne of Allah

Key Takeaway
Al-alam is everything that exists besides Allah and His attributes, and no narrower definition is acceptable.

?
The passage excludes non-existents from the world. Yet you often worry about things that do not yet exist—future trials or imagined catastrophes. How does realizing that these are not part of the actual world free you from anxiety? What will you consciously do when you next feel overwhelmed by an imaginary scenario?

The Meaning of Badī' and the Emphasis of Lakīn
Commentator defines badī' and explains rhetorical use of lakīn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the attribute 'badī'' means created without prior example (necessarily temporal) and that 'lākin' in this context is used for emphasis, not correction.

Core Idea
Badī' means created without prior model; lakīn here emphasizes, not corrects.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a potential misunderstanding about the divine attribute 'badī''. Some might think it implies pre-existence, but the commentator explains that 'badī'' means invented without prior example, which necessarily entails temporality. He also explains that the word 'lakīn' is used for emphasis, not for correction, as shown by a Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The passage is a commentary on a line of poetry discussing the world's perfection and temporality. Recognize that the poet indicates the world is temporal despite its perfection.
2
Define badī'
Read the definition: al-badī' is al-mukhtara' min ghayri mithāli sabaq (that which is invented without a prior example). Note that such a thing is necessarily temporal (ḥādith).
3
Identify the misunderstanding
The phrase 'without an example' might be misinterpreted to imply pre-existence (qidam). The commentator addresses this by clarifying that 'lakīn' here is not for correction but for emphasis.
4
Analyze the use of lakīn
The commentator cites Quran 33:40 where 'lākin' emphasizes the status of the Prophet without negating anything. Apply the same understanding to the poem.
5
Apply the lesson
Recognize that the attribute 'badī'' does not imply eternity; rather, it confirms the temporality of creation and the perfection of Allah's creative act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حيث كان كذلك فهو قديم، فكأنه قال : لكن العالم وإن كان على غاية من الإتقان حادث، وبحث فيه بأن البديعَ هو المخترَعُ من غير مثال سبق، والمخترع لا يكون إلا حادثاً ، فلا يُتوهَّمُ القدمُ حتى يُحتاج للاستدراك، إلا أن يُقال: ربما يُتوهم من عَجُز التعريف أعني قولهم: (من غير (مثال لا من صدره وهو المخترع، والأقرب أن؛ : (لكن) هنا لمجرَّدِ التأكيد؛ كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا كَانَ مُحَمَّدٌ أَبَا أَحَدٍ مِّن رِجَالِكُمْ وَلَكِن رَسُولَ اللهِ) [الأحزاب: ١٤٠ (١) .
Since it is thus, it is eternal (qadīm). It is as if he said: but the world, even though it is at the utmost perfection, is temporal (ḥādith). He discussed that al-badī' (the Incomparable) is that which is invented (mukhtara') without a prior example, and the invented is nothing but temporal (ḥādith). So pre-existence (al-qidam) is not imagined such that there is need for correction, except if it is said: perhaps pre-existence might be imagined from the weakness of the definition, I mean their statement: (without an example, not from its beginning, and it is the invented). The more correct is that (lākin) here is merely for emphasis, as in His statement the Exalted: 'And Muhammad is not the father of any of your men, but (lākin) he is the Messenger of Allah' (al-Aḥzāb: 40).
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from the poem, explaining the meaning of 'badī'' and the use of 'lākin'. The commentator begins by stating that if the world were perfect and pre-existing, it would be eternal (qadīm), but the poet indicates it is temporal (ḥādith). He then defines 'badī'' as something created (mukhtara') without a prior model (mithāl sābiq), and such creation is necessarily temporal (ḥādith). Therefore, pre-existence (qidam) is not supposed, so no correction is needed. However, someone might misunderstand the definition's phrase 'without an example' as implying pre-existence from the word's beginning, but the commentator clarifies that 'lākin' here is not for negation (istidrāk) but for emphasis (ta'kīd). He provides a Quranic parallel (al-Aḥzāb: 40) where 'lākin' affirms rather than denies. This guards against misinterpreting 'badī'' as implying eternity.

Key Terms
البديع
المخترع
القدم
الاستدراك
التأكيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the attribute 'badī'' implies pre-existence (qidam) because it describes creation without a prior model.
Correction: The commentator explains that 'badī'' means invented without prior example, and such invention is necessarily temporal (ḥādith), not eternal. The use of 'lakīn' in the poem is merely for emphasis, not to correct a supposed pre-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does the term 'badī'' mean?
A) Something that is eternal and pre-existing.
B) Something invented (mukhtara') without a prior example.
C) Something that is perfectly made but temporal.
D) Something that requires a prior model.

Key Takeaway
The attribute 'badī'' means created without prior example, hence temporal; and 'lakīn' in this context serves emphasis, not correction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a subtle definition like 'badī‘' meaning 'invented without prior example', do you ever let a small phrase sway your understanding of Allah’s attributes without checking the full context? How can you ensure your faith is built on precise meanings, not assumptions?
?
This lesson shows that even a tiny word like 'lakīn' can be misinterpreted, leading to a wrong belief. Are there any terms or verses about Allah’s names or actions that you have taken at face value, missing the deeper nuance? Will you commit to studying them more carefully?
?
The commentator corrected a potential misunderstanding by pointing to another verse of the Quran. When you encounter a theological doubt, do you immediately turn to the Quran for clarification, or do you rely on your own reasoning? How can you train yourself to always anchor your beliefs in revelation?

Understanding 'Tahqiq' in Kalam: Proof and Accurate Presentation
Interpretation of 'tahqiq' as proof or accurate statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two primary meanings of the term 'tahqiq' as used in kalam discourse and why precision in terminology matters.

Core Idea
Tahqiq means either establishing by proof or presenting accurately.

In the study of theological disputes, the word 'tahqiq' appears frequently. The author clarifies its meaning to prevent confusion: it can refer to adducing proof for a claim or to presenting a claim in its true form. This dual definition grounds the earlier discussion of the dispute in methodological clarity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's words guide your initial understanding.
Notice the structure: 'أي ... الذي هو ... فالمعنى ... أو الذي هو ...'.
2
Identify the two meanings
The passage gives two definitions. Underline them: إثبات الشيء بالدليل and ذكر الشيء على الوجه الحقّ.
3
Reflect on why both meanings matter
Consider why the author felt the need to clarify both options. How does each affect understanding of the dispute?
This connects to the need for clear terminology in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بالتحقيق أي متلبساً بالتحقيق الذي هو إثباتُ الشيء بالدليل، فالمعنى متلبساً بالإثبات بالأدلة القائمة على دعوى كلّ من الفريقين (4)، أو الذي هو ذكر الشيء على الوجه الحقِّ ؛ فالمعنى : متلبساً بذكر كل فريق مدعاه على الوجه الحقِّ عنده .
His saying: 'with verification (bi-al-tahqiq)', i.e., clothed with verification, which is establishing a thing by proof, so the meaning is clothed with proof based on the evidence supporting the claim of each of the two groups, or it is mentioning a thing in its true manner; so the meaning is clothed with each group mentioning its claim in its true manner according to it.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This short passage explains the word 'بالتّحقيق' (bi-al-tahqiq) that appears in the earlier line about the dispute. Two possible meanings are given: (1) 'establishing the thing by proof'—i.e., adducing evidence for each claim; (2) 'mentioning the thing in its correct manner'—i.e., each side presenting its claim accurately. This shows the dispute is grounded in evidence and proper presentation.

Key Terms

الإثبات
الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'tahqiq' means empirical verification in a modern scientific sense.
Correction: In kalam, it means either logical proof or accurate statement, not scientific verification.

Checkpoint

A) Establishing by proof or mentioning correctly
B) Proof and certainty
C) Truth and falsehood
D) Condition and part

Key Takeaway
The term 'tahqiq' in kalam can mean either establishing a claim by evidence or presenting it accurately, both of which are essential for sound theological discourse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Two Meanings of al-Tahqiq: Evidence and Correct Statement
Defines al-tahqiq with two possible meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of al-tahqiq as used in Islamic theological discourse.

Core Idea
Al-tahqiq can mean establishing by evidence or stating correctly.

This passage defines a key term that appears in the poem. Understanding the dual meaning of al-tahqiq is essential for following the author's argument about faith and action. The commentator carefully distinguishes two senses: one of proof-based establishment, the other of accurate presentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, noting the two definitions presented. The footnote clarifies which two groups are referenced.
The footnote (4) refers to those who say faith is conditional (shartiyya) or a part (shatariyya).
2
Identify the two definitions
List the two meanings: (1) establishing something by evidence; (2) mentioning something in the true manner. Mark how each is introduced in the text.
The first uses 'إثباتُ الشيء بالدليل', the second 'ذكر الشيء على الوجه الحق'.
3
Consider the purpose
Reflect on why the commentator distinguishes these two meanings. How does this lexical clarification prepare for the upcoming discussion?
It prevents misunderstanding by showing the term can be used in both a rigorous (evidential) and a general (correct statement) sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بالتحقيق أي متلبساً بالتحقيق الذي هو إثباتُ الشيء بالدليل، فالمعنى متلبساً بالإثبات بالأدلة القائمة على دعوى كلّ من الفريقين (٤)، أو الذي هو ذكر الشيء على الوجه الحقِّ ؛ فالمعنى : متلبساً بذكر كل فريق مدعاه على الوجه الحقِّ عنده .
(٤) يعني: القائلين بالشرطية أو الشطرية، وسيأتي ذكر قولهما .
His saying: (bi-al-tahqiq), i.e., clothed in al-tahqiq, which is establishing the thing by evidence. So the meaning is clothed in establishment by the evidences that are based on the claim of each of the two groups (4), or it is mentioning the thing in the true manner. So the meaning is clothed in each group mentioning its claim in the true manner according to it. (4) Meaning: those who say it is a condition (shartiyya) or a part (shatariyya), and their saying will come later.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage defines the word 'al-tahqiq' as used in the poem. The commentator offers two possible interpretations. First, al-tahqiq means establishing something by evidence (dalil); thus the phrase 'bi-al-tahqiq' means 'clothed in establishment by evidences' that support the claim of each of the two groups (those who say action is a condition of faith and those who say it is a part). Second, al-tahqiq means mentioning something in the correct manner; thus the phrase means each group mentions its claim in the true manner according to its own perspective. The footnote clarifies that the two groups referred to are the proponents of the condition (shartiyya) and the part (shatariyya) views, which will be discussed later. This lexical clarification prepares the reader for the upcoming discussion on the nature of faith and action.

Misconception: One might think al-tahqiq only means verification in the sense of 'checking facts'.
Correction: Here it also means stating correctly according to one's own claim, which is a broader sense than mere verification.

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Al-tahqiq has two interrelated meanings: establishing a claim by evidence, and stating a matter correctly; both are used in theological debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ruling on Addressing a Non-Pilgrim as 'Hajj'
Fatwa on addressing a non-pilgrim as 'Hajj'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic ruling on giving the title 'Hajj' to someone who has not performed the pilgrimage, and the distinction between legal and linguistic meanings.

Core Idea
Calling someone 'Hajj' without having performed Hajj is a lie, unless you mean the linguistic sense.

This lesson examines a specific fatwa by al-Shabramallisi regarding the permissibility of addressing a non-pilgrim as 'Hajj' as an honorific. The ruling hinges on whether the statement constitutes a lie (kadhib) or is simply using the word in its original linguistic sense. Understanding this distinction helps clarify how Islamic law evaluates everyday speech.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question
Read the question posed to al-Shabramallisi: Is it forbidden or permissible to call someone who has not performed Hajj as 'Hajj' as a form of respect?
Note the context of social titles.
2
Analyze the general ruling
Al-Shabramallisi answers that it is forbidden (haram) because it is a lie (kadhib). Reflect on why lying is prohibited in Islam.
The ruling is based on the fact that the person has not actually performed Hajj.
3
Learn the exception
The fatwa allows it if the speaker intends the linguistic meaning of 'Hajj' (one who intends a journey). Consider how intention changes the ruling.
The exception shows the importance of linguistic precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسُئل الشَّبْرامَلِّسِيُّ عن قول الشخص لمن لم يحجّ : يا حاج فلان تعظيماً له : هل يحرم أو يجوزُ ؟ فأجاب بالتحريم ؛ لأنه كذب نعم إن قصد المعنى اللغوي؛ كأن أراد يا قاصد التوجه إلى كذا جاز.
And al-Shabramallisi was asked about a person's saying to one who has not performed Hajj: 'O Hajj, so-and-so' as an honorific for him: is it forbidden or permissible? So he answered with prohibition because it is a lie. Yes, if he intends the linguistic meaning – as if he intends 'O one who intends to go to such-and-such' – it is permissible.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage records a fatwa from al-Shabramallisi regarding the ruling of addressing someone who has never performed Hajj with the title 'Hajj' as a form of respect. The question is whether this is forbidden (haram) or permissible (ja'iz). The answer is that it is forbidden because it is a lie – the person has not actually performed the Hajj. However, an exception is made if the speaker intends the linguistic meaning of 'Hajj' which is 'one who intends a journey' (al-qasid). So if he means 'O you who intends to go to such-and-such,' it becomes permissible. The passage thus distinguishes between the legal and linguistic meanings of 'Hajj' in everyday address.

Key Terms
حاج
كذب
المعنى اللغوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Calling someone 'Hajj' as a title is always permissible and respectful.
Correction: It is forbidden if it implies the person has performed Hajj when they have not, unless the linguistic meaning (intending a journey) is intended.

Checkpoint
Why did al-Shabramallisi rule that calling a non-pilgrim 'Hajj' is prohibited?
A) Because it is disrespectful to those who have performed Hajj
B) Because it is a form of lying (kadhib)
C) Because the person might not intend the linguistic meaning
D) Because it is an innovation (bid‘a)

Key Takeaway
Calling someone 'Hajj' without them having performed Hajj is a lie and forbidden, unless one intends the linguistic meaning of 'one who intends a journey'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you used an honorific or title for someone that you knew wasn't accurate. What does this lesson reveal about the weight of your words in Allah's sight? Will you confront that habit starting today?
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly harmless compliment can be a lie if it misrepresents reality. How many of your daily interactions contain small falsehoods for the sake of politeness? Are you ready to be completely truthful, even if it costs you social comfort?
?
The linguistic meaning of 'Hajj' allows a truthful intention. But ask yourself: when you speak, do you care more about the literal truth of your words or about the impression they create? Which will you choose to prioritize starting from this moment?

The Meaning of 'Fadri' in the Matn
The commentator defines the imperative 'fadri' as commanding knowledge derived from comprehension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of the word 'fadri' and its significance as a command to know in the text.

Core Idea
'Fadri' is a command to know, rooted in deep comprehension, not just surface learning.

Before diving into the theological arguments of the Jawharah, the commentator pauses to clarify a single word: 'fadri'. This is not a mere vocabulary lesson — the choice of the word 'dirayah' over 'ilm' signals a particular kind of knowing. In this short gloss, we learn that the text addresses anyone capable of understanding, inviting us into a deeper mode of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic gloss and translation
Start by reading the original Arabic sentence and its literal English translation. Notice how the commentator breaks down the word.
2
Identify the lexical root
Understand that 'fadri' is derived from 'dirayah', which means knowledge or deep comprehension. This connects the word to a specific type of knowing.
The root is d-r-y, not d-r-r.
3
Note the scope of the address
The commentator states that the command is addressed to everyone capable of comprehension. Reflect on why this inclusiveness matters.
It shows the text is meant for all seekers of truth.
4
Differentiate 'dirayah' from 'ilm'
In Islamic scholarship, 'ilm' often refers to knowledge in general, while 'dirayah' implies a deeper, investigative understanding. Consider how this nuance affects your reading.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (فَادْرِ) أي: اعلم من الدراية، وهي العلم والمخاطب بذلك كلُّ من يتأتى منه الدراية والعلم.
His saying 'fadri' means: know, from al-dirayah, which is knowledge, and the one addressed by that is everyone from whom comprehension and knowledge can be expected.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a short lexical gloss on the word 'fadri' (فَادْرِ) that appears in the matn (the poetic text being commented upon). The commentator explains that it is an imperative meaning 'know', derived from the noun 'dirayah' which means knowledge. He further notes that the address is general to anyone capable of understanding and acquiring knowledge.

Key Terms
فادْرِ
دراية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'fadri' is derived from 'durriyah' meaning offspring, or that it is a casual term for 'know'.
Correction: The commentator explicitly roots it in 'dirayah', which denotes a thorough, insightful knowledge, and the address is to anyone capable of deep understanding.

Checkpoint
What does the word 'fadri' mean according to the commentary?
A) Ask.
B) Know.
C) Strive.
D) Remember.

Key Takeaway
The word 'fadri' commands a deep, investigative knowledge (dirayah) available to all who seek it.

Three Views on Qadim and Azali
Three opinions distinguishing qadim from azali among theologians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly distinctions between qadim and azali and how they affect descriptions of divine attributes.

Core Idea
Qadim and azali are not always synonymous; their relationship depends on key conditions.

Theologians carefully defined terms like qadim (eternal) and azali (beginningless) to avoid confusion. This passage presents three nuanced opinions on how these terms relate, each with implications for describing Allah's essence and attributes. Let's explore each view and its consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the three opinions are clearly numbered.
2
Understand the first opinion
The first opinion distinguishes qadim (existent with no beginning) from azali (anything with no beginning, even non-existential). Note that every qadim is azali but not every azali is qadim.
3
Understand the second opinion
The second opinion adds the condition of self-subsistence (qā'im bi-nafsihi) for qadim, while azali remains broader. This is attributed to al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani).
4
Understand the third opinion
The third opinion treats the terms as synonyms, both meaning 'no beginning' regardless of existential or self-subsistence conditions.
5
Apply to divine attributes
See how each view affects whether negative attributes (e.g., 'not having a partner') and positive attributes (e.g., knowledge) can be called qadim or azali.
The commentator ends with 'ponder' to encourage deep reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن لهم في القديم والأزلي ثلاثة أقوال: الأوَّلُ : أن القديم : هو الموجودُ الذي لا ابتداء لوجوده، والأزلي: ما لا أول له سواء كان وجوديا أو عدميًّا، فكل قديم أزلي ولا عكس. الثاني : أن القديم : هو القائم بنفسِهِ الذي لا أوَّلَ لوجوده، والأزليَّ : ما لا أوَّلَ له عدميّاً أو وجوديًّا، قائماً بنفسه أو بغيره، وهذا هو الذي يفهم من كلام السعد. الثالث: أن كلا منهما ما لا أوّل له عدميًا أو وجوديَّاً قائماً بنفسه أو لا، وعلى هذا فهما مترادفان فعلى الأوّل: الصفاتُ السلبيّة لا توصف بالقدم، وتوصف بالأزليَّةِ، بخلاف الذات العلية والصفات الثبوتية فإنَّهما يوصفان (۳) بالقدم والأزليَّة. وعلى الثاني : الصفاتُ مطلقاً لا توصف بالقدم، وتوصف بالأزلية، بخلاف الذات العليَّة، فإنها توصف بكل منهما . وعلى الثالث : كلُّ من الذات والصفاتِ مطلقاً يُوصف بالقدمِ والأزليَّةِ، فتدبَّر.
Know that concerning qadim and azali they have three opinions: The first: that qadim is an existent which has no beginning of its existence, and azali is what has no first, whether it be existential or non-existential. So every qadim is azali, but not vice versa. The second: that qadim is what is self-subsisting and has no beginning of its existence, and azali is what has no first, whether non-existential or existential, self-subsisting or not. This is what is understood from the words of al-Sa'd. The third: that both of them are what has no first, whether non-existential or existential, self-subsisting or not; on this they are synonymous. So on the first view: the negative attributes are not described by qadim, but are described by azali; unlike the supreme Essence and the positive attributes, for they are described by both qadim and azali. On the second: attributes absolutely are not described by qadim, but are described by azali; unlike the supreme Essence, which is described by both. On the third: both the Essence and the attributes absolutely are described by both qadim and azali. So ponder.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage classifies three scholarly opinions on the terms qadim and azali. The first opinion distinguishes them: qadim applies only to existing things with no beginning; azali is broader, applying to any lack of beginning, even non-existential (like negation of nonexistence). So every qadim is azali but not every azali is qadim. The second opinion, attributed to al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani), adds that qadim requires self-subsistence, while azali includes both self-subsisting and nonsubsisting, existential and non-existential. The third opinion treats them as synonyms, both meaning 'no beginning' regardless of other conditions. The commentator then applies these views to attributes: On the first view, negative (non-existential) attributes are azali but not qadim, while the Essence and positive attributes are both qadim and azali. On the second view, all attributes (including positive) are azali but not qadim (since they are not self-subsisting), but the Essence is both. On the third, everything is both. The commentator says 'ponder' (fa-tadabbar) highlighting the need for careful understanding.

Key Terms
القديم
الأزلي
القائم بنفسه
الصفات السلبية
الصفات الثبوتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qadim and azali are always interchangeable.
Correction: Scholars differ: some distinguish them based on existence or self-subsistence, leading to different applications to attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the second opinion, which of the following can be described as qadim?
A) A positive attribute like knowledge
B) A negative attribute like being without a partner
C) The divine Essence
D) Both attributes and the Essence

Key Takeaway
The terms qadim and azali require careful definition; their relationship depends on whether existence and self-subsistence are required, and this affects how we describe Allah's attributes.

Three Opinions on Qadim and Azali
Enumeration of three views on qadim and azali

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly opinions on the terms qadim and azali and how they affect the description of Allah's attributes.

Core Idea
How you define 'qadim' changes which attributes of Allah are called 'qadim' vs 'azali'.

In this passage, the author presents three different ways Muslim scholars have distinguished between the terms qadim and azali. This might seem like a technical language debate, but it has real consequences for how we speak about Allah's Essence and His attributes. Let's examine each view carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation.
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then the translation. Let the author's own presentation come through.
2
Identify the three opinions.
Find the three definitions of qadim and azali. Notice the criteria used: existence, non-existence, self-subsistence.
3
Map the implications for attributes.
Under each opinion, determine which of Allah's attributes (Essence, positive attributes, negative attributes) are described with qadim and which with azali.
4
Reflect on the theological significance.
Consider why defining these terms matters for speaking about Allah's attributes correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن لهم في القديم والأزلي ثلاثة أقوال: الأوَّلُ : أن القديم : هو الموجودُ الذي لا ابتداء لوجوده، والأزلي: ما لا أول له سواء كان وجوديا أو عدميًّا، فكل قديم أزلي ولا عكس. الثاني : أن القديم : هو القائم بنفسِهِ الذي لا أوَّلَ لوجوده، والأزليَّ : ما لا أوَّلَ له عدميّاً أو وجوديًّا ، قائماً بنفسه أو بغيره، وهذا هو الذي يفهم من كلام السعد. الثالث: أن كلا منهما ما لا أوّل له عدميًا أو وجوديَّاً قائماً بنفسه أو لا، وعلى هذا فهما مترادفان فعلى الأوّل: الصفاتُ السلبيّة لا توصف بالقدم، وتوصف بالأزليَّةِ، بخلاف الذات العلية والصفات الثبوتية فإنَّهما يوصفان بالقدم والأزليَّة. وعلى الثاني : الصفاتُ مطلقاً لا توصف بالقدم، وتوصف بالأزلية، بخلاف الذات العليَّة، فإنها توصف بكل منهما . وعلى الثالث : كلُّ من الذات والصفاتِ مطلقاً يُوصف بالقدمِ والأزليَّة، فتدبَّر.
Know that they have three opinions concerning the pre-eternal (qadim) and the eternal (azali). The first: that qadim is the existent whose existence has no beginning, and azali is that which has no first, whether it is existent or non-existent. So every qadim is azali but not vice versa. The second: that qadim is that which is self-subsistent, and has no first to its existence; and azali is that which has no first, whether non-existent or existent, self-subsistent or not. This is what is understood from the speech of al-Sa'd. The third: that each of them is that which has no first, whether non-existent or existent, self-subsistent or not, and on this they are synonymous. According to the first: negative attributes are not described with qidam, but are described with azaliyya, contrary to the exalted Essence and positive attributes, which are described with both qidam and azaliyya. According to the second: attributes absolutely are not described with qidam, but are described with azaliyya, contrary to the exalted Essence, which is described with both. According to the third: both the Essence and the attributes absolutely are described with both qidam and azaliyya. So reflect.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This is a classification passage that enumerates three scholarly opinions on the terms qadim and azali. The first distinguishes qadim as specific to existence without beginning, while azali applies to anything without a beginning, including non-existents; thus qadim is a subset of azali. The second restricts qadim to self-subsistent beings without beginning, while azali is broader. The third makes them synonymous. The passage then explains the implications for describing Allah's Essence and attributes: according to the first view, negative attributes are azali but not qadim; according to the second, all attributes are azali but only the Essence is qadim; according to the third, all are both. The author ends with 'so reflect' (fatadabbar), inviting the reader to contemplate.

The Meaning and Proof of God's Attribute of Baqa'
Al-Bajuri defines baqa' and presents a rational proof for God's eternality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of the attribute of baqa' (eternity) as it applies to God, and the rational proof that establishes it.

Core Idea
Baqa' is not just endless existence; it is the negation of any end to God's existence.

Al-Bajuri now turns to the attribute of baqa' (eternity). After defining it precisely, he presents a concise rational argument that demonstrates why God must be eternal. This lesson will unpack both the definition and the proof, showing how they connect to the previously established attributes of existence and pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of baqa'
Notice how Al-Bajuri explains the phrase 'كذا بقاء' and gives two equivalent definitions: 'the absence of posteriority for existence' and 'non-termination of existence.' What does each phrase emphasize?
The tanwin indicates diversity and greatness.
2
Follow the rational proof
Read the proof carefully. It uses a conditional: if non-existence were possible, then pre-eternity would be impossible. Since pre-eternity has already been proven, non-existence is impossible.
This is a modus tollens argument.
3
Reflect on the universal agreement
Al-Bajuri notes that all rational beings agree on this proposition. Consider why this principle is so fundamental in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( كذا بقاء) التنوين للتنويع والتعظيم؛ أي نوع من أنواع البقاء عظيم مثل المذكور من الوجود والقدم في الوجوب له تعالى، فاسمُ الإشارة عائد على المذكور من الوجودِ والقدم والجامع هو الوجوب له تعالى، والمراد به في حقه تعالى: عدمُ الآخرية للوجودِ، وإن شئتَ قلت : عدم اختتام الوجود. ودليلُ البقاء له تعالى: أنه لو جاز عليه العدم لاستحال عليه القدمُ، لما تقدَّمَ في كلام المصنف من قوله : وَكُلُّ مَا جَازَ عَلَيْهِ العَدَمُ عَلَيْهِ قَطْعاً يَسْتَجِيلُ القِدَمُ كيف وقد سبق قريباً وجوبُ القدم له تعالى؟! وكلُّ ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه، وقد اتفقت العقلاء على هذه القضية كما في العكاري على الكبرى.
His saying: 'like baqa'' – the tanwin indicates diversification and magnification; meaning a type of the types of baqa' that is great similar to the mentioned [attributes] of existence and pre-eternity in [their] necessity for Him, may He be exalted. So the demonstrative pronoun refers back to the mentioned existence and pre-eternity, and the common factor is the necessity for Him, may He be exalted. And what is meant by it with respect to Him, may He be exalted: the absence of posteriority for existence, and if you wish you may say: the non-termination of existence. And the proof of baqa' for Him, may He be exalted: that if non-existence were permissible upon Him, then pre-eternity would be impossible for Him, because of what preceded in the statement of the author: 'And everything upon which non-existence is permissible, certainly pre-eternity is impossible for it.' How, and the necessity of pre-eternity for Him, may He be exalted, has already been mentioned recently? And everything whose pre-eternity is established, its non-existence is impossible. And the rational beings have agreed upon this proposition, as in al-'Akari on the major [premise].
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of baqa' (eternity) and provides a rational proof. The author begins by explaining the phrase 'كذا بقاء' from the matn. He notes that the tanwin indicates diversity and greatness, meaning a type of eternity similar to the previously mentioned attributes of existence (wujud) and pre-eternity (qidam) in being necessarily possessed by God. The demonstrative pronoun 'ذلك' refers to those attributes, and the common factor is their necessity (wujub) for God. He then defines baqa' in God's right as 'the absence of posteriority for existence' or 'non-termination of existence.' This is followed by a deductive proof: if annihilation were possible for God, then pre-eternity would be impossible, because the author of the matn already stated that anything on which annihilation is possible necessarily has pre-eternity impossible for it. Since pre-eternity has already been proven necessary for God, it follows that annihilation is impossible. This principle is universally agreed upon by rational thinkers, as al-‘Akari notes in his commentary on the major premise.

Key Terms
بقاء
قدم
وجود
عدم
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqa' simply means existing forever without end.
Correction: Al-Bajuri specifies that baqa' in God's right means the absence of posteriority for existence, i.e., the negation of any end. It is not merely a positive continuation but a negative attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of baqa' (eternity) as given by the author with respect to God?
A) Continuous existence without beginning or end
B) The absence of posteriority for existence
C) The impossibility of non-existence
D) The necessity of existence

Key Takeaway
Baqa' for God means that His existence has no end, and this is proven by the impossibility of non-existence given the necessity of His pre-eternity.

Why Allah's Permanence is Not Mixed with Non-Existence
Al-Bajuri explains the phrase 'not mixed with non-existence' and cites Labid's verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological meaning of the phrase 'not mixed with non-existence' and its connection to Labid's verse.

Core Idea
Allah's eternity cannot be mixed with non-existence; everything else is transient.

Al-Bajuri unpacks a dense phrase from the Matn: 'La yushabu bil-'adam.' He explains that this does not mean God's existence is literally mixed with non-existence, but that non-existence never attaches to Him. He then relates this to the famous line of Labid, which distinguishes the falsehood of all creation from Allah's enduring reality. Let's see how he argues this.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice how the author immediately clarifies the phrase.
2
Identify the two interpretations of 'yushabu'
The author first gives a literal meaning (mixed) and then refines it to 'possibility of non-existence' by positing an implied noun.
The key is that mixing implies coexistence, which is impossible for permanence and non-existence.
3
Understand the logical argument
Permanence (baqa') cannot coexist with non-existence; therefore, Allah's attribute of permanence negates any mixture with non-existence.
This is the core rational proof for Allah's eternal endurance.
4
Relate to Labid's verse
Labid's line declares that everything besides God is 'bāṭil' (false/transient), which the author equates to the possibility of non-existence.
Thus, only Allah is free from any possibility of non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لَا يُشَابُ بِالعَدَم أي : لا يخلط بالعدم، والمراد من ذلك : أنه لا يلحقه عدم ؛ لأن حقيقة المخالطة تقتضي الاجتماع، والبقاء لا يجتمع مع العدم، إلَّا أن يقدر مضاف؛ أي: لا يُشابُ بجواز العدم، وهو معنى البطلان في قول لبيد : أَلَا كُلُّ شَيْءٍ مَا خَلَا اللَّهَ بَاطِلُ وَكُلُّ نَعِيمِ لَا مَحَالَةَ زَائِلُ
His saying: 'is not mixed with non-existence' means: is not mixed with non-existence. And what is meant by that is that non-existence does not attach to Him, because the reality of mixing requires conjunction, and permanence does not come together with non-existence, unless an understood noun is posited: i.e., 'is not mixed with the possibility of non-existence.' And that is the meaning of falsehood in the saying of Labid: 'Indeed, everything besides God is false, and every blessing will inevitably vanish.'
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage explains the phrase 'لا يُشَابُ بِالعَدَم' from the matn. The author says it literally means 'is not mixed with non-existence.' The intended meaning is that non-existence never attaches to God, because mixing entails conjunction (ijtima'), and permanence (baqa') cannot coexist with non-existence. Alternatively, one may understand an implicit noun: 'is not mixed with the possibility of non-existence.' This interpretation equates to the meaning of 'falsehood' (butlan) in the famous line of the pre-Islamic poet Labid: 'Indeed, everything besides God is false, and every blessing will inevitably vanish.' The verse contrasts transient things with God's eternal permanence.

Key Terms
يشاب
عدم
باطل
زائل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'not mixed with non-existence' might imply that Allah's existence is partially mixed with non-existence in some way.
Correction: The author clarifies that the phrase negates any form of non-existence attaching to Allah, not that His existence contains non-existence as a component.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is the meaning of 'bāṭil' (false) in Labid's verse?
A) Non-existent
B) Passing away
C) That which is mixed with non-existence
D) The possibility of non-existence

Key Takeaway
Allah's eternal permanence is absolutely free from even the possibility of non-existence, unlike all created things which are transient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Labid said, 'Every blessing will inevitably vanish.' Think of one blessing in your life right now. If it were gone tomorrow, would you still trust Allah completely? What concrete act of gratitude can you perform today to acknowledge that this blessing is from Him alone?
?

What is one attachment you hold that, when it ends, might shake your reliance on Allah? Take one step this week to loosen that grip.
?

Inner Command and Prohibition in Kalam
Defines inner command and classifies command vs prohibition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of inner command (amr nafsi) in Islamic theology, its distinction from verbal command, and how it relates to prohibition in refuting Mu'tazilite claims.

Core Idea
Inner command is a psychic demand for action, not just words.

In this passage, the author addresses a key theological dispute with the Mu'tazila over whether God's will for an act is identical to His command. By introducing the notion of inner command (amr nafsi) as distinct from verbal command, he sets out a precise classification of commands and prohibitions. This classification is essential for understanding how divine address works in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the context of refuting the Mu'tazila.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where the author distinguishes between inner command (amr nafsi) and verbal command (amr lafzi). Understand why this distinction matters for the debate.
The Mu'tazila claim that will is identical to command.
3
Understand the definition of inner command
Inner command is defined as a demand (iqtida') for an act. Note the two types of acts that can be demanded.
Acts can be positive or abstentions, but how they are expressed matters.
4
Contrast command and prohibition
Command has two forms, prohibition has one. See the difference in how abstention is expressed (positively vs negatively).
Prohibition is not a command; it is a separate category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغرض المصنف بذلك الردُّ على من زعم من المعتزلة أن إرادته تعالى لفعل غيره أمره به والمراد الأمرُ النفسي لا اللفظي؛ لأنَّ مغايرتها للأمر اللفظي في غاية الظهور فليس فيه خلافُ، وإنما الخلاف في الأمر النفسي وهو اقتضاء - أي : طلب ـ الفعل الذي ليس بكفّ؛ أي: ترك أو الفعل الذي هو كفّ إذا كان مدلولاً عليه بنحو : كـ ( اترك)، بخلاف الكفّ المدلول عليه بغير نحو : كُفَّ كـ (لا تفعل)، فليس بأمرٍ بل نهي، فتحصل أن الأمر تحته صورتان : الأولى : طلب الفعل غير الكفّ كالصلاة. والثانية : طلب الفعل الذي هو كفُّ المدلول عليه بنحو : كُفَّ. وأما النهي فتحته صورةً واحدةً، وهي طلب الكفّ المدلول عليه بغير نحو : كُفَّ كـ لا تفعل.
And the author's purpose with that is to refute those who claimed from the Mu'tazila that His will, Exalted is He, for another's act is His command to it, and what is meant is the inner command (al-amr al-nafsi), not the verbal command; because its distinctness from the verbal command is extremely apparent, so there is no disagreement about that. Rather, the disagreement is about the inner command, which is a demand – i.e., a request – of an act that is not an abstention (kaf); i.e., omission, or of an act that is an abstention if it is indicated by something like 'do' [utruk], as opposed to abstention indicated by other than something like 'refrain' [kuff] like 'do not do' [la taf'al]; then it is not a command but a prohibition. So it follows that command has two forms: the first: request of an act not being an abstention, like prayer; the second: request of an act which is an abstention indicated by something like 'refrain'. As for prohibition, it has one form: request of abstention indicated by other than something like 'refrain' like 'do not do'.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of command (amr) and prohibition (nahy) in the context of refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that divine will for another's act is identical to command. The author distinguishes between inner command (amr nafsi) – a psychic demand – and verbal command. The former is the subject of dispute. Inner command is defined as a demand for an act that is either not an abstention (like prayer) or an abstention if expressed by a directive such as 'do' (utruk/if'al). If abstention is expressed as 'do not do' (la taf'al), that is a prohibition, not a command. Thus command has two subtypes: (a) demand for a positive act, (b) demand for an abstention expressed positively (e.g., 'refrain'). Prohibition has one subtype: demand for abstention expressed negatively.

Key Terms
الأمر النفسي (al-amr al-nafsi)
الأمر اللفظي (al-amr al-lafzi)
اقتضاء (iqtida')
كفّ (kaf)
نهي (nahy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Command and prohibition are purely linguistic categories with no inner psychic reality.
Correction: The passage argues that the inner command is a psychic demand (iqtida') distinct from verbal expression; the verbal form is secondary.

Checkpoint
How many forms does command have according to this passage?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Inner command is a psychic demand for an act, with two subtypes, while prohibition is a demand for abstention expressed negatively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a command from Allah—like 'pray' or 'pay zakah'—do you treat it as an external verbal instruction that you can delay or rationalise away, or as an inner demand that compels your entire being to respond? What would change if you truly felt the inner command as a psychic demand, not just words?
?
The Mu'tazila confused their own will with Allah's command, assuming that what they desired was what Allah commanded. In your own life, where might you be mistaking your desires for divine commands, ignoring the actual demands of the Qur'an and Sunnah? What would it take to realign your heart with Allah's true commands, even when they contradict your inclinations?

Critique of an Alternative Definition of Divine Knowledge
The commentator attacks an alternative definition of divine knowledge with three logical objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three logical flaws (prior ignorance, circularity, and tautology) in a proposed definition of divine knowledge, and why the commentator's definition is problematic.

Core Idea
A definition that implies imperfection in God cannot stand.

This passage presents a critical analysis of an alternative definition of divine knowledge. The author, after presenting the definition, raises three decisive objections: it implies prior ignorance, leads to circular reasoning, and amounts to a tautology. Understanding these critiques sharpens one's ability to evaluate theological definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall critique.
2
Identify the first objection
Notice how the word 'disclose' (تنكشف) implies previous concealment and thus prior ignorance in God.
This objection targets the wording of the definition.
3
Identify the second objection
Notice the circularity (دور) between knowledge and the known; each depends on the other in the definition.
4
Identify the third objection
Notice the fallacy of obtaining what is already obtained (تحصيل الحاصل); the definition assumes disclosure before disclosure.
5
Reflect on the author's definition
Consider why the author's own definition is superior—it avoids these pitfalls.
The author's definition likely does not include problematic terms like 'disclose' or 'known things'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتعريف الذي ذكرناه أولى من التعريف الذي ذكره الشارح وغيره، وهو قوله: (صفةٌ أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى تنكشف بها المعلومات عند تعلقها بها)؛ لأن هذا التعريف معترض من وجوه : منها أن قوله : (تنكشف يقتضي سبق الجهل ; لأن الانكشاف ظهور الشيء بعد الخفاء، ومنها أن المعلومات جمع معلوم ; وهو مشتق من العلم، والمشتق متوقف على المشتق منه، كما أن العلم متوقف على معرفة المعلوم; لأنه أخذ في تعريفه، فكل منهما متوقف على الآخر، فجاء الدور. ومنها أن قوله : (المعلومات) يقتضي أنها منكشفة قبل الانكشاف، فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل .
And the definition we have mentioned is more appropriate than the definition mentioned by the commentator (al-sharih) and others, which is his saying: (an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, exalted, by which the known things are disclosed when they are attached to it). Because this definition is open to objection from several aspects: among them is that his saying: 'disclose' implies prior ignorance, because disclosure is the appearance of a thing after concealment. Among them is that 'the known things' (al-ma'lumat) is the plural of 'known' (ma'lum), which is derived from 'knowledge' (al-'ilm), and the derivative depends on the root, just as knowledge depends on knowing the known, because it is taken in its definition, so each depends on the other, resulting in circularity (al-dawr). Among them is that his saying 'the known things' implies that they are disclosed before disclosure, so the acquisition of what is already obtained (tahsil al-hasil) follows.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage critiques an alternative definition of divine knowledge proposed by the commentator (الشارح) and others. The definition is: 'an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence by which known things are disclosed when attached to it.' The author raises three objections. First, the word 'disclose' (تنكشف) implies that the known things were hidden before, which would imply prior ignorance in God — unacceptable. Second, the term 'known things' (المعلومات) is derived from 'knowledge' (العلم), and the derivative depends on its root. If we define knowledge in terms of the known, we must already know the known, leading to circularity (دور) because knowledge and the known would each depend on the other. Third, calling them 'known things' before disclosure implies they are already known/disclosed, so the definition merely says that knowledge discloses what is already disclosed, which is the fallacy of 'تحصيل الحاصل' (obtaining what is already obtained). Therefore, the definition we gave earlier (the simple one without these problematic terms) is superior.

Key Terms
تعريف العلم
تنكشف
دور
تحصيل الحاصل
سبق الجهل
المعلومات
مشتق
مشتق منه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any definition of divine knowledge is acceptable as long as it seems reasonable.
Correction: Definitions must avoid implying imperfection in God (like ignorance) and logical fallacies such as circularity and tautology.

Checkpoint

A) It implies prior ignorance
B) It is circular
C) It is tautological
D) It is too long

Key Takeaway
A sound definition of divine knowledge must not imply imperfection, circularity, or redundancy.

Three Answers to Objections on the Definition of Knowledge
Author gives three rejoinders to objections about the definition of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars defend the definition of knowledge by carefully distinguishing different meanings of terms like 'unveiling', 'derived', and 'known things'.

Core Idea
Objections often arise from conflating different senses of a word; clear distinctions dissolve them.

In this passage, the commentator addresses three criticisms of a previously given definition of divine knowledge. Each objection seems to point out a logical flaw, but the author shows that the flaw disappears once we carefully separate the various meanings of key terms. This lesson will walk you through each answer and clarify the underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get an overview of the three answers.
Notice that the author responds to each objection in order.
2
First answer: Redefining 'unveiling'
The first objection claims 'unveiling' implies prior concealment. The answer redefines it as appearance without prior hiddenness.
This is a semantic clarification.
3
Second answer: Separating perspectives
The second objection involves a seeming circularity in derivation. The answer distinguishes two perspectives: knowledge as source (infinitive) and known as attribute, and notes the aspect is separable.
Dependence is from different angles, so no circularity.
4
Third answer: Defining 'known things' as entities
The third objection raises circularity between knowledge and its objects. The answer redefines 'known things' as matters themselves, not as things actually known, breaking the cycle.
Also criticizes a phrase implying knowledge might not always relate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب عن الأول: بأن المراد بالانكشاف هنا ظهور الشيء من غير سبق خفاء. وعن الثاني بأن المشتق منه هو العلم الذي هو المصدر، والمعرف بمعنى الصفة، وبأن الجهة منفكَّةٌ ؛ لأن توقف العلم على المعلوم من حيث المعرفة، وتوقف المعلوم على العلم من حيث الاشتقاق . وعن الثالث بأن المراد بالمعلومات الأمور من غير نظر لوقوع العلم عليها، وبه يندفع الدورُ أيضاً، وبأن المراد بالمعلومات ما من شأنها أن تُعلم، وكان الأولى حذف قوله : ( عند تعلقها بها؛ لأنه يقتضي أن العلم تارة يتعلق بالمعلومات، وتارة لا يتعلق بها، وليس كذلك ؛ لأن علم الله متعلق بالمعلومات أزلاً وأبداً .
And the first was answered: that what is meant by 'unveiling' here is the appearance of something without prior concealment. And the second was answered: that the derived-from is knowledge which is the infinitive, and the 'known' is in the sense of the attribute, and that the aspect is separable; because knowledge's dependence on the known is from the perspective of intellection, and the known's dependence on knowledge is from the perspective of derivation. And the third was answered: that what is meant by 'known things' are matters without consideration of knowledge's occurrence upon them, and by this the circularity is also repelled, and that what is meant by 'known things' is what is apt to be known, and it would have been better to delete his saying: 'when it relates to them'; because it implies that knowledge sometimes relates to known things and sometimes does not, and it is not so; because Allah's knowledge relates to known things eternally and everlastingly.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a set of three answers to objections raised against a definition of knowledge mentioned earlier. The first objection concerned the term 'unveiling' (انكشاف) implying prior concealment. The author replies that here 'unveiling' means simply appearing without previous hiddenness. The second objection involved the derivative relationship between knowledge (العلم) and the known (المعلوم). The author clarifies: knowledge in the sense of the infinitive is that from which 'known' is derived, and 'known' is an attribute; the dependence is from two different perspectives—knowledge depends on the known for its intelligible content, while the known depends on knowledge for its derivation. The third objection raised a circularity (دور) concerning knowledge and its objects. The author responds that 'known things' refer to entities themselves prior to the act of knowing, so there is no circularity. He also criticizes a phrase in the definition that suggests knowledge may not always relate to its objects, since Allah's knowledge is eternal and perpetually related to all known things.

Key Terms
انكشاف
المشتق
المعرف
الجهة منفكة
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answers to the objections are arbitrary and do not really resolve the problems.
Correction: Each answer carefully distinguishes the specific sense of a term (unveiling, derivation, known things) to show that the objection relies on an equivocation.

Checkpoint
How does the author resolve the third objection about circularity (دور)?
A) By redefining 'known things' as entities prior to knowledge
B) By deleting the phrase 'when it relates to them'
C) By stating that knowledge is eternal
D) By distinguishing two types of knowledge

Key Takeaway
The three objections are resolved by carefully distinguishing different senses of key terms, showing that apparent contradictions arise from equivocation.

The Three Opinions on Divine Perception
The commentator presents three opinions on God's perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly opinions regarding whether God possesses the attribute of perception (idrak), and the grammatical form used to present this disagreement.

Core Idea
Divine perception is debated: affirmation, denial, or suspension.

In this verse from Jawharat al-Tawhid, Ibn al-Zayn al-Makki raises a theological question: Does God have perception? The commentator explains that there are three positions among scholars. He also clarifies the grammatical construction of the verse. This lesson will help you grasp the range of opinions on this divine attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the question form and the mention of 'waqf' (suspension).
Notice the use of 'فهل' and the commentator's discussion of waw vs. fa.
2
Identify the three opinions
The commentator explicitly states that there are three opinions. Find them in the text: affirmation (thubuut), denial (intifaa'), and suspension (waqf).
These correspond to the phrase 'قيل: بثبوتها، وقيل: بانتفائها، وقيل: بالوقف'.
3
Reflect on the grammatical nuance
The commentator prefers the waw of resumption over the fa. Consider why this independence matters for the flow of discussion.
Think about how the question of perception is a new topic not directly derived from previous issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣٠ - فَهَلْ لَهُ إِدْرَاكَ اَوْ لا؟ خُلْفٌ وَعِنْدَ قَوْمٍ صَحَ فِيهِ الوَقْفُ قوله : (فَهَلْ لَهُ ... إلخ) التعبير بواو الاستئناف أوضح من التعبير بالفاء؛ لأن هذا لا يتفرع على ما قبله ويمكن جعل الفاء للاستئناف، ويصح أن تجعل فاء الفصيحة فتكون في جواب شرط مقدر والتقدير : إذا أردت تحقيق مسألة الإدراك فأقول لك : (هل له ... إلخ)، وحاصل ما ذكره الناظم أنه قيل : بثبوتها، وقيل: بانتفائها، وقيل: بالوقف، فهي أقوال ثلاثة .
And he (the poet) said: 'Does He have perception or not? There is disagreement, and according to some people suspension of judgment is valid.' The commentator said: His statement (فَهَلْ لَهُ ... etc.) The expression with the waw of resumption is clearer than with the fa; because this does not branch from what preceded, and it is possible to make the fa the fa of explanation, so it is in response to an implicit condition, the estimation being: if you want to investigate the issue of perception, I say to you: 'Does He have... etc.' The summary of what the versifier mentioned is that it was said: affirming it, and said: denying it, and said: suspending it, so they are three opinions.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on verse 30 of Jawharat al-Tawhid, which presents the disagreement on whether God possesses the attribute of perception (idrak). The commentator first discusses the grammatical form of the question, noting that the waw (and) is more appropriate than the fa (then) because this question does not follow directly from the preceding discussion. He then summarizes the three positions mentioned by the author: some affirm that God has perception, some deny it, and some withhold judgment. This sets up the detailed arguments that follow.

Key Terms

خلف (ikhtilaf)
وقف (waqf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three opinions are about whether God can perceive in a general sense.
Correction: The debate specifically concerns the status of perception as an eternal attribute of God, not merely the act of perceiving.

Key Takeaway
The passage presents three scholarly positions on divine perception: affirmation, denial, and suspension, along with a grammatical note on the verse's wording.

The Four Categories of Things and the Theory of States
Classifies things into four categories based on the theory of ahwal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourfold classification of things according to the theory of ahwal and the position of the verifying scholars on the existence of states.

Core Idea
Things are either existents, non-existents, states, or relational matters—but the verifying scholars deny the reality of states.

This lesson presents a classification of all things into four categories based on the theory of states (ahwal), which some theologians affirm. However, the author notes that the chosen position among verifying scholars is that states do not exist. Let's explore the four categories as outlined in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the four categories
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Identify the four numbered categories and their brief definitions.
2
Understand the position on states
Notice that the author begins by stating the chosen opinion: there is no state. Then he presents the classification for those who affirm states. This sets up a dialectic.
3
Consider the intermediate nature of states
States are described as having subsistence but not reaching the level of existents (seen) nor the level of non-existents (pure nothing). Reflect on this middle ground.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمختار عند المحققين أنه لا حال، وأن الحال محال ; فعلى القول بثبوت الأحوال تكون الأمور أربعة أقسام : 1 موجودات : وهي التي وجدت في الخارج بحيث تُرى. 2 - ومعدومات : وهي التي ليس لها ثبوت أصلاً. 3 - وأحوال : وهي التي لها ثبوت لكن لم تصل إلى درجة الموجود حتى تُرى، ولم تنحط إلى درجة المعدوم حتى تكون عدماً محضاً . 4 - وأمور اعتبارية : وهي قسمان: أمور اعتبارية انتزاعية كـ (قيام زيد)، فهو
And the chosen position according to the verifying scholars is that there is no state (hal) and that the state is impossible. So according to the opinion affirming states (ahwal), things are four categories: 1. Existents: which are those found in external reality such that they are seen. 2. Non-existents: which have no subsistence at all. 3. States: which have subsistence but did not reach the degree of the existent such that they are seen, nor did they descend to the degree of the non-existent such that they are pure nothingness. 4. Relational matters (umur i'tibariyya): and they are two types: abstractive relational matters like (the standing of Zayd), it is...
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage introduces the classification of things into four categories according to those who affirm the theory of ahwal (states). The author states that the preferred view among verifying scholars is that there is no state and that the state is impossible. Nevertheless, for those who hold the view that states exist, things are divided into: (1) existents, which are found externally and are seen; (2) non-existents, which have no subsistence at all; (3) states, which have subsistence but are not as existents, not seen, yet not pure nothingness – they are intermediate; and (4) relational matters (umur i'tibariyya), which are further divided into abstractive relational matters, exemplified by 'the standing of Zayd.' The passage is cut off, but it introduces the four categories and indicates that further explanation follows.
