The Fourfold Classification of Things According to the View Affirming States
Bajuri enumerates the four categories of things on the view affirming states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of things as defined by those who affirm the existence of states (aḥwāl), and the two subtypes of considerative matters.

Core Idea
Things are not simply existent or non-existent; states and considerative matters bridge the gap.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary presents a precise classification of all possible things according to the theological school that affirms the reality of states (aḥwāl). Beyond the basic binary of existent and non-existent, the author introduces two intermediate categories: states and considerative matters. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for grasping later arguments about divine attributes and their relationship to Allah's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the English translation. Let the classification structure emerge naturally.
Notice the numbering and the colon after each category.
2
Identify the four main categories
Find the four categories: existents (mawjūdāt), non-existents (maʿdūmāt), states (aḥwāl), and considerative matters (umūr iʿtibāriyya). Note their definitions.
The first two are opposites, the third is intermediate.
3
Break down considerative matters
Notice that the fourth category is split into two subtypes: derived (intizāʿiyya) and invented (ikhtirāʿiyya). Study the examples provided.
The derived type does not depend on a considerer, unlike the invented type.
4
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this classification matters in kalam: it allows precise talk about attributes and mental concepts without conflating existence levels.
This foundation is used in debates about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلى القول بثبوت الأحوال تكون الأمور أربعة أقسام: ۱ موجودات: وهي التي وجدت في الخارج بحيث تُرى. ۲ - ومعدومات: وهي التي ليس لها ثبوت أصلاً. ٣ - وأحوال: وهي التي لها ثبوت لكن لم تصل إلى درجة الموجود حتى تُرى، ولم تنحط إلى درجة المعدوم حتى تكون عدماً محضاً . ٤ - وأمور اعتبارية (۳) : وهي قسمان: أمور اعتبارية انتزاعية كـ (قيام زيد)، فهو أمر اعتباري انتزاعي؛ لأنه انتزع من الهيئة الثابتة في الخارج، وأمور اعتبارية اختراعية ك (بحر من زئبق فهو أمرٌ اعتباري اختراعي ؛ لأنه اخترعه الشخص، والقسم الأول لا يتوقف على اعتبار المعتبر وفرض الفارض والقسم الثاني يتوقف على ذلك.
And according to the view affirming states, things are four categories: 1. Existents: which exist externally such that they are seen. 2. Non-existents: which have no reality at all. 3. States: which have reality but have not reached the degree of the existent such that they are seen, and have not fallen to the degree of the non-existent such that they are pure nothingness. 4. Considerative matters (umūr iʿtibāriyya): and they are two types: considerative matters that are derived (intizāʿiyya) like 'the standing of Zayd', for it is a derived considerative matter because it is derived from a form established externally; and considerative matters that are invented (ikhtirāʿiyya) like 'a sea of mercury', for it is an invented considerative matter because a person invented it. The first type does not depend on the consideration of a considerer or the supposition of a supposer, while the second type does depend on that.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of things according to those who affirm the existence of states (aḥwāl). The author lists four categories: (1) Existents (mawjūdāt) – things that exist in the external world and are perceptible; (2) Non-existents (maʿdūmāt) – things that have no reality whatsoever; (3) States (aḥwāl) – things that have some reality but not enough to be perceived as existents nor fully non-existent, serving as an intermediate; (4) Considerative matters (umūr iʿtibāriyya) – which are further divided into two types: derived (intizāʿiyya), such as 'the standing of Zayd', which is abstracted from an external configuration, and invented (ikhtirāʿiyya), such as 'a sea of mercury', which is a mental construct. The derived type does not require an act of consideration or supposition, while the invented type does.

Key Terms
موجودات
معدومات
أحوال
أمور اعتبارية
انتزاعية
اختراعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: States are either fully existent or fully non-existent.
Correction: States have an intermediate reality: they have thubūt but are not perceptible like existents nor pure nothingness like non-existents.

Checkpoint
How many categories are there on the view affirming states, and what are they?
A) Three: existents, non-existents, states
B) Four: existents, non-existents, states, considerative matters
C) Two: existents and non-existents
D) Four: existents, non-existents, states, accidents

Key Takeaway
Things are classified into four categories on the view affirming states: existents, non-existents, states, and considerative matters (derived and invented).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you treat your own invented assumptions (like 'a sea of mercury') as if they were real existents? Are there beliefs you hold that are merely products of your own imagination, not grounded in external reality? Take a moment to identify one such belief and consider how you might test it against the categories of existence discussed here.
?
The category of 'states' describes realities that are real but not perceptible. Think about divine attributes: do you grapple with affirming God's attributes as real without reducing them to created, perceptible things? How does this category challenge you to deepen your understanding of God's nature beyond what your senses can grasp?
?
Are there aspects of your faith that you treat as merely 'considerative' — mental constructs you can modify at will? Reflect on the derived versus invented distinction: is your understanding of tawḥīd derived from revelation and external truth, or have you invented a version that suits your desires? Commit to seeking the derived, true understanding.

The Grammatical and Rhetorical Structure of the Divine Names
Explication of grammatical structure and rhetorical device ihtibak.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical parsing of the line and the rhetorical device of ihtibak that links the names and attributes of Allah.

Core Idea
Ihtibak elegantly ties the greatness of Allah's names to the pre-eternity of His attributes.

This lesson unpacks a classic line from the poem on divine names. The author not only explains the grammar but also reveals a sophisticated rhetorical figure that emphasizes the inseparable connection between the names of Allah and His attributes. We will see how the omitted words are supplied by parallel clauses, creating ihtibak.

Guided Path
1
Understand the grammar
Identify the subject, predicate, and parenthetical clause in the first parsing. Note that 'His names' is the subject, 'the great' is an adjective, and 'pre-eternal' is the predicate.
The second clause 'thus the attributes of His Essence' is parsed as a parenthetical with fronted predicate.
2
Recognize the omission pattern
Notice how the commentator offers an alternative parsing based on omission: the predicate of the first clause is omitted and indicated by the later 'pre-eternal', and the adjective 'great' is omitted from the second clause and supplied by the first.
This creates a symmetry where each clause supplies what the other lacks.
3
Appreciate ihtibak
Understand that this omission pattern is the rhetorical device called ihtibak (interweaving). It emphasizes the parallelism and unity between the names and attributes, and the comparison is for emphasis.
The device is not redundancy; it is a deliberate rhetorical strengthening.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أسماؤه ... إلخ) الأسماء جمع اسم، والمراد به ما دل على الذات بمجردها كـ (الله) و(خداي)؛ أي في اللغة الفارسية أو باعتبار الصفة كالعالم والقادر، ثم إن (أسماؤه) مبتدأ و(العظيمة) وصف كاشف، والخبر (قديمة). وقوله : (كذا صفات ذاته) مبتدأ وخبر، فـ (كذا) خبر مقدم و(صفات ذاته) مبتدأ مؤخر، والجملة معترضة بين المبتدأ وخبره والتشبيه في القدم، وأشار الشارح لإعراب آخر، فجعل خبر قوله : (أسماؤه) محذوفاً دل عليه قوله فيما بعد: (قديمة)، وجعل قوله : (قديمة) الآتي خبراً عن قوله : (صفات ذاته) فيكون المصنف حذف من الأول لدلالة الثاني، كما حذف من الثاني (عظيمة) لدلالة الأول عليه، وحينئذ ففي كلامه من المحسنات البديعية نوع احتباك؛ وهو أن يحذف من كل نظير ما أثبته في الآخر، وعلى هذا فالتشبيه للتأكيد، والأول هو المتبادر من كلام المصنف. قوله: (العظيمة) أي: الجليلة المقدسة؛ أي: المطهرة عن أن يسمى بها الغير أو عن أن تفسر بما لا يليق أو أن تذكر على غير وجه التعظيم كما قاله السعد، وعظم أسمائه تعالى مجمع عليه.
His statement: 'His names... etc.' 'The names' is the plural of ism, and what is meant by it is what indicates the Essence by itself, like 'Allah' and 'Khudāy' (in Persian), or by consideration of the attribute, like al-ʿĀlim (the Knowing) and al-Qādir (the Powerful). Then, indeed, 'His names' is the subject, and 'the great' is a descriptive adjective, and the predicate is 'pre-eternal'. And His statement: 'thus the attributes of His Essence' is subject and predicate, so 'thus' is a fronted predicate and 'attributes of His Essence' is a delayed subject, and the sentence is parenthetical between the subject and its predicate, and the comparison is in pre-eternity. The commentator indicated another parsing, making the predicate of 'His names' omitted, indicated by His statement later 'pre-eternal', and making 'pre-eternal' that comes later a predicate for 'attributes of His Essence', so the author omitted from the first due to the indication of the second, just as he omitted from the second the term 'great' due to the indication of the first upon it. Thus, in his speech there is a type of rhetorical device called ihtibak (interweaving), which is to omit from each counterpart what is confirmed in the other. Upon this, the comparison is for emphasis. And the first is what is apparent from the author's speech. His statement 'the great' means the majestic, the sanctified, i.e., purified from being used for other than Him or being interpreted improperly or being mentioned without veneration, as al-Saʿd said. And the greatness of His names is agreed upon.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and rhetorical analysis of a line from the poem declaring the names of Allah are great and pre-eternal, and so are the attributes of His essence. The author first explains the lexical meaning: 'names' is the plural of ism, referring to names that indicate the essence alone (like Allah and the Persian Khuday) or names that indicate an attribute (like al-Ālim and al-Qādir). Then he parses the sentence: 'His names' is the subject, 'the great' is an adjective, and 'pre-eternal' is the predicate. The subsequent clause 'thus the attributes of His Essence' is parsed with 'thus' as a fronted predicate and 'attributes' as a delayed subject, making the entire clause parenthetical, with the comparison being in pre-eternity. The commentator offers an alternative parsing based on omission: the predicate of the first clause is omitted and indicated by the later 'pre-eternal', and the adjective 'great' is omitted from the second clause and indicated by its presence in the first. This creates the rhetorical figure of ihtibak (interweaving), where what is omitted from one is supplied by the other, emphasizing the parallelism. The description 'great' is further defined as majestic and sanctified, meaning purified from being used for other than Allah or from improper interpretation, and its greatness is a matter of consensus.

Key Terms
احتباك (ihtibak)
مبتدأ (mubtada')
خبر (khabar)
بديع (badi')
تشبيه (tashbih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ihtibak device is seen as redundancy or incomplete syntax.
Correction: Ihtibak is a deliberate rhetorical figure that uses omission to create parallelism and emphasis, not a grammatical error.

Checkpoint
What is the rhetorical device identified in the line?
A) Iqtibas
B) Ihtibak
C) Iltifat
D) Ighraq

Key Takeaway
The names and attributes of Allah are linked by the rhetorical device of ihtibak, which through grammatical omission emphasizes their mutual greatness and pre-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or hear the names of Allah, do you truly feel their greatness and pre-eternity, or do they become mere words? How can you cultivate a deeper sense of awe before them, starting today?
?
The ihtibak shows that Allah's names and attributes are inseparable and equally eternal. Does your worship reflect this unity, or do you sometimes focus on one aspect of Allah while neglecting others?
?
The commentator described the names as 'sanctified' and 'purified from improper interpretation'. Are there any misconceptions or careless uses of Allah's names in your own speech or heart that you need to repent from?

Defining Name and Attribute in Theology
Definitions of theological 'name' and 'attribute'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theologians define 'name' and 'attribute' specifically in the context of Allah's names and attributes.

Core Idea
Names point to essence; attributes point to added meanings.

Before diving into whether divine names are tawqifiyyah, we need a clear definition of what we mean by 'name' and 'attribute' in theology. This lesson establishes the precise theological usage, distinguishing it from everyday linguistic categories like nouns vs. verbs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the nuance: 'name' here means what indicates the essence, not the grammatical noun.
The drop of the first hamza is a poetic license, not a doctrinal point.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the definitions: 'al-ism mā dalla 'alā adh-dhāt' and 'as-sifa mā dalla 'alā ma‘nan zā’id ‘alā adh-dhāt'. Understand the contrast.
3
Understand the exclusion
The passage explicitly rules out two other meanings of 'ism': the grammatical (noun vs verb/particle) and the onomastic (kunya vs laqab). Why is this important?
This prevents confusion with non-theological uses of the term.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Why define name as indicating essence? This sets the stage for discussing whether names are knowable by reason or only by revelation.
The definition impacts the whole debate on tawqifiyyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أَنَّ اسْمَاهُ) بدرج) همزة أسماءه الأولى مع القصر للوزن، والمراد بالأسماء: ما قابل الصفات، بدليل قوله : (كذا الصفات)، الاسم ما دلّ على الذات، والصفة ما دلّ على معنى زائد على الذات وليس المراد بالاسم ما قابل الفعل والحرف ولا ما قابل الكنية واللقب.
His saying: (that His names) [with dropping] the first hamza of 'asma'ihi (His names) due to poetic meter (shortening for weight), and what is meant by 'names' is what is opposite to attributes, evidenced by his saying: (likewise the attributes). The name is what indicates the essence, and the attribute is what indicates a meaning additional to the essence. And what is meant by 'name' is not what is opposite to verb and particle, nor what is opposite to patronymic and nickname.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'ism' (name) and 'sifa' (attribute) as they are used in the context of the previous discussion on whether they are tawqifiyyah. The commentator notes that in the line of poetry being explained, the word 'asmā'uhu' (His names) has had its first hamza dropped for the sake of meter. He then clarifies that what is meant by 'names' here are those that correspond to attributes, not the linguistic categories of noun, verb, or particle. The key definitions: a 'name' is something that indicates the essence itself (e.g., the proper name 'Allah'), while an 'attribute' is something that indicates a meaning that subsists in the essence but is additional to it (e.g., 'knowledge', 'power'). Finally, the commentator emphasizes that the term 'name' here does not refer to the grammatical category (opposed to verb and particle), nor does it refer to teknonymy (kunya) or nicknames (laqab). Thus, the discussion is specifically about divine names and attributes.

Key Terms
الاسم (ism)
الصفة (sifa)
الذات (dhat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'ism' in this context means any noun or name, including common nouns or nicknames.
Correction: The passage clarifies that here 'ism' is specifically what indicates the essence, and not what is opposite to verb/particle or to kunya/laqab.

Checkpoint

A) A grammatical noun
B) The essence (adhdhat) of Allah
C) A meaning additional to the essence
D) A teknonym or nickname

Key Takeaway
In theology, 'name' means that which indicates the essence, and 'attribute' means that which indicates an additional meaning, distinct from common linguistic categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah', do you think of His essence or do you primarily associate it with His attributes like mercy and power? How does the lesson's distinction between name (essence) and attribute (additional meaning) challenge you to deepen your awareness of Allah's very being, beyond just His qualities? Take a moment to consciously remember Allah as the Self-Subsisting Essence.
?
The lesson excludes linguistic and onomastic definitions of 'name' — it focuses only on the theological. Are you guilty of treating Allah's names as mere labels or titles, without reflecting on the attribute they denote? Repent and commit to pondering one divine name each day, asking: 'Does this name indicate the essence itself or an attribute? How does that affect my faith?'
?
If you truly believed that Allah's attributes are 'additional meanings' to His essence — not separate entities but real and eternal — how would that transform your reliance on His mercy? Would you feel more assured that His mercy is a genuine quality that He acts upon? The lesson defines attribute as indicating an added meaning. Does that comfort you or confuse you? Bring your heart to clarity.

The Meaning of 'Opposite' in Divine Impossibilities
Commentator defines 'ضد' and interprets 'في حقه'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific meaning of 'ضد' in the context of divine impossibilities and the phrase 'في حقه' as used by the commentator.

Core Idea
In kalam, 'opposite' of Allah's attributes includes both existing and non-existing contradictions.

This passage clarifies a crucial distinction in Islamic theology: when we say that something is impossible for Allah, we often describe it as the 'opposite' of His attributes. The commentator explains exactly what 'opposite' means here—and why it isn't the same as the philosophical definition. You'll also learn two ways to understand the phrase 'في حقه'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the full literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins by situating this section within the threefold division (necessary, impossible, possible).
2
Focus on the definition of 'ضد'
The commentator defines 'ضد' as 'absolute contradiction, whether existent or non-existent'. Compare this with the technical definition (two existent things). Ask yourself: why does the commentator reject the technical definition here?
The technical definition would not cover non-existence, which is part of what is impossible for Allah.
3
Understand the two interpretations of 'في حقه'
The commentator gives two possible meanings: (1) 'upon His essence' and (2) 'in the judgment due to Him'. Notice which one he prefers and why.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَيَسْتَحِيلُ ... إلخ) هذا شروعٌ في ثالث الأقسام المتقدمة في قوله : فكلُّ مَنْ كُلِّفَ شَرْعاً وَجَبًا عَلَيْهِ أَنْ يَعْرِفَ مَا قَدْ وَجَبًا لله والجائز والممتنعا فهذا هو القسم الثالث في الإجمال السابق، وإن كان ثانياً في التفصيل، وإنما أخر الجائز في التفصيل، لطول الكلام عليه، ولا شك في علم استحالة هذا القسم من وجوب القسم الأول له تعالى، وإنما تعرَّض له المصنف على طريق القوم من عدم اكتفائهم بدلالة الالتزام ولا بدلالة التضمن بل مالوا إلى الدلالة المطابقيَّةِ لخطر الجهل في هذا الفنّ . [معنى الضدين] تحفة المريد على جوهرة التوحيد وقوله : ضِدُّ ذِي الصِّفَاتِ) أي: منافي هذه الصفات المتقدمة بأسرها، فالمراد من الضد هنا المعنى اللغوي ; وهو مطلق المنافي وجوديًّا كان أو عدميا، وليس المراد الأمر الوجودي كما هو المعنى الاصطلاحي; لأن الضدين اصطلاحاً هما الأمران الوجوديان اللذان بينهما غاية الخلاف لا يجتمعان، وقد يرتفعان كالسواد والبياض ; لأنَّ هذا المعنى لا يظهر في جميع ما ذكروه هنا . خصوص وقوله : (فِي حَقِّهِ) أي: على ذاته تعالى، فـ (في) بمعنى (على) و(حق) بمعنى الذات، والإضافة للبيان; لأنَّ الحق اسمٌ مِنْ أسمائه تعالى; أي: حق هو هو، ويحتمل أن (في) باقية على بابها، والمراد من الحقِّ الحكم الواجب له، والإضافة حقيقية، والمعنى حال كون استحالة ما ذكر مندرجة في الحكم الواجب له تعالى، وهذا هو الذي اقتصر عليه الشارح.
His saying: ('And it is impossible... etc.') This is a beginning of the third of the preceding divisions in his saying: 'For everyone who is obligated legally, it is necessary upon him to know what is necessary for Allah, and the possible and the impossible.' So this is the third division in the previous summary, although it is second in detail. And he only delayed the possible in detail due to the length of discussion on it. And there is no doubt in the knowledge of the impossibility of this division from the obligation of the first division for Him. Rather, the author addressed it according to the method of the people [of kalam] who are not satisfied with implication (dalalat al-iltizam) nor with inclusion (dalalat al-tadamun), but inclined to correspondence (dalalat al-mutabaqah) due to the danger of ignorance in this art. [Meaning of the two opposites] Tuhfat al-Mureed 'ala Jawharat al-Tawhid. And his saying: ('the opposite of the one possessing the attributes') i.e., the contradictory of all these preceding attributes. So what is meant by 'opposite' here is the linguistic meaning, which is absolute contradiction, whether existent or non-existent. And what is not meant is the existential thing as the technical meaning, because the two opposites technically are the two existential things between which there is utmost opposition and they do not coexist, but they may both be absent like blackness and whiteness, because this meaning does not appear in all that they have mentioned here. Particularly. And his saying: ('in His right') i.e., upon His essence. So 'fi' (in) is in the meaning of 'ala' (upon), and 'haqq' (right) is in the meaning of essence, and the idafa (annexation) is for explanation, because 'al-Haqq' is one of His names. i.e., the true One who is He. And it is possible that 'fi' remains on its original meaning, and what is meant by 'al-haqq' is the obligatory judgment due to Him, and the idafa is genuine, and the meaning is the state of the impossibility of what is mentioned being included in the obligatory judgment due to Him. And this is what the commentator restricted himself to.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the verse line from the poem that introduces the section on impossibilities. It first situates this section as the third of the three divisions (necessary, impossible, possible) mentioned earlier in the poem. The author explains that the impossible is dealt with here, and the possible is delayed. The commentator notes that the author follows the method of the kalam scholars who do not rely solely on implied or inclusive indication but prefer correspondence (mutabaqah) to avoid ignorance in this art. Then the passage provides definitions: 'ضد ذي الصفات' means any contradiction to all the preceding (necessary) attributes. The term 'ضد' here is used in its linguistic sense, i.e., any contradictory whether existent or non-existent, not the technical sense which applies only to two existing things (like black and white). This is important because some of the impossibilities (like non-existence) are not existent opposites. The phrase 'في حقه' is interpreted in two ways: either as 'upon His essence' (where 'haqq' means essence) or as 'in the judgment due to Him' (where 'haqq' means obligatory ruling). The commentator prefers the first interpretation but notes the second is also possible.

Key Terms
الضد
المعنى اللغوي
المعنى الاصطلاحي
في حقه
دلالة التضمن
دلالة المطابقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'ضد' in this context means the philosophical opposite (e.g., black vs white), which requires both to exist.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states that 'ضد' here is used in its linguistic sense as absolute contradiction, covering both existent and non-existent contradictions, because the technical sense would not apply to all impossibilities.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say that 'ضد' in this passage means the linguistic meaning rather than the technical one?
A) Because the technical meaning requires both opposites to be existent, which does not apply to all impossibilities.
B) Because the author prefers linguistic definitions over technical ones.
C) Because the technical meaning is used only for attributes.
D) Because the linguistic meaning is broader and includes all contradictions, even non-existent ones.

Key Takeaway
The word 'ضد' in this passage refers to any contradiction to Allah's attributes, whether existent or not, and 'في حقه' is best understood as 'upon His essence'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever settled for a vague understanding of Allah's attributes because precise definitions felt too technical or uncomfortable? What would it cost you to now demand the same clarity the scholars demanded for every belief you hold about Him?
?
The commentator warns of 'the danger of ignorance in this art'—what areas of your own faith are you leaving dangerously vague, assuming that a rough idea is enough instead of pursuing direct, clear knowledge?
?
If you were to apply the same painstaking precision to your own concept of Allah's perfection as these scholars apply to defining impossibilities, which lazy assumptions or borrowed beliefs would you have to abandon right now?

Understanding 'Opposite' and 'In His Right' in Kalam
Al-Ajhuri explains the linguistic vs. technical meaning of 'opposite' and interprets 'fi haqqihi'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the linguistic meaning of 'opposite' is used instead of the technical one, and the two possible interpretations of the phrase 'fi haqqihi' in theological texts.

Core Idea
Precision in terms like 'opposite' and 'haqq' protects theology from conceptual confusion.

In this passage, the commentator Al-Ajhuri clarifies two key expressions from the Jawharah: 'opposite of the possessor of the attributes' and 'in His right'. These clarifications are essential because technical terms in logic have narrower meanings that would not apply to all the impossible attributes listed. Understanding the intended linguistic sense and the possible readings of 'fi haqqihi' ensures we grasp the precise theological point being made about Allah's essence and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get a sense of the original wording and flow.
Pay attention to the terms 'ضد' and 'في حقه'.
2
Distinguish the two meanings of 'ضد'
Notice that Al-Ajhuri contrasts the linguistic meaning (absolute opposition, including non-existent) with the technical meaning (two positive existents that cannot coexist). Understand why the technical meaning fails here.
The technical meaning would not apply to all the impossible attributes, so the broader linguistic sense is intended.
3
Explore the two interpretations of 'في حقه'
Al-Ajhuri offers two possible readings: (1) 'upon His essence' with 'haqq' meaning essence, or (2) 'in the judgment obligatory for Him' with 'haqq' meaning necessary judgment. Compare them.
The first reading takes 'fi' as 'upon' and 'haqq' as a name of Allah; the second keeps 'fi' in its original sense.
4
Reflect on the purpose of these clarifications
Ask yourself: why does the author spend time on these terminological nuances? How do they affect our understanding of divine attributes and their negations?
This shows the care in kalam to avoid misunderstandings about Allah's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : ضِدُّ ذِي الصِّفَاتِ) أي: منافي هذه الصفات المتقدمة بأسرها، فالمراد من الضد هنا المعنى اللغوي ؛ وهو مطلق المنافي وجوديًّا كان أو عدميا، وليس المراد الأمر الوجودي كما هو المعنى الاصطلاحي؛ لأن الضدين اصطلاحاً هما الأمران الوجوديان اللذان بينهما غاية الخلاف لا يجتمعان، وقد يرتفعان كالسواد والبياض ؛ لأنَّ هذا المعنى لا يظهر في جميع ما ذكروه هنا . وقوله : (فِي حَقِّهِ) أي: على ذاته تعالى، فـ (في) بمعنى (على) و(حق) بمعنى الذات، والإضافة للبيان؛ لأنَّ الحق اسمٌ مِنْ أسمائه تعالى؛ أي: حق هو هو، ويحتمل أن (في) باقية على بابها، والمراد من الحقِّ الحكم الواجب له، والإضافة حقيقية، والمعنى حال كون استحالة ما ذكر مندرجة في الحكم الواجب له تعالى، وهذا هو الذي اقتصر عليه الشارح.
And his statement: 'opposite of the possessor of the attributes' means: that which opposes all these mentioned attributes entirely. So what is intended by 'opposite' here is the linguistic meaning, which is absolute opposition, whether it is existent or non-existent. And what is not intended is the existent thing as is the technical meaning, because 'the two opposites' technically are the two existent things between which there is utmost opposition and they cannot coexist, though they may both be absent, like blackness and whiteness; because this meaning does not appear in everything they mentioned here. And his statement: 'in His right (fi haqqihi)' means: upon His exalted essence, so 'fi' is in the sense of 'upon' and 'haqq' is in the sense of essence, and the annexation is for clarification, because 'al-Haqq' is one of His names, meaning: the truth that is He. And it is possible that 'fi' remains on its original meaning, and what is intended by 'al-haqq' is the judgment obligatory for Him, and the annexation is real, and the meaning is: the state of the impossibility of what was mentioned being included in the judgment obligatory for Him, exalted. And this is what the commentator restricted himself to.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed terminological clarification. First, the author explains that when the poet says 'opposite of the possessor of the attributes', the word 'opposite' (ضد) is taken in its linguistic sense, meaning anything that contradicts or opposes something else, whether that opposite is a positive existent or a non-existent. He contrasts this with the technical meaning used in logic and philosophy, where 'opposites' (الضدان) are two positive existents that cannot coexist but may both be absent, like blackness and whiteness. The technical meaning does not fit all the cases of impossible attributes listed later, so the linguistic sense is intended. Second, the author explains the phrase 'fi haqqihi' (في حقه). He gives two possible interpretations: either it means 'upon His essence' with 'fi' meaning 'upon' and 'haqq' meaning 'essence', making the phrase an expression of the fact that these impossibilities apply to God's very being; or it means 'in the judgment obligatory for Him', with 'haqq' meaning 'obligatory judgment', indicating that these impossibilities are part of the necessary rulings concerning God. The commentator (al-Sharh) preferred the first interpretation.

Correction: The author explicitly states that the intended meaning is the broader linguistic sense, including non-existent opposites, because the technical meaning does not apply to all the attributes mentioned.

In kalam, 'opposite' often carries its broad linguistic sense of absolute opposition, and phrases like 'fi haqqihi' require careful interpretation to preserve theological precision about Allah's essence.

Grammatical Analysis of 'Li'abdihi wa ma 'amila' and Refutation of Mu'tazila
Grammatical analysis of 'li'abdihi wa ma 'amila' with Mu'tazili refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical analysis of the phrase 'li'abdihi wa ma 'amila' and its function in refuting the Mu'tazili claim that humans create their own voluntary actions.

Core Idea
The grammar of 'wa ma 'amila' defends divine creation of all actions.

This passage from the commentary on the poem dives into the precise wording of the line, analyzing the Arabic grammar to affirm that God creates both the servant and his action. The grammatical choices are not merely linguistic—they serve as a direct refutation of the Mu'tazila, who argued that humans are the creators of their own voluntary deeds. Understanding this analysis shows how Islamic theology uses language to defend core beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing on details. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall flow and conclusion.
Notice how the author begins by stating the saved belief, then moves to specific grammar.
2
Understand the two grammatical possibilities
Focus on the section explaining 'wa ma 'amila'. The passage presents two interpretations: 'ma' as masdariyya (making it a verbal noun) or mawsula (relative pronoun). Note why the first is preferred—it has no omission.
This grammatical choice directly impacts the meaning: 'what he did' vs. 'his action'.
3
Connect to the refutation of Mu'tazila
The passage explicitly states that both interpretations support the refutation of the Mu'tazila. Identify how each reading affirms that God creates both the servant and his action, contradicting the Mu'tazili view.
The same analysis applies to the Quranic verse, strengthening the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن اعتقد أنَّ المؤثر هو الله، وجعل بين الأسباب والمسببات تلازماً عاديا ، بحيث يصح تخلفها، فهو المؤمن الناجي، إن شاء الله تعالى، فالفرق في ذلك أربعة، كما يؤخذ من كُتب السنوسي.
قوله : (لِعَبْدِهِ) اللام للتقوية؛ والمراد من العبد كل مخلوق يصدر عنه الفعل عاقلاً كان أو غيره، خلافاً لبعضهم حيث قصَرَه على المكلَّف؛ لأنَّ بعض الأدلة التي ذكروها لا تجري في غير فعله، وإنما ذكر المصنف العبد مع أنه متفق على خلق الله إيَّاه توصلاً لما بعده، واقتداءً بقوله تعالى : ﴿ وَالله خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ [الصافات: ٩٦] . وقوله : (وَمَا عَمِلْ) معطوف على عبده، و(ما) مصدرية، فيؤول الفعل بعدها بمصدر، والتقدير : فخالق لعبده ولعمله، ويحتمل أن تكون موصولة، و(عمل) صلة، والعائد محذوف، وعليه فالتقدير : فخالق لعبده وللذي عمله، والأول أولى؛ لأنَّه لا حذف عليه، والأصل عدم الحذف.
ويجري الاحتمالان المذكوران في قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَاللهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ ) : الصافات: ١٩٦) ، وفي ذلك ردُّ على المعتزلة في قولهم : بأن العبد يخلق أفعال نفسه الاختياريَّة، وأما الأفعال الاضطراريَّة ؛ كحركة المرتعش فهي مخلوقة الله تعالى اتفاقاً .
And whoever believes that the effective agent is God, and posits between causes and effects a customary concomitance such that their failure to occur is valid, then he is the saved believer, if God wills. So the differences in that are four, as taken from the books of al-Sanusi. His statement: 'to His servant' – the lam is for emphasis; and what is meant by 'the servant' is every creature from whom action originates, whether rational or otherwise, contrary to some who restricted it to the morally responsible; because some of the proofs they mentioned do not apply to other than his action. The author only mentioned 'the servant' despite it being agreed upon that God created him, as a lead-in to what follows, and in emulation of His statement: 'And Allah created you and what you do' [al-Saffat:96]. And His statement: 'and what he did' is coordinated with 'His servant', and 'ma' is masdariyya, so the verb after it becomes a verbal noun, so the estimation is: 'Creator of His servant and of his action'. And it is possible that it is mawsula, with 'amila' as the relative clause and the relative pronoun omitted, and accordingly the estimation is: 'Creator of His servant and of what he did'. The first is preferable because it has no omission, and the principle is non-omission. Both possibilities apply to God's statement: 'And Allah created you and what you do' [al-Saffat:96], and in that is a refutation of the Mu'tazila in their statement that the servant creates his own voluntary actions. As for involuntary actions, like the movement of a trembling person, they are created by God by consensus.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from the poem, explicating the phrase 'li'abdihi wa ma 'amila'. It begins by setting the theological context: true faith requires believing that God alone is the ultimate effecter (al-mu'aththir) and that causes and effects are only customarily conjoined, not necessarily. Then it explains the word 'abd (servant) as encompassing all creatures that perform actions, rational or not, contrary to those who restrict it to the morally obligated (mukallaf). The author then analyzes the grammatical construction of 'wa ma 'amila'. Two possibilities are considered for 'ma': it could be a masdariyya (verbal noun particle) making the phrase mean 'and his action', or a mawsula (relative pronoun) meaning 'and what he did'. The first is preferred because it involves no deletion. The same analysis applies to the Quranic verse, and this serves as a refutation of the Mu'tazili claim that humans create their own voluntary actions. Involuntary actions are agreed to be created by God.

Correction: The passage explicitly states that both grammatical possibilities support the refutation, as both affirm divine creation of the action.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'wa ma 'amila' affirms that God creates both the servant and his action, refuting the Mu'tazili claim that humans create their own voluntary actions.

The Broad Meaning of 'Servant' in Islamic Theology
The commentator glosses the word 'al-'abd' in the poetic line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'servant' (al-‘abd) in Islamic theological discourse is expanded to include any creature capable of voluntary action, not just humans.

Core Idea
'Servant' includes every creature that can perform a voluntary action.

This lesson focuses on a brief but important lexical clarification from the commentary on a poetic verse. The commentator explains that the word 'servant' in the poem does not refer only to human beings. Instead, it encompasses all creatures—even trees and pebbles—that can be described as performing a voluntary act. This understanding has implications for how we interpret references to non-human entities in the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the definition of 'abd.
The definition is given in the first sentence.
2
Examine the examples
The author provides three examples of voluntary actions from non-human entities. Identify each one: moaning trunk, walking tree, glorifying pebbles.
These examples illustrate the breadth of the term.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this broad definition matters. It expands the concept of moral agency beyond humans and aligns with Ash'ari theology on kasb (acquisition).
This connects to the theme of divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لِلْعَبْدِ) المراد به كل مخلوق يصدر عنه فعل اختياري، قال المصنف : فيشمل حنين الجذع ، ومشي الشجر ، وتسبيحَ الحصى. انتهى
His saying: 'for the servant' (lil-'abd): what is meant by it is every creature from whom a voluntary action proceeds. The author said: so it includes the moaning of the trunk, the walking of the tree, and the glorification of the pebbles. End.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This is a lexical gloss on the phrase 'لِلْعَبْدِ' (for the servant) appearing in the verse of the poem. The commentator explains that the term 'servant' here is not restricted to human beings; rather, it refers to any creature that can be the source of a voluntary action. The author of the original poem (Ibn al-Jazari or the commentator's reference) is quoted as saying that this includes even non-human entities such as the moaning of a tree trunk (when cut), the movement of a tree, and the glorification of pebbles (referring to the tradition that pebbles glorified God in the Prophet's hand). This broadens the scope of moral agency in the context of kasb.

The Definition of Kasb and Its Two Interpretations
Sanusi defines kasb and gives two interpretations regarding its createdness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what kasb means in the Ash'ari tradition and why its interpretation as a connection (rather than a will) is considered uncreated.

Core Idea
Kasb is either a created relation or a created will — and this determines whether it is created.

The commentator begins by clarifying the technical term 'kasb', which is central to the Ash'ari theory of human action. He presents two possible meanings: the attaching of originated power to the act, or the originated will itself. This distinction has direct consequences for whether kasb is considered a created entity or not.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of kasb
Start by reading the Arabic and translation of the first line: 'kasb is the attaching of the originated power to the brought-about thing'. Note the alternative opinion.
The term 'kasb' is from the Qur'anic verse.
2
Understand the four matters
The text mentions four things: God's will, power and act conjoined, and the connection between them. This sets the stage for interpreting kasb.
3
Compare the two interpretations
Identify that if kasb is the connection, it is not created (it is a conceptual relation). If it is the originated will, it is created. Ask yourself why this matters.
Conceptual things have no independent existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (كَسْبٌ) هو تعلُّق القدرة الحادثة بالمقدور، وقيل: هو الإرادة الحادثة؛ فإنَّ الأمور أربعة : إرادة سابقة، وقدرةً وفعل مقترنان و ارتباط بينهما، فعلى تفسير الكسب بهذا الارتباط ؛ وهو تعلق القدرة بالمقدور ليس مخلوقاً ؛ لأنه من الأمور الاعتبارية، وعلى تفسيره بالإرادة الحادثة يكون مخلوقاً . وقد عرفوا الكسب بتعريفين : الأول : أنَّه ما يقعُ به المقدور من غير صحةِ انفراد القادر به؛ أي: ارتباط وتعلق أو إرادة على ما سبق من القولين يقعُ المقدورُ ، كالحركة متلبساً ومصحوباً به، من غير صحة كون القادر ؛ وهو العبد ينفرد بذلك المقدور، بل ومن غير صحةِ المشاركة إذ لا تأثير منه بوجه ما ، وإنما له مجرد المقارنة والخالقُ الحقُّ منفرد بعموم التأثير . الثاني : أنَّه ما يقع به المقدور في محل قدرته ؛ أي ارتباط وتعلق أو إرادة، على ما مرَّ من القولين يقع المقدور كالحركة متلبساً ومصحوباً به حال كون هذا المقدور في محلّ قدرته ؛ كاليد. وقوله : (كُلّفَا) ألفه للإطلاق، وهو مبني للمفعول، ونائب الفاعل ضمير يعود على العبد، والأصل: كلَّفَه الله ؛ أي: ألزمه ما فيه كُلفة، أو طلب منه ما فيه كُلفة، على الخلاف في تفسير التكليف ؛ ويُفهم من إثبات الكسب الذي هو سبب في التكليف ردُّ مذهب الجبرية.
And His saying: 'kasb' (acquisition) is the attaching of the originated power to the brought-about thing, and it is said: it is the originated will. For the matters are four: a preceding will, power and act conjoined, and a connection between them. So on the interpretation of kasb as this connection—which is the attaching of power to the brought-about thing—it is not created because it is among the conceptual things; and on its interpretation as the originated will, it is created. And they defined kasb with two definitions: The first: that by which the brought-about thing comes about without the validity of the agent being alone with it—that is, a connection and attachment or a will according to the two sayings mentioned, the brought-about thing comes about clothed and accompanied by it, without the validity of the agent—which is the servant—being alone with that brought-about thing, nor even the validity of participation, for there is no effect from it in any way; rather, it merely has concomitance, and the Real Creator is alone in pervasive effect. The second: that by which the brought-about thing comes about in the locus of his power—that is, a connection and attachment or a will, according to the two sayings mentioned, the brought-about thing comes about clothed and accompanied by it while this brought-about thing is in the locus of his power, like the hand. And His saying: 'kullifa' (he was tasked): its alif is for the absolute form, and it is passive voice, and the deputy subject is a pronoun referring to the servant; the original is 'kallafahu Allah' (God tasked him), i.e. He obligated him to that which involves difficulty, or requested from him that which involves difficulty, according to the disagreement in the interpretation of obligation (taklif). And from affirming the kasb which is the cause of obligation, the refutation of the Jabriyya doctrine is understood.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage provides the technical definition of kasb in the Ash'ari school. It begins by stating that 'kasb' is the relation (ta'alluq) of the created power (al-qudra al-haditha) to the act (al-maqdur). An alternative view says it is the created will (al-irada al-haditha). The commentator explains the context: there are four things: God's eternal will, the created power and the act conjoined, and the connection between power and act. If kasb is interpreted as this connection, it is not a created thing because it is a conceptual relation (amr i'tibari). If interpreted as the created will, then it is created. Then two formal definitions are given. The first: kasb is that by which the act occurs, but without the servant being able to produce it alone or even participate effectively. The servant's role is mere concomitance (mujarrad al-muqarana) with the act, while God alone has universal effect. The second definition adds that the act occurs in the locus of the servant's power (e.g., the hand), emphasizing that the act is in the servant's limb. Then a grammatical explanation of the word 'kullifa' (from the verse) is given: it is passive, meaning 'he was tasked by God' with something involving difficulty (kulifa). This obligation is based on the servant's kasb, and affirming kasb as the basis for obligation refutes the Jabriyya doctrine that denies human responsibility.

Key Terms
كسب
قدرة حادثة
إرادة حادثة
ارتباط
تعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think kasb is a separate created entity like the act itself.
Correction: The passage clarifies that kasb may be merely a conceptual relation, not a created object.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on which interpretation of kasb is it considered not created?
A) When kasb is interpreted as the originated will.
B) When kasb is interpreted as the connection between power and the act.
C) When kasb is interpreted as the act itself.
D) When kasb is interpreted as the servant's volition.

Key Takeaway
Kasb is either a conceptual relation (uncreated) or a created will, and this distinction shapes the Ash'ari theory of acquisition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your every action depends on a connection between God's power and your will, where do you find true ownership of your deeds? How does this affect your sense of responsibility before Allah today?
?
When you sin, do you excuse yourself by saying 'it was written' or do you grapple with the kasb within you — your will and its relation to the act? What would it mean to own your sin as your acquisition while knowing it is ultimately from Allah?
?
The scholars split over whether kasb is a conceptual relation or a created will. Which interpretation makes you more conscious of your choices in the next hour? Why?

The Two Formal Definitions of Kasb and the Refutation of Jabriyya
Sanusi gives two definitions of kasb and explains 'kullifa' to refute Jabriyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two formal definitions of kasb and how affirming kasb refutes the Jabriyya doctrine of coercion.

Core Idea
Kasb is the mere concomitance of the servant's power with the act, not effective causation.

After clarifying the basic meaning of kasb, the commentator presents two formal definitions that capture the precise relationship between the servant and his action. These definitions emphasize that the servant's role is merely being concomitant with the act, not causing it. Finally, the passage ties kasb to the concept of obligation (taklif) and shows how this refutes the Jabriyya school.

Guided Path
1
Read the first definition
Read the first definition: kasb is that by which the act occurs without the agent being validly alone. Understand that the servant does not create the act.
This rules out both independece and partnership in creation.
2
Read the second definition
The second definition adds that the act occurs in the locus of the servant's power (e.g., the hand). This locates the act as occurring through the servant's limb.
3
Understand the explanation of 'kullifa'
The word 'kullifa' is parsed grammatically and linked to obligation. It means God tasked the servant with something involving difficulty.
Taklif is based on the reality of kasb.
4
Refutation of Jabriyya
The passage ends by stating that affirming kasb as the basis of obligation refutes the Jabriyya, who deny all human efficacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (كَسْبٌ) هو تعلُّق القدرة الحادثة بالمقدور، وقيل: هو الإرادة الحادثة؛ فإنَّ الأمور أربعة : إرادة سابقة، وقدرةً وفعل مقترنان و ارتباط بينهما، فعلى تفسير الكسب بهذا الارتباط ؛ وهو تعلق القدرة بالمقدور ليس مخلوقاً ؛ لأنه من الأمور الاعتبارية، وعلى تفسيره بالإرادة الحادثة يكون مخلوقاً . وقد عرفوا الكسب بتعريفين : الأول : أنَّه ما يقعُ به المقدور من غير صحةِ انفراد القادر به؛ أي: ارتباط وتعلق أو إرادة على ما سبق من القولين يقعُ المقدورُ ، كالحركة متلبساً ومصحوباً به، من غير صحة كون القادر ؛ وهو العبد ينفرد بذلك المقدور، بل ومن غير صحةِ المشاركة إذ لا تأثير منه بوجه ما ، وإنما له مجرد المقارنة والخالقُ الحقُّ منفرد بعموم التأثير . الثاني : أنَّه ما يقع به المقدور في محل قدرته ؛ أي ارتباط وتعلق أو إرادة، على ما مرَّ من القولين يقع المقدور كالحركة متلبساً ومصحوباً به حال كون هذا المقدور في محلّ قدرته ؛ كاليد. وقوله : (كُلّفَا) ألفه للإطلاق، وهو مبني للمفعول، ونائب الفاعل ضمير يعود على العبد، والأصل: كلَّفَه الله ؛ أي: ألزمه ما فيه كُلفة، أو طلب منه ما فيه كُلفة، على الخلاف في تفسير التكليف ؛ ويُفهم من إثبات الكسب الذي هو سبب في التكليف ردُّ مذهب الجبرية.
And His saying: 'kasb' (acquisition) is the attaching of the originated power to the brought-about thing, and it is said: it is the originated will. For the matters are four: a preceding will, power and act conjoined, and a connection between them. So on the interpretation of kasb as this connection—which is the attaching of power to the brought-about thing—it is not created because it is among the conceptual things; and on its interpretation as the originated will, it is created. And they defined kasb with two definitions: The first: that by which the brought-about thing comes about without the validity of the agent being alone with it—that is, a connection and attachment or a will according to the two sayings mentioned, the brought-about thing comes about clothed and accompanied by it, without the validity of the agent—which is the servant—being alone with that brought-about thing, nor even the validity of participation, for there is no effect from it in any way; rather, it merely has concomitance, and the Real Creator is alone in pervasive effect. The second: that by which the brought-about thing comes about in the locus of his power—that is, a connection and attachment or a will, according to the two sayings mentioned, the brought-about thing comes about clothed and accompanied by it while this brought-about thing is in the locus of his power, like the hand. And His saying: 'kullifa' (he was tasked): its alif is for the absolute form, and it is passive voice, and the deputy subject is a pronoun referring to the servant; the original is 'kallafahu Allah' (God tasked him), i.e. He obligated him to that which involves difficulty, or requested from him that which involves difficulty, according to the disagreement in the interpretation of obligation (taklif). And from affirming the kasb which is the cause of obligation, the refutation of the Jabriyya doctrine is understood.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage provides the technical definition of kasb in the Ash'ari school. It begins by stating that 'kasb' is the relation (ta'alluq) of the created power (al-qudra al-haditha) to the act (al-maqdur). An alternative view says it is the created will (al-irada al-haditha). The commentator explains the context: there are four things: God's eternal will, the created power and the act conjoined, and the connection between power and act. If kasb is interpreted as this connection, it is not a created thing because it is a conceptual relation (amr i'tibari). If interpreted as the created will, then it is created. Then two formal definitions are given. The first: kasb is that by which the act occurs, but without the servant being able to produce it alone or even participate effectively. The servant's role is mere concomitance (mujarrad al-muqarana) with the act, while God alone has universal effect. The second definition adds that the act occurs in the locus of the servant's power (e.g., the hand), emphasizing that the act is in the servant's limb. Then a grammatical explanation of the word 'kullifa' (from the verse) is given: it is passive, meaning 'he was tasked by God' with something involving difficulty (kulifa). This obligation is based on the servant's kasb, and affirming kasb as the basis for obligation refutes the Jabriyya doctrine that denies human responsibility.

Key Terms
كسب
قدرة حادثة
تكليف
ارتباط
تعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jabriyya believe that humans have no role at all in their actions, making obligation pointless.
Correction: Ash'ari kasb affirms a real but non-effective association, providing a basis for moral obligation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what role does the servant have in the first definition of kasb?
A) He creates the act independently.
B) He participates in creation alongside God.
C) He has mere concomitance with the act.
D) He has no role at all.

Key Takeaway
Kasb is a non-effective association of the servant's power with the act, which grounds moral obligation and refutes Jabriyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your actions are only accompanied by your power but not caused by you, where does true accountability for your sins lie? How does this reshape your repentance and resolve to change?
?
The passage says the servant has no effect in any way. Does this make you feel passive or liberated? In what area of your life do you most struggle to accept that your effort is only a context, not a cause?
?
You affirm kasb: you are responsible but not effective. When you fail to obey Allah, do you treat that failure as your own fault or as something forced? Be honest: how does this belief actually affect your daily struggles with sin?

Refuting the Claim that the Slave Creates His Own Actions
Commentary explains the linguistic rule of negation and refutes Mu'tazila and Jabriyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic rule of negation with generality and how the commentator uses it to refute both the Mu'tazila and the Jabriyya on the issue of human action.

Core Idea
In Arabic, 'not every' can mean 'none' when context demands it.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid tackles a subtle linguistic point: the phrase 'the slave does not create every action' could be misread as allowing some actions to be created by the slave. The commentator clarifies that the intended meaning is absolute negation—the slave creates no actions at all—and explains why the author explicitly refutes both the Mu'tazila and Jabriyya.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the gist.
Pay attention to the grammatical structure: 'kulla' as object, 'yaf'alu' meaning to create.
2
Understand the linguistic rule
The commentator explains a rule: when the tool of negation (e.g., 'la') precedes the tool of generality (e.g., 'kull'), it normally indicates negation of generality (not all). Example: 'I did not take all the dirhams' typically means I took some but not all.
This rule is important for interpreting the poem correctly.
3
Identify the context clue that changes the meaning
The phrase 'and he was not effective' serves as a context clue that the intended meaning is not just 'not all' but 'none at all'. The rule is predominant, not universal.
The commentator references Quran 57:23 as a similar example.
4
Recognize the refutation of Mu'tazila and Jabriyya
The author explicitly states the refutation of Mu'tazila (who affirm the slave creates his actions) and Jabriyya (who deny human agency). Explicit refutation is necessary because false sects require clear rejection.
Both sects are refuted in one line, with the understanding already implied in the previous line.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفعل اختياراً ، فـ (كُلَّا) مفعول لـ ( يفعل ) مقدَّمٌ عليه، و(يفعل) بمعنى يخلق، فالمعنى: ليس العبد يخلقُ كلَّ فعل من أفعاله الاختيارية، وظاهر ذلك أنه يخلق بعض أفعاله الاختياريَّةِ؛ لأن القاعدة أنه إذا تقدَّمت أداة السلب على أداة العموم أفادت سلب العموم؛ كما في قولهم : لم آخذ كل الدراهم، مع أن المراد أنه لا يخلق فعلاً أبداً. وقد يُقال : قوله : (ولم يكن مؤثّراً قرينة على المعنى المراد، والقاعدة أغلبية لا كُليَّةٌ، فالمراد هنا عموم السلب، كما في قوله تعالى: (وَاللهُ لَا يُحِبُّ كُلَّ مُخْتَالٍ فَخُورٍ [الحديد: (۲۳)، وغرضُ المصنفِ بذلك التصريح بالرد على المعتزلة في قولهم : إنَّ العبد يخلق أفعال نفسه الاختياريَّةً، وإِنَّما صرّح بالردّ على كُلِّ من الجبرية والمعتزلة في هذا البيت مع فهم الردّ على كلِّ منهما من البيت ،قبله كما تقدَّم التنبيه عليه؛ لأنَّ القوم لا يكتفون في مقام ردّ المذاهب الفاسدة إلا بالتصريح.
The action with choice, so (kulla) is the object of (yaf'alu) placed before it, and (yaf'alu) means to create. So the meaning: the slave does not create every action from his voluntary actions, and its apparent meaning is that he creates some of his voluntary actions, because the rule is that if the tool of negation precedes the tool of generality, it indicates negation of generality; as in their saying: 'I did not take all the dirhams,' while the intended meaning is that he never creates an action at all. And it may be said: His saying: 'And he was not effective' is a context clue for the intended meaning, and the rule is predominant not universal, so the intended here is the negation of all, as in the Almighty's saying: 'And Allah does not love every conceited boastful person' [Al-Hadid: 23]. And the author's purpose by that is explicit refutation of the Mu'tazila in their saying that the slave creates his own voluntary actions. And he only made explicit refutation of both Jabriyya and Mu'tazila in this line, while understanding the refutation of each from the previous line as previously indicated, because the people are not satisfied in the position of refuting corrupt schools except with explicitness.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line of the poem that denies that the slave creates every action. The commentator explains a linguistic point: when the negation word precedes a universal quantifier like 'every', it normally negates the universal, meaning 'not all' rather than 'not any'. However, the intended meaning here is absolute negation (the slave never creates any action), and the context clue 'and he was not effective' clarifies this. The commentator then states that the author explicitly refutes both the Mu'tazila (who say the slave creates his own voluntary actions) and the Jabriyya (who hold an extreme determinism). The reason for explicit refutation is that in refuting false sects, one must be explicit.

Key Terms
فعل اختياري (voluntary action)
أداة السلب (tool of negation)
أداة العموم (tool of generality)
سلب العموم (negation of generality)
المعتزلة (Mu'tazila)
الجبرية (Jabriyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'not every' always means 'not all' (partial negation).
Correction: The commentator shows that with context clues, 'not every' can mean absolute negation (none), as in the Quranic example.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the intended meaning of 'لا يخلق كل فعل' in the poem?
A) He does not create every action.
B) He never creates any action.
C) He creates some actions.
D) He creates all actions.

Key Takeaway
The slave creates none of his voluntary actions, refuting both the Mu'tazila who claim he creates them and the Jabriyya who deny any human efficacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. When you read 'the slave does not create every action,' was your first reaction to assume he creates some? What does that reveal about your own assumptions when interpreting religious texts? Will you now be more careful to consider context before drawing conclusions?
?
2. The commentator stresses the need for explicit refutation of false sects. Are there any beliefs you hold that might be mistaken, but you avoid examining them because they feel comfortable? What would it take to explicitly test them against the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
3. This lesson shows that even a linguistic rule ('predominant, not universal') can be overridden by context. In your own life, where have you clung to a rule or habit without considering the deeper intention behind it? How can you realign your actions with the true spirit of Islamic teachings?

Ignorance as a Cause of Disbelief and Its Harmless Forms
Defines ignorance as a cause of disbelief and distinguishes harmless ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that ignorance is a cause of disbelief but not all ignorance leads to disbelief, and how kufr is defined as denial of necessary religious knowledge.

Core Idea
Not all ignorance is disbelief – only ignorance of necessary religious truths.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid clarifies the relationship between ignorance and disbelief. It defines kufr precisely and explains that some ignorance, such as that of God's essence, is not harmful. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for sound theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the content.
Notice the key terms like الجهل, الكفر, العناد.
2
Identify the causes of disbelief
The passage mentions two causes: ignorance and obstinacy. Find them in the text.
Obstinacy is deliberate rejection despite knowing the truth.
3
Understand the definition of kufr
Kufr is defined as denying what is necessarily known from the Messenger's religion. Note the example of throwing a Qur'an in filth.
This shows that actions can also constitute disbelief.
4
Reflect on harmless ignorance
The passage clarifies that ignorance of God's attributes not indicated by actions is not harmful, quoting Abu Bakr: 'Inability to perceive is perception.'
This distinguishes theology from metaphysics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنَّ الجهل سبب للكفر ، وإن كان له سبب آخر وهو العناد، وقد تقدم تعريف الجهل وانقسامه إلى بسيط ومركب والكفر ضدُّ الإيمان، فهو إنكارُ ما عُلِمَ مجيء الرسول به من الدين بالضرورة، أو ما يستلزم ذلك كإلقاء مصحف في القاذورة، وإنّما أضاف الناظم الجهل إلى الكفر لينبه على أن من الجهل ما لا يضرُّ ، كجهلنا بجلال الله وصفاته التي لم تدل عليها أفعاله، كما يشير إليه قول الصديق الأكبر: (العجز عن الإدراك إدراك ) .
Indeed, ignorance is a cause of disbelief, although there is another cause, which is obstinacy. The definition of ignorance has preceded, and its division into simple and compound. Disbelief is the opposite of faith; it is the denial of what is known to have been brought by the Messenger from religion necessarily, or what entails that, such as throwing a copy of the Qur'an into filth. The poet added ignorance to disbelief only to indicate that among ignorance there is what is not harmful, like our ignorance of the majesty of God and His attributes that His actions have not indicated, as is indicated by the statement of the Greatest Truthful One (al-Siddiq al-Akbar): 'Inability to perceive is perception.'
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage defines ignorance and its relation to disbelief (kufr). It begins by stating that ignorance is a cause of disbelief, though there is another cause: obstinacy (عناد). It refers to an earlier definition of ignorance, which divides it into simple (بسيط) and compound (مركب). Then it defines kufr as the denial of what is necessarily known to have been brought by the Messenger from religion, or anything that implies such denial, like throwing a mushaf into filth. The commentator then explains why the poet (al-nazim) added ignorance to disbelief: to point out that some ignorance is not harmful, such as our ignorance of God's majesty and attributes that are not indicated by His actions. This is supported by the saying of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq: 'Inability to perceive is perception' (al-ʿajz ʿan al-idrak idrak). Thus, the passage clarifies that not all ignorance leads to kufr; only ignorance of what is necessarily known from religion is harmful.

Key Terms
الجهل
الكفر
الجهل البسيط
الجهل المركب
العناد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All ignorance leads to disbelief.
Correction: Only ignorance of necessarily known religious truths leads to disbelief. Ignorance of God's essence is not harmful.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is disbelief (kufr)?
A) Ignorance of God's attributes.
B) Denial of what is necessarily known from religion brought by the Messenger.
C) Simple ignorance.
D) Obstinacy.

Key Takeaway
Ignorance can cause disbelief, but only ignorance of necessary religious truths; ignorance of God's ineffable essence is not blameworthy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt guilty for not being able to fully comprehend God's essence? What does the saying of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq—'Inability to perceive is perception'—teach you about accepting this limitation, and how will you change the way you approach divine mysteries?
?
The passage warns that obstinacy (ʿinad) is a separate cause of disbelief. Is there any teaching of the Prophet ﷺ that you find difficult to accept because of personal pride or stubbornness? What concrete step will you take today to humble yourself before that truth?
?
Disbelief is defined as the denial of necessary religious truths. Pause and ask yourself: do you truly affirm these truths in your heart, or have you become so familiar with them that they no longer stir your conviction? What will you do to renew your certainty today?

Qadr and Qada: Definitions in the Two Schools
Defines Qadr and Qada for both schools and shows attribute classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools define Qadr and Qada and why they classify them as attributes of action or essence.

Core Idea
Qadr is action for Ash'aris, knowledge for Maturidis; Qada is essence for Ash'aris, action for Maturidis.

This passage from Tuhfat al-Mureed lays out the two major positions on the terms qadr and qada in Islamic theology. The definitions differ fundamentally between the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools, which affects how they understand divine decree and predestination. Let's see how each school defines these terms and what that implies for their classification of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic passage and translation, focusing on how each school defines qadr and qada. Notice the key terms like إيجاد and تحديد.
The passage contrasts the two schools in parallel.
2
Identify the attribute type
For each definition, note whether the school considers the term an attribute of action (صفة فعل) or attribute of essence (صفة الذات). This is the main point of the passage.
Attributes of essence are eternal, attributes of action are contingent.
3
Compare the results
The passage states that for Ash'aris, qadr is contingent (حادث) and qada is pre-eternal (قديم), but the opposite for Maturidis. Consider why this follows from the definitions.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to check if you can match each term to its school and attribute type.
This will solidify the key distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أنَّ الأشاعرة والماتريدية اختلفوا في كل من القدر والقضاء : فالقَدَر عند الأشاعرة إيجاد الله الأشياء على قدرٍ مخصوص ووجه معين تعالى، فيرجع عندهم لصفة فعلٍ ؛ لأنَّه عبارة عن الإيجاد، وهو من صفات الأفعال. وعند الماتريدية: تحديدُ اللهِ أزلاً كل مخلوق بحده الذي يوجد عليه من حسن وقُبْحٍ ونَفْعِ وضُرٍّ إلى غير ذلك؛ أي: علمه تعالى أزلاً صفات المخلوقات، فيرجعُ عندهم لصفة العلم وهي من صفات الذات. والقضاء عند الأشاعرة: إرادة الله الأشياء في الأزل على ما هي عليه فيما لا يزالُ فهو من صفات الذات عندهم . وعند الماتريدية : إيجاد الله الأشياء مع زيادة الإحكام والإتقان، فهو صفة فعل عندهم، فالقَدَر حادث والقضاء قديمٌ عند الأشاعرة، ولا كذلك عند الماتريدية. وقد حمل الشارحُ كلام المصنفِ على مذهب الماتريدية في القدر والقضاء دون مذهب الأشاعرة؛ لأنَّ القضاء في اللغة له نحو معانٍ سبعة أشهرها الحكم، وهو يرجع للفعل، فناسب أن يُفسَّرَ في الاصطلاح بالفعل. وأمَّا القَدَر فلم يرد أن معناه في اللغة الفعل فناسب ألا يُفسَّرَ في الاصطلاح بالفعل بل بالعلم.
Know that the Ash'aris and the Maturidis differed regarding both al-qadr (divine decree) and al-qada (divine predestination). Al-qadr according to the Ash'aris is God's bringing things into existence according to a specific measure and a particular manner, so it reverts in their view to an attribute of action because it is an expression of bringing into existence, and it is among the attributes of actions. According to the Maturidis, it is God's pre-eternal determination of every creature by its limit upon which it comes to be, of good and evil, benefit and harm, etc.; that is, His pre-eternal knowledge of the qualities of creatures, so it reverts in their view to the attribute of knowledge, which is among the attributes of essence. Al-qada according to the Ash'aris is God's willing of things in pre-eternity as they will be forever, so it is from the attributes of essence in their view. According to the Maturidis, it is God's bringing things into existence with added perfection and precision, so it is an attribute of action in their view. Thus al-qadr is contingent and al-qada is pre-eternal according to the Ash'aris, but not so according to the Maturidis. The commentator (al-sharih) interpreted the author's words according to the Maturidi school regarding al-qadr and al-qada rather than the Ash'ari school, because al-qada in language has about seven meanings, the most famous of which is 'judgment' (hukm), and it reverts to action, so it was fitting that it be interpreted in technical usage as action. As for al-qadr, its meaning in language is not 'action', so it was fitting that it not be interpreted in technical usage as action but rather as knowledge.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage defines and compares the terms al-qadr and al-qada according to the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools. It begins by stating that the two schools differ on both terms. Then it defines al-qadr for Ash'aris as God's bringing things into existence according to a specific measure and manner, so it is an attribute of action. For Maturidis, it is God's pre-eternal determination of every creature's characteristics, so it is an attribute of knowledge. Then it defines al-qada for Ash'aris as God's pre-eternal will, so it is an attribute of essence; for Maturidis, it is God's bringing things into existence with perfection and precision, so it is an attribute of action. Thus, for Ash'aris, al-qadr is contingent (hadith) and al-qada is pre-eternal (qadim), but the opposite holds for Maturidis. The commentator explains that he follows the Maturidi understanding because linguistically al-qada means 'judgment' (hukm), which relates to action, so it is appropriate to interpret it as an action in technical terms, whereas al-qadr does not mean 'action' in language, so it is better interpreted as knowledge. This passage establishes the lexical and technical basis for the school's position.

Key Terms
القدر (al-Qadr)
القضاء (al-Qada)
الأشاعرة (Ash'aris)
الماتريدية (Maturidis)
صفة فعل (attribute of action)
صفة الذات (attribute of essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Qadr and Qada mean the same thing in both schools.
Correction: The passage shows that each school defines them differently, leading to opposite classifications as attributes of action or essence.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari school, al-Qadr is an attribute of action because it refers to:
A) pre-eternal will
B) bringing into existence
C) knowledge of characteristics
D) judgment

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari and Maturidi schools define Qadr and Qada in opposite ways, leading to different classifications as attributes of action or essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems unjust, which definition of qada (divine predestination) helps you maintain faith: that God willed it in pre-eternity (Ash'ari) or that He brought it into existence with perfection (Maturidi)? Reflect on a specific incident. What does your inclination reveal about your theology?
?
The Ash'aris say qadr is contingent (hadith) and qada is pre-eternal (qadim). How does the temporal nature of God's actions (as actions) affect your daily practice of seeking His help? Are you asking the Eternal to act in time? How does this shape your du'a?
?
