Why the Commentator Follows the Maturidi View
Explains the commentator's linguistic choice for the Maturidi interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's linguistic justification for interpreting Qadr as knowledge and Qada as action, aligning with the Maturidi school.

Core Idea
Qada's linguistic meaning of 'judgment' favors action, while Qadr's language points to knowledge.

After presenting the two schools' definitions, the commentator of Tuhfat al-Mureed explains why he interprets the poetic verses according to the Maturidi view. He uses a linguistic argument: the word qada in Arabic has many meanings, but the most famous is 'judgment' (hukm), which is an action, so it fits to interpret qada as an act. On the other hand, qadr does not mean 'action' linguistically, so it should be interpreted as knowledge. This lesson explores that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's argument
Read the second part of the passage starting from 'وقد حمل الشارح'. Notice he bases his choice on the linguistic meanings of the terms.
The commentator is not choosing arbitrarily; he uses language as a guide.
2
Understand the linguistic point
He says qada has seven meanings, the most prominent being 'judgment' (hukm), which is an action. Therefore, in technical usage, it should be interpreted as an action. Contrast this with qadr, which does not mean 'action' in language.
This shows the importance of linguistic analysis in kalam.
3
Connect to the earlier definitions
Recall how the Maturidis define qada as action and qadr as knowledge. The commentator's linguistic finding supports the Maturidi position.
4
Reflect on methodology
Consider why a commentator might prefer a particular school's interpretation. This example shows that linguistic analysis is a key tool in reconciling different views.
Not all differences are arbitrary; some are based on language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أنَّ الأشاعرة والماتريدية اختلفوا في كل من القدر والقضاء : فالقَدَر عند الأشاعرة إيجاد الله الأشياء على قدرٍ مخصوص ووجه معين تعالى، فيرجع عندهم لصفة فعلٍ ؛ لأنَّه عبارة عن الإيجاد، وهو من صفات الأفعال. وعند الماتريدية: تحديدُ اللهِ أزلاً كل مخلوق بحده الذي يوجد عليه من حسن وقُبْحٍ ونَفْعِ وضُرٍّ إلى غير ذلك؛ أي: علمه تعالى أزلاً صفات المخلوقات، فيرجعُ عندهم لصفة العلم وهي من صفات الذات. والقضاء عند الأشاعرة: إرادة الله الأشياء في الأزل على ما هي عليه فيما لا يزالُ فهو من صفات الذات عندهم . وعند الماتريدية : إيجاد الله الأشياء مع زيادة الإحكام والإتقان، فهو صفة فعل عندهم، فالقَدَر حادث والقضاء قديمٌ عند الأشاعرة، ولا كذلك عند الماتريدية. وقد حمل الشارحُ كلام المصنفِ على مذهب الماتريدية في القدر والقضاء دون مذهب الأشاعرة؛ لأنَّ القضاء في اللغة له نحو معانٍ سبعة أشهرها الحكم، وهو يرجع للفعل، فناسب أن يُفسَّرَ في الاصطلاح بالفعل. وأمَّا القَدَر فلم يرد أن معناه في اللغة الفعل فناسب ألا يُفسَّرَ في الاصطلاح بالفعل بل بالعلم.
Know that the Ash'aris and the Maturidis differed regarding both al-qadr (divine decree) and al-qada (divine predestination). Al-qadr according to the Ash'aris is God's bringing things into existence according to a specific measure and a particular manner, so it reverts in their view to an attribute of action because it is an expression of bringing into existence, and it is among the attributes of actions. According to the Maturidis, it is God's pre-eternal determination of every creature by its limit upon which it comes to be, of good and evil, benefit and harm, etc.; that is, His pre-eternal knowledge of the qualities of creatures, so it reverts in their view to the attribute of knowledge, which is among the attributes of essence. Al-qada according to the Ash'aris is God's willing of things in pre-eternity as they will be forever, so it is from the attributes of essence in their view. According to the Maturidis, it is God's bringing things into existence with added perfection and precision, so it is an attribute of action in their view. Thus al-qadr is contingent and al-qada is pre-eternal according to the Ash'aris, but not so according to the Maturidis. The commentator (al-sharih) interpreted the author's words according to the Maturidi school regarding al-qadr and al-qada rather than the Ash'ari school, because al-qada in language has about seven meanings, the most famous of which is 'judgment' (hukm), and it reverts to action, so it was fitting that it be interpreted in technical usage as action. As for al-qadr, its meaning in language is not 'action', so it was fitting that it not be interpreted in technical usage as action but rather as knowledge.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage defines and compares the terms al-qadr and al-qada according to the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools. It begins by stating that the two schools differ on both terms. Then it defines al-qadr for Ash'aris as God's bringing things into existence according to a specific measure and manner, so it is an attribute of action. For Maturidis, it is God's pre-eternal determination of every creature's characteristics, so it is an attribute of knowledge. Then it defines al-qada for Ash'aris as God's pre-eternal will, so it is an attribute of essence; for Maturidis, it is God's bringing things into existence with perfection and precision, so it is an attribute of action. Thus, for Ash'aris, al-qadr is contingent (hadith) and al-qada is pre-eternal (qadim), but the opposite holds for Maturidis. The commentator explains that he follows the Maturidi understanding because linguistically al-qada means 'judgment' (hukm), which relates to action, so it is appropriate to interpret it as an action in technical terms, whereas al-qadr does not mean 'action' in language, so it is better interpreted as knowledge. This passage establishes the lexical and technical basis for the school's position.

Key Terms
الحكم (hukm)
القدر (al-Qadr)
القضاء (al-Qada)
الشارح (al-sharih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The commentator's choice is arbitrary or biased toward Maturidism.
Correction: The commentator provides a linguistic justification based on the primary meaning of the terms, showing it is a reasoned preference.

Checkpoint

A) Because the Ash'aris say so
B) Because it means 'judgment' in language
C) Because it is an attribute of essence
D) Because the author said so

Key Takeaway
The commentator's preference for the Maturidi interpretation is based on linguistic analysis: qada means 'judgment' (action) and qadr does not mean action, so qadr is best interpreted as knowledge.

Two Interpretations of Qada and Qadr
Summarizes two interpretations of Qada and Qadr in verse and explains them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main interpretations of Qada and Qadr as summarized by al-Ajhuri, and how they relate to God's attributes of will and knowledge.

Core Idea
Qada is eternal will or knowledge; Qadr is the temporal act of creation.

Al-Ajhuri's poem succinctly captures a key theological distinction: whether Qada refers to God's eternal will or His eternal knowledge. Both views agree that Qadr is the contingent act of creation. This passage is essential for grasping how Sunni theologians discuss divine decree without falling into error.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and explanation
Read the Arabic poem and its literal translation carefully. Then read the commentary to understand the structure.
The poem uses rajaz meter, so it is memorizable.
2
Identify the two opinions
Notice that the poem gives two opinions: the first links Qada to will, the second to knowledge. Both agree Qadr is creation.
3
Understand the attributes involved
Recognize that Qada is associated with eternal attributes (will or knowledge), while Qadr is the temporal act of bringing into existence.
This reflects the Ash'ari principle that divine attributes are eternal, but acts are temporal.
4
Grasp the eternity-contingency distinction
The commentator emphasizes that Qada is pre-eternal (qadim) in both opinions, while Qadr is contingent (hadith). This is crucial for avoiding misunderstanding.
5
Reflect on why this matters
The passage concludes that Qada and Qadr reduce to knowledge, will, and power, but are explicitly mentioned to prevent ignorance. Reflect on the danger of ignorance in this topic.
This shows the pedagogical importance of clarifying these terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد نظم العلامة الأجهوري معنى القضاء والقدر، وحكى فيه الخلاف على غير هذا الوجه، فقال: [من الرجز]
إرَادَةُ اللهِ مَعَ التَّعَلُّقِ فِي
أَزَلٍ قَضَاؤُهُ فَحَقِّقِ
وَالقَدَرُ الإيجادُ لِلأَشْيَا
عَلَى وَجْهِ مُعَيَّنٍ أَرَادَهُ عَلَا
وَبَعْضُهُمْ قَدْ قَالَ: مَعْنَى الأَوَّلِ
العِلْمُ مَعْ تَعَلُّقٍ فِي الأَزَلِ
وَالقَدَرُ الإيجادُ لِلأُمُورِ
عَلَى وَفْقِ عِلْمِهِ المَذْكُورِ
فأنت تراه جعل القضاء هو الإرادة مع التعلُّقِ الأزلي على القول الأول، أو العلم مع التعلَّقِ الأزليّ على القول الثاني، وعلى كل من القولين فهو قديم، وجعل القدر هو الإيجاد على وفق الإرادة على القول الأول، أو الإيجاد على وفق العلم على القول الثاني، وعلى كل من القولين فهو حادث. وبعد هذا كله فالقضاء والقدر راجعان لما تقدَّم من العلم والإرادة وتعلق القدرة، لكن لما كان خطر الجهل في هذا الفن عظيماً صرَّحوا بهما.
And the scholar al-Ajhuri versified the meaning of al-qada and al-qadr, and narrated therein the disagreement in a manner different from this [previous] way, and said (in rajaz meter): / 'God's will with [pre-eternal] attachment / In pre-eternity is His decree, so verify. / And al-qadr is the bringing into existence of things / In a specific manner that He, the Most High, willed. / And some of them said: the meaning of the first [i.e., al-qada] / Is knowledge with attachment in pre-eternity. / And al-qadr is the bringing into existence of things / According to that mentioned knowledge.' / So you see that he made al-qada to be will with pre-eternal attachment according to the first opinion, or knowledge with pre-eternal attachment according to the second opinion, and in either opinion it is pre-eternal. And he made al-qadr to be bringing into existence in accordance with the will according to the first opinion, or bringing into existence in accordance with knowledge according to the second opinion, and in either opinion it is contingent. And after all this, al-qada and al-qadr refer back to what has preceded of knowledge, will, and the attachment of power, but because the danger of ignorance in this art is great, they explicitly mentioned them.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents a poetic summary by al-Ajhuri of the different interpretations of al-qada and al-qadr. The poem gives two opinions. The first opinion: al-qada is God's will with pre-eternal attachment, and al-qadr is the creation of things in a specific manner that He willed. The second opinion: al-qada is God's knowledge with pre-eternal attachment, and al-qadr is the creation of things according to that knowledge. The commentator explains that in both opinions, al-qada is pre-eternal (since will and knowledge are essential attributes) and al-qadr is contingent (since creation is temporal). He concludes by noting that al-qada and al-qadr ultimately reduce to the previously discussed attributes of knowledge, will, and power, but because the danger of ignorance in this topic is great, they explicitly mention them.

القضاء (al-Qada)
القدر (al-Qadr)
الإرادة (al-irada)
العلم (al-ilm)
الإيجاد (al-ijad)
الأجهوري (al-Ajhuri)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Qadr (predestination) is also eternal like Qada, or that both refer to the same thing.
Correction: The passage clarifies that while Qada is eternal (being God's will or knowledge), Qadr is the temporal act of creation. They are distinct: one is the eternal decree, the other its temporal execution.

Checkpoint

A) God's will
B) God's knowledge

D) God's judgment

Key Takeaway
Al-Qada is an eternal divine attribute (will or knowledge), while al-Qadr is the temporal act of creation in accordance with that attribute.

Elongation or Shortening in the Shahada: Scholarly Differences
Author enumerates three positions on elongation vs shortening of shahada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly debate on whether to elongate or shorten the shahada and the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
Pronunciation of the shahada has deeper meaning: elongation emphasizes divine exclusivity, shortening guards against death.

The simple act of pronouncing the testimony of faith carries scholarly differences. Should one elongate the word 'Allah' or pronounce it quickly? This passage explores the wisdom behind each choice, linking pronunciation to spiritual awareness and practical caution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the succinct style of the author.
2
Identify the three positions
The passage presents three positions: elongation, shortening, and a detailed position. Find each one and note the reasoning given.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Elongation helps feel the negation of divinity from all else; shortening avoids risk of death before completing the word. Ask yourself: Which reasoning emphasizes spiritual awareness? Which emphasizes practicality?
Both are valid considerations in Islamic law.
4
Consider the detailed position
The third position distinguishes based on context: if the shahada is the first thing you say, shorten; otherwise, elongate. Why might this be a practical compromise?
This shows that rulings can depend on circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعضهم : الأوسع للذاكر أن يلاحظ أخذهما من القرآن ليثاب عليهما مطلقاً، المتلفظ وقد اختلف العلماء هل الأفضل المدُّ أو القصر؟ فمنهم من اختار المدَّ ليستشعر بهما نفي الألوهية عن كل موجود سواه تعالى ومنهم من اختار القصر لئلا تخترمه المنيَّة قبل التلفظ بذكر الله تعالى، وفصَّل بعضهم بين أن يكون أوّل كلامه بهما فيقصر، وإلا فيمُد، وأما حذف ألف الله فهو لحن لا ذكر، ولا تنعقد معه يصح يمين.
And some said: It is broader for the rememberer to observe taking them from the Quran so that he is rewarded for them absolutely. The one who utters: And the scholars have differed over whether elongation or shortening is better. Some chose elongation so that by them he feels the negation of divinity from every existent other than Him, exalted. And some chose shortening lest death should cut him off before uttering the remembrance of Allah, exalted. And some detailed: if it is the beginning of his speech with them, he shortens; otherwise, he elongates. And as for dropping the alif of Allah, it is a solecism, not remembrance, and an oath concluded with it is not valid.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage addresses practical rulings regarding the utterance of the two testimonies. It begins with an opinion that it is preferable for the one who remembers (al-dhakir) to take the shahada from the Qur'an to receive reward. Then the author presents the scholarly dispute on whether to pronounce the shahada with elongation (madd) of the word 'Allah' or shortening (qasr). Those favoring elongation argue it helps feel the negation of divinity from all else; those favoring shortening fear death before completing the word 'Allah'. A third position distinguishes the situation: if the shahada is the first thing one says, shorten; otherwise, elongate. Finally, the author states that deleting the alif of 'Allah' is a grammatical error (lahn) and not valid remembrance, and an oath made with such pronunciation is not valid. This shows the importance of correct pronunciation in ritual and legal contexts.

Key Terms
المد
القصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When it is the first thing one says
B) When one is in a hurry

D) When one is making an oath

Key Takeaway
Scholars differ on whether to elongate or shorten the shahada, each with valid spiritual and practical reasons.

Dropping the Alif of Allah: A Solecism in Remembrance and Oaths
Author rules on dropping alif of Allah: it is a solecism invalidating remembrance and oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling on dropping the alif of 'Allah' and its legal consequences for remembrance and oaths.

Core Idea
Dropping the alif of 'Allah' is a serious error that invalidates the remembrance and oath.

The shahada is a sacred formula, and its precise pronunciation matters. What happens if one drops a single letter? The author rules that dropping the alif of 'Allah' is not only a grammatical mistake but a legal defect that invalidates both the remembrance itself and any oath pronounced with it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the three rulings: it is a solecism (lahn), not remembrance (la dhikr), and an oath is not valid.
This is a short but decisive statement.
2
Understand 'lahn'
The term 'lahn' denotes a serious error in pronunciation that changes the meaning. Research the term or think of examples in Arabic where dropping a letter changes the word.
In this case, 'Allah' without alif could be mistaken for 'lah' or 'lillah'.
3
Reflect on the two consequences
The author declares that such utterance is not remembrance (dhikr) and that any oath made with it is invalid. Why does precision in the name of Allah matter so much?
This shows the importance of correct pronunciation in ritual and legal contexts.

The Scope of Negation in 'La ilaha illa Allah'
Sanusi clarifies the scope of negation in the shahada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the negation in 'La ilaha illa Allah' must be understood as referring to real objects of worship in external reality, not merely mental concepts.

Core Idea
The shahada negates false gods in reality, not just in the mind.

Before we can understand the logical form of the testimony of tawhid, we must first clarify what the negation is targeting. Is it negating mental concepts, or real objects of worship? This passage from Sanusi's commentary explains why only the real-world interpretation refutes disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author distinguishes between two possible scopes of negation.
2
Identify the two scopes
The passage presents two ways the negation could be directed: at the concept in the believer's mind, or at real objects in the world. Find each.
3
Understand why the mental scope fails
The author argues that applying negation to the believer's mental concept doesn't refute the disbeliever, because the disbeliever's false objects are fixed in his mind. Only the real-world negation achieves refutation.
4
Internalize the key takeaway
The negation must be directed at reality to be effective in refuting polytheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن النفي منصب على المعبود بحق في الواقع، فالمعنى انتفى المعبود بحق في الواقع إلا الله، كما يصح جعله مُنصبا على ما في ذهن المؤمن؛ لأنه يتصور إفراد المعبود بحق على سبيل الفرض، ثم يحكم عليها بالنفي إلا الله، لكنه لا يحصل الرد على الكفار إلا باعتبار الواقع، ولا يصح أن يكون مُنصبا على ما في ذهن الكافر؛ لأنَّ ما في ذهنه من الأصنام ثابت لا يصح نفيه. والتحقيقُ أنَّ الكلمة المشرفة من قبيل عموم السلب؛ أي: السلب العام لجميع أفراد الإله، ما عدا المستثنى؛ لأنه يجب على المتكلّم بهذه الكلمة أن يلاحظ أن الحكم بالنفي منصب على جميع أفراد الإله غير المستثنى؛ لأنه لو جعله شاملاً للمستثنى لكَفّر فقوله : (إلا) الله قرينة على ما أراده أولاً ، لكن جعلها (۱) من عموم السلب على خلاف القاعدة من أنه إذا تقدَّمت أداة السلب على أداة العموم كان الكلام من سلب العموم ؛ كما في قولهم : لم آخذ كل الدراهم)، فإن الحقَّ أنها قاعدة أغلبية، ولا يصح أن تكون الكلمة المشرفة من سلب العموم على القاعدة؛ لأنَّها حينئذ لا تفيد التوحيد وقول بعضهم: إنها من سلب العموم محمول على أنها سلبت الألوهية لغير المستثنى، وقصرتها على المستثنى، لكن لا يفيد ذلك جوهر الكلمة المشرفة.
And know that the negation is directed at the object of worship in truth in reality. So the meaning is: the object of worship in truth is negated in reality except Allah. As it is valid to make it directed at what is in the mind of the believer, because he conceives a singular object of worship in truth by way of supposition, then he judges its negation except Allah. But the refutation of the disbelievers is only achieved by considering reality. And it is not valid to make it directed at what is in the mind of the disbeliever, because what is in his mind of idols is established, so its negation is not valid. And the verified truth is that the noble word is of the type of 'umum al-salb' (universal negation); that is, the universal negation of all individuals of divinity, except the exempted. Because the speaker of this word must observe that the judgment of negation is directed at all individuals of divinity other than the exempted; because if he makes it include the exempted, he disbelieves. So his saying 'except Allah' is a contextual indication of what he intended first. But making it (1) from 'umum al-salb' is contrary to the rule that when the tool of negation precedes the tool of universality, the speech is from 'salb al-'umum' (negation of universality), as in their saying 'I did not take all the dirhams'. Indeed, the truth is that it is a general rule. And it is not valid for the noble word to be from 'salb al-'umum' according to the rule, because then it would not convey tawhid. And the saying of some that it is from 'salb al-'umum' is interpreted as meaning that it negated divinity from other than the exempted and restricted it to the exempted, but that does not benefit the essence of the noble word.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a sophisticated logical analysis of the negation in the testimony 'la ilaha illa Allah'. The author first clarifies the scope of the negation: it is directed at 'the object of worship in truth' in external reality (al-waqi'). The meaning is 'there is no object of worship in truth in reality except Allah.' It is also possible to interpret the negation as directed at the concept in the believer's mind, who conceives a hypothetical singular object of worship and then negates all except Allah. However, this does not refute disbelievers because they have false objects of worship in their minds. The refutation of disbelievers requires the external real-world interpretation. The author then states the verified position (al-tahqiq): the noble word (al-kalimat al-musharrafa) is of the type 'umum al-salb' (universal negation), meaning it negates all individuals of divinity except the exempted (Allah). The speaker must intend that the negation covers all false gods, otherwise if he includes Allah in the negation, he disbelieves. The word 'illa' (except) indicates this. However, grammarians generally say that when the negation tool precedes the universal tool (e.g., 'ma akhadhtu kulla al-darahimi'), it is 'salb al-'umum' (negation of universality), not 'umum al-salb'. The author asserts that this rule is only a general rule (ghalibiyya), and applying it to the shahada would ruin tawhid because it would negate the universality of divinity rather than universally negating false gods. Some scholars try to interpret the shahada as 'salb al-'umum' by meaning that it negates divinity from others and restricts it to Allah, but the author says this does not capture the essence of the noble word, which requires universal negation. Thus, the correct analysis is 'umum al-salb' despite the grammatical rule.

Key Terms
النفي
المعبود بحق
الواقع
ذهن المؤمن
ذهن الكافر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The negation in 'La ilaha illa Allah' only applies to what is in one's mind or imagination.
Correction: The negation must apply to real objects of worship in external reality to effectively refute disbelief, as the author explains.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, directing the negation at the believer's mind is:
A) The only valid interpretation.
B) Valid but does not refute disbelievers.
C) Invalid because the believer's mind has no false gods.
D) The same as directing it at reality.

Key Takeaway
The negation in 'La ilaha illa Allah' must be understood as negating all false objects of worship in external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly negate all false gods in your life in reality, or do you only mentally acknowledge the shahada while something else—like status, wealth, or desire—occupies the place of Allah in your heart? What concrete action can you take today to uproot that hidden idol?
?
When you say 'La ilaha illa Allah', does your declaration shake your attachments to worldly idols, or has it become a comfortable phrase that leaves your actions unchanged? Which false god are you still serving in practice?
?
If your faith were tested by the reality of your deeds—not just your inner conviction—would your life prove that you negate all false gods externally? What one step can you take this week to align your reality with your declaration?

The Verified Position: 'Umum al-Salb' (Universal Negation)
Sanusi defines 'umum al-salb' for the shahada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the verified position that the shahada is an example of 'umum al-salb'—universal negation of all false gods.

Core Idea
The shahada is a universal negation of all false deities, reserving divinity only for Allah.

After clarifying the scope of negation, the author presents the verified position (al-tahqiq). The noble word is of the type 'umum al-salb' (universal negation). This means the negation covers all individuals of the category of divinity except the exempted one (Allah). This lesson unpacks that definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic text defining 'umum al-salb'. Note the key phrase: 'al-salb al-'amm li-jami' afrad al-ilah ma 'ada al-mustathna'.
2
Understand the requirement
The speaker must observe that the judgment of negation covers all individuals of divinity other than the exempted. If he includes the exempted, he disbelieves.
3
Notice the role of 'illa'
The word 'illa' (except) indicates the intended scope.
4
Internalize the concept
Contrast this with a simple negation of a specific claim; here it is a universal, all-encompassing negation.

The Grammatical Rule and the Exception of the Shahada
Sanusi refutes the application of the grammatical rule to the shahada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the shahada cannot follow the usual grammatical rule that negation + universal yields 'salb al-'umum', and why it is specifically 'umum al-salb'.

Core Idea
The shahada defies the usual linguistic rule because applying that rule would nullify tawhid.

The formula 'La ilaha illa Allah' appears to follow the pattern 'negation + universal noun (ilaha)'. According to Arabic grammar, when the negation tool precedes the universal tool (like 'kull'), the statement is usually 'salb al-'umum' (negation of the universal). But the author argues that applying this rule to the shahada would destroy its meaning. This lesson examines that grammatical nuance and why the shahada is an exception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the final part where the author discusses the rule and the exception.
2
Understand the rule
Normally, when negation precedes universality (e.g., 'ma akhadhtu kulla al-darahimi'), it means 'I did not take all the dirhams'—negation of the universal. That is 'salb al-'umum'.
3
Understand why the shahada cannot be that
If it were 'salb al-'umum', it would mean 'there is no god who encompasses all divinity' or 'the category of divinity is not universal'—which doesn't negate false gods. That would not convey tawhid.
4
Author's resolution
The rule is a general rule (ghalibiyya), not absolute. The shahada is an exception due to its unique meaning.
5
Rejection of alternative interpretation
Some try to reinterpret it as 'salb al-'umum' but that misses the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a sophisticated logical analysis of the negation in the testimony 'la ilaha illa Allah'. The Negation in the Shahada: General Negation vs. Negation of the General
Ibn al-Mubarak discusses the ruling on elongation and the logical structure of the shahada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two aspects of the shahada 'La ilaha illa Allah': the ruling on elongating 'Allah' and the critical logical distinction between general negation (عموم السلب) and negation of the general (سلب العموم).

Core Idea
The shahada negates all false gods collectively — it is general negation despite the word order.

This passage from Ibn al-Mubarak's commentary tackles two important issues regarding the shahada: a practical one about pronunciation and a deep logical one about what the negation really means. Understanding why the shahada is 'general negation' rather than 'negation of the general' is essential for a correct tawhid. Let's unpack the author's reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on elongation
Read the first part about whether to elongate or shorten 'Allah'. Notice the different scholarly views and the reasoning behind each.
This is a practical fiqh issue but shows how even pronunciation can reflect theology.
2
Understand the direction of negation
Read the second part where the author explains that negation is directed at the truly worshipped in reality, not at mental concepts of disbelievers. This establishes the objective basis of the shahada.
3
Grasp the key logical distinction
Read the third part carefully. The author argues that the shahada is 'عموم السلب' (general negation), not 'سلب العموم' (negation of the general). Understand why the latter would not benefit tawhid.
The standard rule about word order is only a majority rule; the shahada is an exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اختلف العلماء هل الأفضل المدُّ أو القصر؟ فمنهم من اختار المدَّ ليستشعر بهما نفي الألوهية عن كل موجود سواه، تعالى ومنهم من اختار القصر لئلا تخترمه المنيَّة قبل التلفظ بذكر الله تعالى، وفصَّل بعضهم بين أن يكون أوّل كلامه بهما فيقصر، وإلا، فيمد، وأما حذف ألف الله فهو لحن لا ذكر، ولا تنعقد معه يمين .
واعلم أن النفي منصب على المعبود بحق في الواقع، فالمعنى انتفى المعبود بحق في الواقع إلا الله، كما يصح جعله مُنصبا على ما في ذهن المؤمن؛ لأنه يتصور إفراد المعبود بحق على سبيل الفرض، ثم يحكم عليها بالنفي إلا الله، لكنه لا يحصل الرد على الكفار إلا باعتبار الواقع، ولا يصح أن يكون مُنصبا على ما في ذهن الكافر؛ لأنَّ ما في ذهنه من الأصنام ثابت لا يصح نفيه .
،
والتحقيقُ أنَّ الكلمة المشرفة من قبيل عموم السلب؛ أي: السلب العام لجميع أفراد الإله، ما عدا المستثنى؛ لأنه يجب على المتكلّم بهذه الكلمة أن يلاحظ أن الحكم بالنفي منصب على جميع أفراد الإله غير المستثنى؛ لأنه لو جعله شاملاً للمستثنى لكَفّر فقوله : (إلا) الله قرينة على ما أراده أولاً، لكن جعلها (۱) من عموم السلب على خلاف القاعدة من أنه إذا تقدَّمت أداة السلب على أداة العموم كان الكلام من سلب العموم؛ كما في قولهم : لم آخذ كل الدراهم)، فإن الحقَّ أنها قاعدة أغلبية، ولا يصح أن تكون الكلمة المشرفة من سلب العموم على القاعدة؛ لأنَّها حينئذ لا تفيد التوحيد وقول بعضهم: إنها من سلب العموم محمول على أنها سلبت الألوهية لغير المستثنى، وقصرتها على المستثنى، لكن لا يفيد ذلك الكلمة المشرفة.
(1) في (أ) : زيادة (بعضهم).
(۲) في :(أ) (عممت) بدل (سلیت عموم).
And the scholars differed: is elongation or shortening better? Some chose elongation to perceive by them the negation of divinity from every existing other than Him, Most High. And some chose shortening so that death does not cut him off before uttering the remembrance of Allah, Most High. And some detailed: if it is the beginning of his speech with them, then shorten; otherwise, elongate. As for omitting the alif of Allah, it is a grammatical error, not remembrance, and an oath is not valid with it.
And know that the negation is directed at the truly worshipped in reality. So the meaning is: the truly worshipped in reality is negated except Allah. It is also valid to make it directed at what is in the believer's mind, because he conceives singling out the truly worshipped hypothetically, then judges negation on it except Allah. But refutation of the disbelievers is not achieved except by considering reality. And it is not valid to direct it at what is in the disbeliever's mind, because what is in his mind of idols is established and its negation is not valid.
And the verification is that the noble word is of the type of general negation (عموم السلب), i.e., the general negation of all individuals of deity except the excluded. This is because the speaker of this word must observe that the judgment of negation is directed at all individuals of deity other than the excluded. For if he made it include the excluded, he would disbelieve. So his saying 'except Allah' is an indication of what he intended initially. But making it of general negation is contrary to the rule that when the tool of negation precedes the tool of generality, the statement is of negation of the general (سلب العموم), as in their saying 'I did not take all the dirhams.' Indeed, the truth is that it is a majority rule, and it is not valid for the noble word to be of negation of the general according to the rule, because then it would not benefit tawhid. And the saying of some that it is of negation of the general is interpreted as that it negated divinity to other than the excluded and restricted it to the excluded, but that does not benefit the noble word.
(1) In (A): addition of 'some of them.'
(2) In (A): 'generalized' instead of 'negated generality.'
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the shahada 'La ilaha illa Allah', focusing on two aspects: the rule of elongation/shortening of the word 'Allah' in pronunciation, and the logical structure of its negation. The author begins by presenting the scholarly disagreement on whether it is better to elongate or shorten the word 'Allah' when uttering the shahada. Some prefer elongation to emphasize the negation of divinity from all besides God; others prefer shortening to ensure one does not die before finishing the utterance. Some differentiate based on whether it is the first utterance (shorten) or not (elongate). Omitting the alif is a grammatical error and invalidates an oath.

The author then explains that the negation in the shahada is directed at the truly worshipped in reality. The meaning is that there is no truly worshipped deity except Allah. It can also be directed at what is in the believer's mind (i.e., the believer's conception of a single true deity), but the disbelievers are refuted only by considering reality, not their mental concepts. The author argues that the shahada is of the type 'عموم السلب' (general negation), meaning the negation applies to all individuals of the category 'deity' except the one excluded. This is because the speaker must intend to negate all false gods collectively. If he includes the true God in the negation, he would be a disbeliever. The exception 'except Allah' clarifies the intention.

The author then addresses a logical nuance: normally, when the negation tool precedes the generality tool, it is 'سلب العموم' (negation of the general), as in 'I did not take all the dirhams' (meaning I took some). According to this rule, 'لا إله إلا الله' would be negation of the general, implying that some deities are negated but not all. However, this interpretation would not convey tawhid. Therefore, the author states that the rule is only a majority rule, and the shahada is an exception: it is 'عموم السلب' (general negation) despite the word order. Some scholars interpret it as negation of the general but relegate the exception, but that interpretation does not benefit the noble word. Footnotes indicate variant readings in a manuscript (A).

Key Terms
عموم السلب
سلب العموم
المد
القصر
التوحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The shahada is an example of negation of the general (سلب العموم) because the negation precedes the general tool.
Correction: The author explains that this rule is only a majority rule, and the shahada is an exception because it is meant to negate all false gods collectively; otherwise it would not convey tawhid.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the shahada cannot be of the type 'سلب العموم' (negation of the general) according to the standard rule?
A) Because the negation precedes the generality tool.
B) Because it would then not benefit tawhid.
C) Because the rule is absolute and cannot be violated.
D) Because the exception 'except Allah' makes it general negation.

Key Takeaway
The shahada 'La ilaha illa Allah' is a general negation negating all false deities collectively, and its logical structure is 'عموم السلب' despite the word order.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'La ilaha illa Allah', do you truly negate every false god in your heart—including your own desires, status, and attachments? What false deity do you struggle to fully negate, and what step will you take today to remove it from your reality?
?
The author emphasizes that the negation must be directed at reality, not just mental concepts. Are there areas in your life where you theoretically affirm tawhid but practically rely on other things? How can you make your daily actions a living reflection of the shahada's comprehensive negation?
?
The shahada is an exception to the logical rule because it must benefit tawhid. Is your recitation of the shahada merely a habitual utterance, or does it actively shape your worldview and decisions? What one change can you make this week to let the shahada's meaning transform your priorities?

Grammatical Analysis of the Line on Isma
Commentator parses the line on divine protection, discussing readings and objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple readings of the word 'hatman' in the context of divine protection and the grammatical principles governing its interpretation.

Core Idea
One word, three grammatical possibilities, one unwavering creed.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid delves into the grammar of a key line about 'isma (divine protection). The commentator examines two readings of the word 'hatman' (decisively), explaining how each fits syntactically and what theological point they reinforce. Before you read the analysis, consider how subtle grammatical choices can affirm or nuance core beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the gist of the grammatical discussion.
Pay attention to the different readings indicated by vowel marks.
2
Identify the two readings of 'hatman'
The passage presents 'hatman' with fatha (imperative) and with damm (passive past). Note the different syntactical consequences.
The light nun of emphasis plays a key role in the first reading.
3
Understand the objection and answer
An objection is raised about the verb not governing what precedes it. The answer distinguishes between technical explication and general indication.
This is a subtle point of Arabic grammatical theory.
4
Extract the theological conclusion
Despite grammatical variations, the meaning remains constant: belief in necessary, unceasing divine protection for prophets and angels.
The grammatical discussion serves to affirm the creed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منقلبة. المصدر لفاعله و(لكل) متعلّق بعصمة، و(حتما) بفتح الحاء على أنه فعل أمر، وألفه عن تون التوكيد الخفيفة في الوقف بعد حذف الرابط، والأصل: حتمنها، والجملة خبر المبتدأ ، وهو (عصمة إن قرئ بالرفع، ويصح أن يقرأ بالنصب على أنه مفعول لمحذوف يدلُّ عليه المذكور والتقدير وحتم عصمة الباري، ولم يجعل مفعولاً للمذكور؛ لأنه مقترن بنون التوكيد الخفيفة، وهو حينئذ لا يعمل فيما قبله . فإن قيل : إذا لم يعمل لا يفسر عاملاً أجيب بأن قولهم: (ما لا يعمل لا يفسر عاملاً) إنما هو في التفسير الاصطلاحي، فلا ينافي أنه يشير له في الجملة، أو بضم الحاء على أنه فعل ماض مبني للمجهول وألفه للإطلاق، وعلى هذا فـ (عصمة) بالرفع لا غير على أنه مبتدأ والجملة من الفعل ونائب الفاعل خبره وتذكير الضمير الذي هو نائب الفاعل مع كونه عائداً على العصمة لتذكيرها باعتبار كونها وصفاً، وعلى كل فالمعنى: اعتقد أن عصمة الباري لكلِّ واحدٍ من الأنبياء والملائكة متحتمة وواجبة؛ بمعنى أنها لا تنفك ولا تقبل الانتفاء،
Inverted. The verbal noun is for its subject, and 'li-kulli' is attached to 'isma. As for 'hatman' (a decisive) with fatha on the ha, it is an imperative verb, and its alif is from the light nun of emphasis in pausal form after deletion of the connector, and the original is 'hatamnaha'. The sentence is the predicate of the subject, which is 'isma if read in the nominative. It is also correct to read it in the accusative as an object of a deleted verb indicated by what is mentioned, the implied meaning being 'and He decreed the protection of the Creator'. It is not made an object of the mentioned verb because it is paired with the light nun of emphasis, and then it does not govern what precedes it. If it is said: when it does not govern, it cannot explicate a governing factor, the answer is that their statement 'what does not govern does not explicate a governing factor' only applies in technical explication, so it does not preclude that it indicates it in general. Alternatively, with damm on the ha as a passive past verb and its alif for absolute ending, and on this, 'isma is only in the nominative as a subject and the sentence of the verb and its agent is its predicate, and the masculine pronoun of the agent despite referring to 'isma is due to its being considered masculine as a description. In any case, the meaning is: believe that the Creator's protection for each of the prophets and angels is decreed and obligatory, meaning it never departs and cannot be removed.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and lexical analysis of a line of poetry from the Jawharat al-Tawhid concerning the concept of 'isma (divine protection). The commentator begins by noting that the word 'munqalibah' (inverted) at the end of the previous line is a verbal noun acting as the subject. He then clarifies the attachment of 'li-kulli' (for every) to 'isma. The main point of discussion is the word 'hatman' (decisively). Two readings are presented: 1) with fatha on the ha as an imperative verb, with the alif representing a light nun of emphasis after deletion of the connector, the original being 'hatamnaha'. The predicate of 'isma (in nominative) is the sentence. 2) It can also be read in the accusative as an object of a deleted verb. However, the verb with the light nun of emphasis does not govern what precedes it. An objection is raised: if it does not govern, it cannot explicate a governing factor. The reply distinguishes between technical explication and mere indication. Another reading: with damm on the ha as a passive past verb, making 'isma in the nominative as the subject of a nominal sentence. The conclusion is that the meaning is the same: belief that God's protection for all prophets and angels is definitive and necessary, never ceasing.

Key Terms
حتما
عصمة
نون التوكيد الخفيفة
التفسير الاصطلاحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'hatman' only functions as an adverb and has a single grammatical role.
Correction: The commentator shows that 'hatman' can be read as an imperative verb or a passive past verb, each with distinct syntactical implications, yet all lead to the same theological meaning.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is 'hatman' not allowed to govern what precedes it when read with fatha?
A) Because it is an imperative verb
B) Because it is paired with the light nun of emphasis
C) Because it is a verbal noun
D) Because it is in the accusative case

Key Takeaway
The grammatical flexibility in the line on 'isma does not alter the core belief that God's protection for prophets and angels is decisive and necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The commentator distinguishes between technical grammatical explication and general indication. How can you apply the lesson's balance between precision and core understanding in your own study?

Saraf and 'Adl: Understanding Divine Acceptance and Cursing Sinners
Al-Laqqani defines 'saraf' and 'adl' and rules on cursing unspecified sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the interpretation of the terms 'saraf' and 'adl' as used in a hadith, and the ruling on cursing unspecified sinners.

Core Idea
Saraf and 'Adl describe types of deeds Allah may reject; cursing unspecified sinners is allowed.

This short passage from Al-Laqqani's commentary clarifies two key terms that appear in a well-known hadith. It also addresses a practical question: is it permissible to curse sinners in general? Let's examine the text to understand the scholarly discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning.
2
Identify the two definitions
Notice how the passage defines 'saraf' and 'adl' with two different opinions. Which one is presented as the first view?
3
Understand the ruling on cursing
The passage states that cursing unspecified sinners is permissible. Why might this be restricted? The text mentions an exception for the one who considers it lawful.
This is a nuance in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والناس أجمعين، لا يقبلُ الله منه صَرْفاً ولا عَدْلاً (١) ، ومعلوم (۲) جواز لَعْنِ غيرِ المعين من العصاة والصرف الفرضُ، والعدلُ النقلُ . وقيل : بالعكس، وقيل غير ذلك، وهذا في المستحل، أو خارج مخرج المبالغة في الزَّجْر .
And all people, Allah does not accept from him saraf nor 'adl (1), and it is known (2) the permissibility of cursing the unspecified among the sinners. And al-saraf is the supererogatory [act], and al-'adl is the obligatory [act]. And it is said: the opposite, and it is said other than that, and this is concerning the one who considers it lawful, or it is to be understood as hyperbolic deterrent.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line of the poem. It defines two terms 'saraf' and 'adl' which appear in a hadith. The author states the common interpretation: saraf means voluntary act, 'adl means obligatory act. Then he notes that there is a contrary opinion, and that this discussion is applied to the one who considers these practices lawful, or it is a hyperbolic expression of deterrence. The passage also mentions the permissibility of cursing unspecified sinners.

Key Terms

عدل
لعن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Cursing any sinner is always forbidden.
Correction: The passage states that cursing unspecified sinners (i.e., not naming individuals) is permissible.

Checkpoint

B) Saraf is supererogatory, 'adl is obligatory.
C) Both are obligatory.
D) Both are supererogatory.

Key Takeaway
The terms 'saraf' and 'adl' in the hadith are interpreted as obligatory and supererogatory acts, and cursing unspecified sinners is permissible.

Definition of Wali: A Saint in Islamic Theology
Sanusi defines the wali and explains its conditions and etymology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of a wali (saint) in Sunni theology, including its conditions, the nuance about sin and repentance, and the two meanings of the term.

Core Idea
A saint is not sinless, but one who knows Allah and repents quickly.

The term wali appears frequently in Islamic discourse, but its technical meaning in kalam is precise. This passage from Sanusi's commentary clarifies what truly makes someone a wali, distinguishing it from common misconceptions about saintly infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Let the author's own wording establish the definition.
2
Identify the key conditions
Find the conditions for being a wali: knowledge of Allah, constant obedience, avoidance of sin with repentance. Note the explicit denial of sinlessness.
3
Understand 'the wali lies not'
This phrase means the saint's outward state matches his inner state, and he abstains from excessive lawful pleasures. Note that lawful enjoyment for strength in worship is allowed.
4
Learn the two derivations of 'wali'
The term wali comes from Allah taking charge of the saint's affair, and from the saint's continuous worship without sin. Both meanings must be realized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النبوات ورُوي أن عبد الله الشقيق كان إذا مرت عليه سحابة يقول لها : أقسمت عليك بالله إلا أمطرت، فتمطر في الحال. والأولياء جمع وَلِيّ، وهو العارف بالله تعالى وبصفاته حسب الإمكان، المواظب على الطاعات المجتنب للمعاصي بمعنى أنه لا يرتكب معصية بدون توبة، وليس المراد أنه لا تقع منه معصية بالكلية؛ إذ ليس معصوماً . وقولهم : (لا) يكذب (الولي أي بلسان حاله بأن يُظهر خلاف ما يُبطن المعرضُ عن الانهماك في اللذات والشهوات المباحة، وأما أصل التناول فلا مانع منه، لا سيما إذا كان بقصد التقوي على العبادة، وسُمي (وليا) ؛ لأن الله تولَّى أمره فلم يكله إلى نفسه ولا إلى غيره لحظةٌ، ولأنه يتولَّى عبادة الله على الدوام من غير أن يتخللها عصيان، وكلا المعنيين واجب تحققه حتى يكون الوليُّ عندنا وليا في نفس الأمر. والكرامةُ : أمرٌ خارقٌ للعادة يظهر على يد عبد ظاهر الصلاح ملتزم لمتابعة نبي بشريعته، مصحوب بصحيح الاعتقاد والعمل الصالح، علم بها أو لم يُعلم بها ، وسبق ما يتعلق بخوارق العادة عند المعجزات.
Prophethoods: And it is related that Abdullah al-Shiqdiq, when a cloud passed over him, would say to it: ‘I adjure you by Allah, except that you rain,’ and it would rain immediately. And al-awliya (the saints) is the plural of wali, and he is the one who knows Allah Most High and His attributes to the extent possible, who is constant in acts of obedience and avoids sins, meaning that he does not commit a sin without repentance, and it is not meant that no sin occurs from him at all, for he is not infallible. And their saying: (no) the wali lies not (i.e., by his state, that he shows contrary to what he conceals, turning away from immersion in lawful pleasures and desires; but as for the original taking [of lawful things] there is no objection, especially if it is for the purpose of strengthening oneself for worship). And he is called wali because Allah has taken charge of his affair, not entrusting him to himself or others for even a moment, and because he continuously occupies himself with the worship of Allah without sin intervening. Both meanings must be realized so that the wali is, according to us, a wali in reality. And al-karama (the miracle of a saint) is an extraordinary matter that appears at the hand of a servant of apparent righteousness, committed to following a prophet in his law, accompanied by correct belief and righteous action, whether he knows it or not; and what pertains to extraordinary matters has already preceded in the discussion of miracles.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage defines the concepts of wali (saint) and karama (miracle of a saint). It begins with an anecdote about Abdullah al-Shiqdiq, a saint who commanded a cloud to rain by adjuring it by Allah, demonstrating a karama. The author then defines wali: a person who knows Allah and His attributes as much as possible, perseveres in obedience, and avoids sins, but with the nuance that he may commit a sin but repents; he is not sinless like a prophet. The statement 'the wali lies not' is explained to mean that his outward state does not contradict his inner state, and he does not indulge excessively in lawful pleasures, though using them for strength in worship is fine. The term wali is derived from Allah taking charge of his affairs and from his constant worship without sin. Then karama is defined as an extraordinary event occurring at the hands of a righteous person who follows a prophet's law, has correct belief and good deeds, whether he is aware of it or not. This builds on the earlier discussion of extraordinary matters in the context of prophetic miracles.

Key Terms
ولي
ولاية
كرامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Saints are sinless like prophets.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the wali is not sinless; he may commit a sin but repents.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a condition for being a wali?
A) Never committing any sin at all.
B) Being constant in acts of obedience.
C) Being a prophet.
D) Knowing Allah completely without limit.

Key Takeaway
A wali is a righteous believer who knows Allah, obeys Him, and repents from sin, but is not infallible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The wali is defined as one who knows Allah 'to the extent possible.' You claim to know Allah, but when was the last time you genuinely sought to increase that knowledge through study or reflection? What one specific attribute of Allah will you learn more about this week?
?
A wali does not commit a sin without immediate repentance. Think of your last sin: did you rush to repent right away, or did you delay? What does your delay reveal about your love for Allah? Will you now make a firm resolve to repent instantly after every slip?
?
The wali's outward state never contradicts his inner state. Is your public conduct—your speech, your social media, your interactions—a true reflection of what is in your heart? In which area are you most tempted to show a false image? What will you do today to align the two?

Definition of Karama: The Miracle of a Saint
Sanusi defines karama using an anecdote and lists its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what a karama (saintly miracle) is, its conditions, and how it differs from a prophetic miracle.

Core Idea
A karama is an extraordinary event at the hands of a righteous follower of a prophet.

Extraordinary events occur not only through prophets but also through saints. This lesson defines karama and sets it apart from miracles of prophets, with a vivid example from a saint's life.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote of Abdullah al-Shiqdiq
This story demonstrates a karama: a saint commands a cloud to rain by adjuring it by Allah, and it rains immediately.
2
Read the definition of karama
Find the formal definition: an extraordinary matter at the hand of a righteous servant following a prophet, with correct belief and action.
3
Identify the conditions for karama
Conditions include: apparent righteousness, commitment to following a prophet, correct belief, and righteous action. The saint may or may not be aware of the karama.
4
Connect to the broader discussion
Note that karama falls under the general category of extraordinary matters (khawariq al-‘adah), previously discussed in the context of prophetic miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النبوات ورُوي أن عبد الله الشقيق كان إذا مرت عليه سحابة يقول لها : أقسمت عليك بالله إلا أمطرت، فتمطر في الحال. والأولياء جمع وَلِيّ، وهو العارف بالله تعالى وبصفاته حسب الإمكان، المواظب على الطاعات المجتنب للمعاصي بمعنى أنه لا يرتكب معصية بدون توبة، وليس المراد أنه لا تقع منه معصية بالكلية؛ إذ ليس معصوماً . وقولهم : (لا) يكذب (الولي أي بلسان حاله بأن يُظهر خلاف ما يُبطن المعرضُ عن الانهماك في اللذات والشهوات المباحة، وأما أصل التناول فلا مانع منه، لا سيما إذا كان بقصد التقوي على العبادة، وسُمي (وليا) ؛ لأن الله تولَّى أمره فلم يكله إلى نفسه ولا إلى غيره لحظةٌ، ولأنه يتولَّى عبادة الله على الدوام من غير أن يتخللها عصيان، وكلا المعنيين واجب تحققه حتى يكون الوليُّ عندنا وليا في نفس الأمر. والكرامةُ : أمرٌ خارقٌ للعادة يظهر على يد عبد ظاهر الصلاح ملتزم لمتابعة نبي بشريعته، مصحوب بصحيح الاعتقاد والعمل الصالح، علم بها أو لم يُعلم بها ، وسبق ما يتعلق بخوارق العادة عند المعجزات.
Prophethoods: And it is related that Abdullah al-Shiqdiq, when a cloud passed over him, would say to it: ‘I adjure you by Allah, except that you rain,’ and it would rain immediately. And al-awliya (the saints) is the plural of wali, and he is the one who knows Allah Most High and His attributes to the extent possible, who is constant in acts of obedience and avoids sins, meaning that he does not commit a sin without repentance, and it is not meant that no sin occurs from him at all, for he is not infallible. And their saying: (no) the wali lies not (i.e., by his state, that he shows contrary to what he conceals, turning away from immersion in lawful pleasures and desires; but as for the original taking [of lawful things] there is no objection, especially if it is for the purpose of strengthening oneself for worship). And he is called wali because Allah has taken charge of his affair, not entrusting him to himself or others for even a moment, and because he continuously occupies himself with the worship of Allah without sin intervening. Both meanings must be realized so that the wali is, according to us, a wali in reality. And al-karama (the miracle of a saint) is an extraordinary matter that appears at the hand of a servant of apparent righteousness, committed to following a prophet in his law, accompanied by correct belief and righteous action, whether he knows it or not; and what pertains to extraordinary matters has already preceded in the discussion of miracles.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage defines the concepts of wali (saint) and karama (miracle of a saint). It begins with an anecdote about Abdullah al-Shiqdiq, a saint who commanded a cloud to rain by adjuring it by Allah, demonstrating a karama. The author then defines wali: a person who knows Allah and His attributes as much as possible, perseveres in obedience, and avoids sins, but with the nuance that he may commit a sin but repents; he is not sinless like a prophet. The statement 'the wali lies not' is explained to mean that his outward state does not contradict his inner state, and he does not indulge excessively in lawful pleasures, though using them for strength in worship is fine. The term wali is derived from Allah taking charge of his affairs and from his constant worship without sin. Then karama is defined as an extraordinary event occurring at the hands of a righteous person who follows a prophet's law, has correct belief and good deeds, whether he is aware of it or not. This builds on the earlier discussion of extraordinary matters in the context of prophetic miracles.

Key Terms
كرامة
خارق للعادة
معجزة
ولي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A karama is the same as a prophetic miracle and can be performed by anyone.
Correction: A karama occurs only at the hands of a righteous follower of a prophet, not a prophet himself, and requires correct belief and action.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is required for a karama to occur?
A) The saint must be aware of the karama.
B) The saint must be a prophet.
C) The saint must have correct belief and righteous action.
D) The saint must never have committed any sin.

Key Takeaway
A karama is an extraordinary event granted by Allah to a righteous saint who follows a prophet, distinct from prophetic miracles.

Why Knowledge Is Superior to Intellect
Sanusi rules on the debate between intellect and knowledge, favoring knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classical Sunni scholars consider knowledge (al-'ilm) superior to the intellect (al-'aql), based on the divine nature of knowledge and the fabricated nature of contrary reports.

Core Idea
Knowledge is superior because it is a divine attribute; the intellect is created.

In Islamic theology, a debate arose about whether the intellect or revealed knowledge is superior. Some argued that the intellect is the tool for acquiring knowledge, hence superior. However, the authoritative position, as stated by Imam al-Sanusi and supported by al-Suyuti, is that knowledge itself is superior. This lesson examines the reasoning behind this preference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English side by side to grasp the author's conclusion.
2
Identify the two reasons
Notice the two reasons given: (1) knowledge is an attribute of Allah, (2) reports praising intellect are fabricated.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters for Islamic theology: it affirms the primacy of divine attributes over created faculties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اختلف في تفضيل العقل على العلم أو العكس، والراجح تفضيل العلم على العقل؛ لأن العلم من صفاته تعالى وما يُروى في تفضيل العقل فهو موضوع لا أصل له، كما صرح به الجلال السيوطي.
And there has been disagreement concerning preferring the intellect over knowledge or the opposite, and the preponderant view is preferring knowledge over the intellect; because knowledge is among His attributes—exalted is He—and what is narrated concerning preferring the intellect is fabricated, having no basis, as al-Jalal al-Suyuti explicitly stated.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a brief ruling on a debated issue: whether the intellect (al-'aql) is superior to knowledge (al-'ilm) or vice versa. The author declares that the preponderant (al-rajih) view is that knowledge is superior to the intellect. He gives two reasons: first, knowledge is one of the attributes of Allah (sifatuhu ta'ala), whereas the intellect is a created quality; second, any traditions (reports) that claim the superiority of the intellect over knowledge are fabricated (mawdu') and have no authentic basis. He cites the authority of al-Jalal al-Suyuti to support this claim.

Key Terms
العلم (al-'ilm)
العقل (al-'aql)
موضوع (mawdu')
صفات الله (sifat Allah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the intellect is superior because it is the tool for acquiring knowledge.
Correction: The passage clarifies that knowledge is superior because it is an attribute of Allah, while the intellect is a created faculty.

Checkpoint
Why is knowledge considered superior to the intellect in this passage?
A) Because the intellect can err.
B) Because knowledge is an attribute of Allah.
C) Because the intellect is limited.
D) Because knowledge is more useful.

Key Takeaway
The preponderant view in Sunni theology is that knowledge (al-'ilm) is superior to the intellect (al-'aql), because knowledge is a divine attribute and contrary reports are fabricated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily pursuit of knowledge, do you truly regard it as a divine attribute, or do you treat it as a mere human achievement? What would change if you saw every act of learning as connecting to Allah's attribute of knowledge?
?
When you rely on your intellect to understand deen, have you ever felt that your reasoning is superior to a clear text or scholarly consensus? How can you humble your intellect before revealed knowledge, knowing that the intellect is created and limited?
?
If you have ever heard or passed on a hadith praising the intellect over knowledge without verifying its authenticity, what does that reveal about your commitment to preserving the purity of the religion? Will you now check the source before repeating such claims?

Definition of Forgiveness (Ghafr)
Defines forgiveness of sins and presents two interpretations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of forgiveness of sins and the two scholarly interpretations regarding the record of the sin.

Core Idea
Forgiveness is non-accountability, but scholars differ on whether the sin is hidden or erased.

This lesson introduces the kalam term 'ghafr' (forgiveness) as defined by Sanusi. The passage clarifies that forgiveness means not being held accountable for a sin, and then presents two possible mechanisms: concealment of the sin while it remains in the record, or complete erasure from the angels' scrolls. We will explore these interpretations and note which is considered correct by the investigators.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the definition and the two options presented.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Notice that after defining forgiveness as non-accountability, the author gives two ways this can occur: concealment or erasure. Look for the Arabic keywords 'بستره' and 'بمحوه'.
The first interpretation retains the sin in the record but hides it; the second removes it entirely.
3
Understand the scholarly preference
The passage ends by noting that some scholars consider concealment to be the correct view according to the investigators (muhaqqiqun). Ask yourself why this might be the preferred interpretation.
This reflects a theological nuance: forgiveness does not necessarily entail complete deletion from the record.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن غفر الذنب العفو عنه؛ أي: عدم المؤاخذة به؛ إما بستره عن أعين مع بقائه في الصحيفة، وإما بمحوه من صحف الملائكة. وحكى بعضهم أن الأول هو الصحيح عند المحققين.
And know that the forgiveness of the sin is pardon from it; i.e., not being taken to account for it; either by concealing it from eyes while it remains in the record, or by erasing it from the scrolls of the angels. And some narrated that the first is the correct view according to the investigators.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage defines forgiveness (ghafr) as pardon and non-accountability for the sin. It explains two possible mechanisms: either the sin is hidden from sight but remains recorded in the book of deeds, or it is completely erased from the angels' scrolls. The passage notes that some scholars report the first interpretation (concealment while retaining the record) as the correct view according to the investigators (muhaqqiqun).

Key Terms

عفو
مؤاخذة
صحيفة
صحف الملائكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Forgiveness always means total erasure of the sin from all records.
Correction: The text presents a view that forgiveness can also mean concealment while the sin remains recorded, and this is considered correct by some investigators.

Checkpoint

A) Erasure of the sin or ignoring it
B) Concealment while it remains recorded, or erasure from the angels' records
C) Pardon only, with no change in record
D) Destruction of the sin or forgetting it

Key Takeaway
Forgiveness in kalam is defined as non-accountability, with two interpretations regarding the sin's record, and the preferred view among investigators is concealment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Two Types of Rizq: Apparent and Hidden
Classification of rizq into two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that rizq (provision) encompasses both material sustenance for the body and spiritual sustenance for the heart, as classified by the author.

Core Idea
Rizq is not just food — it includes knowledge and spiritual insight.

Before we delve deeper into the nature of provision, the author presents a foundational classification: rizq (provision) comes in two forms. The first is tangible and physical, like food for the body. The second is intangible and spiritual, like knowledge for the soul. This brief but powerful classification broadens our understanding of Allah's provision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic sentence and its English translation. Notice the concise structure.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage explicitly names two types: ظاهرة (apparent) and باطنة (hidden). Note what each is for.
3

4

Many assume rizq is only material. This passage corrects that by including spiritual benefits as rizq.

The categories of provisions are two types: apparent, for bodies, like foods; and hidden, for hearts, like sciences and cognitions.

This passage is a classification. The author states that rizq (provisions) can be divided into two categories: (1) apparent sustenance, which is for the physical bodies, such as food and drink; and (2) hidden sustenance, which is for the hearts, such as knowledge and spiritual insights. This highlights that spiritual benefits are also considered rizq from Allah.

The Thing Is the Existent: Definition and Sectarian Contrast
Definition of 'thing' as existent and contrast with Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari theologians define 'thing' (shay') exclusively as the existent and how this refutes the Mu'tazilite notion of non-existent things having reality.

Core Idea
For Sunnis, 'thing' = existent; non-existents are not things.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid establishes a foundational terminological principle: the word 'thing' (shay') in technical theological discourse is synonymous with 'existent' (mawjud). The author clarifies that this definition excludes all non-existent entities—whether possible or impossible—and then contrasts this with the Mu'tazilite view that non-existents are 'things' with a fixed reality. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping later discussions on divine attributes and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic source passage once, then its English translation. Notice the concise, definitional style.
Pay attention to the phrase 'وعندنا الشيء هو الموجود' which states the position clearly.
2
Understand the core definition
The passage explains that 'thing' and 'existent' are coextensive: everything that is a thing is existent, and vice versa. Ask yourself what this implies about non-existent objects.
3
Identify the implication for non-existents
The author explicitly states that the non-existent is not a thing, whether it is possible or impossible. Reflect on why this contradicts the Mu'tazilite position.
The contrast is drawn with the Mu'tazila who say non-existents are 'things' with a concealed reality.
4
Contrast Sunni and Mu'tazilite views
The passage contrasts the Sunni view that things are created by a maker (jāʿil) with the Mu'tazilite view that realities are pre-existing and only manifested. Understand how this affects the concept of divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[الشيء هو الموجود] ١٢٢ - وَعِنْدَنَا الشَّيءُ هو المَوْجُودُ وثَابِتُ في الخارج المَوْجُودُ قوله: (وَعِنْدَنَا الشَّيءُ هو المَوْجُودُ) أي: وعندنا معاشر أهل الحقِّ من الأشاعرة وغيرهم الشيء هو الموجود، فإن الأمر (۱) باعتبار تحققه في نفسه يقال له : شيء، وباعتبار تحققه في الخارج يُقال له : موجود، فهما متساويان ما صدقاً، فكل ما صدق عليه الشيء صدق عليه الموجود وبالعكس، فكلُّ شيء موجود، وكلُّ موجود شيء، والمعدوم ليس بشيء سواء كان ممكناً أو مُمتنعاً ؛ لأن الأشياء قبل وجودها لا ثبوت لها في نفس الأمر، خلافاً للمعتزلة، فالمعدوم عندهم شيء؛ لأن الأشياء قبل وجودها ثابتة في نفسها، إلا أنها مستترة كاستتار الثوب في الصندوق؛ ولذلك يقولون : إن الحقائق ليست بجعل جاعل ولم تتعلق القدرة إلا بظهورها لاستتارها قبل ذلك. وأما أهل السنة فيقولون : إنها بجعل جاعل تعلقت القدرة بوجودها لعدم ثبوتها قبل ذلك، وهذا كله إنما هو في الشيء اصطلاحاً ، وأما لغة فالشيء هو الأمر مطلقاً موجوداً أو معدوماً (٢) . 6 (1) قوله : (فإن الأمر ... إلخ) حاصل عبارة المصنف في شرحه أن الأمر الخارجي باعتبار كونه متميزاً في الخارج عمَّا عداه يقال له : شيء، وباعتبار تقرره خارجاً يقال له : موجود، وعلى هذا فالشيئية هي تميزه في الخارج عمّا عداه ؛ والوجود هو تقرره خارجاً بحيث تصح رؤيته، وهذا بناء على أن الشيء والموجود متغايران مفهوماً، متساويان ما صدقا ؛ لأن مفهوم الشيء ما تميّز في الخارج عما عداه، ومفهوم الموجود ما تقرر في خارج الأعيان . : وقيل : الشيء والموجود مترادفان على معنى واحد؛ وهو ما تقرر في خارج الأعيان . وكلام المصنف صالح للقولين، ولكنه إلى الترادف أقرب وكلام المحشي يميل إلى أنهما متغايران ،مفهوماً، وكان الأنسب على هذا أن يقول : فإن الأمر باعتبار تميزه في الخارج عما عداه يقال له : شيءٌ ليكون موافقاً لكلام المصنف في شرحه انتهى أجهوري (۲) ولخص شيخ الإسلام زكريا الأنصاري هذه المسألة بقوله : الشيء عند أهل السنة الموجود، وعند المعتزلة ما له تحقق ذهناً أو خارجاً ، انظر الحدود الأنيقة والتعريفات الدقيقة (٦٦).
'[The thing is the existent] 122 - And according to us, the thing is the existent and it is established in the external, the existent. His saying: 'And according to us, the thing is the existent' means: according to us, the community of the people of truth from the Ash'aris and others, the thing is the existent. For the matter (1) in terms of its actualization in itself is called 'thing', and in terms of its actualization in the external is called 'existent'. So they are coextensive in extension: everything that is predicated of 'thing' is predicated of 'existent' and vice versa. Thus everything is existent, and everything existent is a thing. And the non-existent is not a thing, whether it is possible or impossible, because things before their existence have no establishment in themselves, contrary to the Mu'tazila. For the non-existent according to them is a thing because things before their existence are established in themselves, but they are concealed like the concealment of a garment in a chest. Therefore they say: realities are not by the making of a maker, and power is only related to their manifestation due to their being concealed before that. As for Ahl al-Sunna, they say: they are by the making of a maker; power is related to their existence because they were not established before that. All this is concerning 'thing' terminologically. Lexically, 'thing' is absolutely anything, existent or non-existent (2). 6 (1) His saying: 'For the matter... etc.' The summary of the author's phrase in his commentary is that the external matter in terms of being distinguished in the external from what is other than it is called 'thing', and in terms of being fixed externally is called 'existent'. On this, thingness (shay'iyya) is its distinction in the external from what is other than it, and existence is its being fixed externally such that it can be seen. This is based on the view that 'thing' and 'existent' are conceptually distinct but coextensive, because the concept of thing is what is distinguished in the external from what is other than it, and the concept of existent is what is fixed in external entities. And it is said: 'thing' and 'existent' are synonymous in one meaning; that is, what is fixed in external entities. The author's statement is suitable for both views, but it is closer to synonymy. The commentator's statement leans towards them being conceptually distinct. It would have been more appropriate on this view to say: 'the matter in terms of its distinction in the external from what is other than it is called thing' so that it agrees with the author's statement in his commentary. Ended, Ajhuri. (2) And Shaykh al-Islam Zakariyya al-Ansari summarized this issue by saying: 'Thing, according to Ahl al-Sunna, is the existent, and according to the Mu'tazila, it is what has mental or external actualization.' See al-Hudud al-Aniqa wa al-Ta'rifat al-Daqiqa (66).
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'thing' (shay') within the Ash'ari theological framework. The commentator begins by stating that according to Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth, i.e., the Ash'aris and others), 'thing' is identical with 'existent' (mawjud). He explains that an entity considered in itself, in terms of its actualization (tahaqquq), is called 'thing', and considered in terms of its actualization in the external world is called 'existent'. Thus 'thing' and 'existent' are coextensive (mutasawiyan ma sadaqan): whenever 'thing' applies, 'existent' applies, and vice versa. Therefore everything that is a thing is existent, and every existent is a thing. The non-existent (ma'dum) is not a thing, whether it is a possible non-existent (mumkin) or an impossible one (mumtani'), because things have no established reality (thubut) in themselves before their existence. This contrasts with the Mu'tazilite view, which holds that non-existent things are 'things' because they have a fixed reality in themselves before existence, merely concealed like a garment in a chest. Consequently, the Mu'tazila believe that realities are not created by a maker, and divine power only brings them forth from concealment. The Sunnis affirm that things are created by a maker, and power is connected to their existence because they had no prior established reality. This is the technical (istilahi) definition; lexically, 'thing' can refer to anything, existent or non-existent. The commentator then includes a footnote from the commentator (likely al-Ajhuri) explaining that according to some, 'thing' and 'existent' are conceptually distinct (mutaghayiran mafhuman) but coextensive; according to others they are synonymous (mutaradifan). The author's statement can accommodate both, though it leans towards synonymy. He also summarizes Zakariyya al-Ansari's view: for Sunnis, 'thing' is the existent; for Mu'tazila, it is what has mental or external actualization.

Key Terms
شيء (shay')
موجود (mawjud)
معدوم (ma'dum)
ثبوت (thubut)
الشيئية (shay'iyya)
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'thing' includes non-existents, such as possible objects before creation.
Correction: In Ash'ari technical usage, 'thing' is limited to what actually exists externally; non-existents (even possibilities) are not things.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'aris as presented in this passage, how is the non-existent (ma'dum) described in the technical sense?
A) It is a thing in the technical sense.
B) It is not a thing in the technical sense.
C) It is a thing only if possible.
D) It is always a thing because it has mental reality.

Key Takeaway
In Ash'ari theology, 'thing' is equivalent to 'existent'; non-existents are not things, contrary to the Mu'tazilite view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is a 'thing' only because Allah willed it and brought it from nothing, does your daily life reflect that same total dependence on Him? In what area are you living as if you have an independent reality apart from His creation?
?
When you think about your future—what you will achieve, own, or become—do you treat those possibilities as 'things' already fixed, or do you acknowledge they are pure non-existents until Allah creates them? How would that shift your reliance on Him vs. your own planning?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed non-existents have a reality hidden like a garment in a chest. Is there any 'garment' in your heart—a hidden reality, an assumption, a view of yourself—that you treat as already fixed, independent of Allah's creative act? What would it take to surrender that to the One who alone gives existence?
