Existence Identical to Essence and the Created Atom
Introduces two Ash'ari doctrines: existence=essence and created atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari positions that existence is identical to essence and that the indivisible atom is created, and how these views relate to debates with al-Razi and the theory of states.

Core Idea
In Ash'ari thought, existence is not separate from essence; the indivisible atom is created and real.

This lesson introduces two foundational Ash'ari doctrines: the identity of existence with essence, and the creation of the indivisible atom. These principles are central to Ash'ari metaphysics and cosmology, and they are clarified here through a comparison with al-Razi's view and a discussion of the theory of states (al-ahwal).

Guided Path
1

The passage includes a long footnote; focus on the main text first.

The passage states two positions: (1) existence is identical to essence, and (2) the indivisible atom is created. Find these statements in the text.
The atom part is brief: 'والجوهر الفرد حادث عندنا لا ينكر'.
3
Understand the debate with al-Razi
Notice how the Ash'ari position is contrasted with al-Razi's view that existence is a 'state' (al-hal). The commentator explains why al-Ash'ari holds that existence is not an extra attribute.
The footnote mentions al-Baqillani and al-Juwayni on states.

The atom is affirmed as created. Consider why this matters: it supports Ash'ari atomism and the contingency of the world.

Existence is identical to the existent and the indivisible atom. 123 - The existence of a thing is its essence, and the indivisible atom is created; with us it is not denied. His saying: (The existence of a thing is its essence) means: The existence of something among existents is identical to its reality, as al-Ash'ari and those who followed him said. And Imam al-Razi said: The existence of a thing is not identical to its reality, and he interpreted it as the state (al-hal) that is established for the essence as long as the essence lasts, and this state is not caused by a cause. Then some of them kept the expression... (1) His saying: (He means that the fixed etc.) This interpretation is based on the apparent meaning of the author's words, that his intent is to define the existent as what is fixed externally. But this is not the author's intended meaning at all. Rather, his intent is that the realities which we conceive and name with names, such as the referent of 'human', the referent of 'animal', the referent of 'earth' and 'sky', are fixed in reality, as a refutation of the Sophists in their saying that they are illusions with no reality. So by 'existent' he meant those realities. This is taken from the expression of Shaykh Abd al-Salam. End of al-Ujhuri. (2) That is, al-ʿAqa'id al-Nasafiyya. (3) Al-Mallawi said in his gloss on the commentary on al-Sanusiyya: (The existence of every thing is its essence, meaning that there is no existential attribute in the external that is existence, rather existence is a mental consideration that is equivalent to actuality). So we conceive the existence of the essence without knowledge and power, for example, but we do not conceive the essence without the attribute of existence. (4) The relied-upon view is what the majority agreed upon in negating the state (al-hal). Among those who affirmed states were Abu Bakr al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni; they said: They are fixed in themselves, not existent, so they can be seen, and not non-existent, but they are =
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage states two doctrinal positions: (1) the existence of a thing is identical to its essence (not an additional attribute), and (2) the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد) is created. The commentator explains the first point by quoting al-Ash'ari and his followers, who hold that the existence of something is exactly its reality. This opposes Imam al-Razi, who asserts that existence is a 'state' (الحال) that inheres in the essence and is not caused. The footnote clarifies that for al-Ash'ari, existence is not an extra attribute in the external world; it is a mental consideration (أمر اعتباري) equivalent to actuality. We can conceive an essence without some attributes (like knowledge), but we cannot conceive an essence without existence. The passage also touches on the theory of 'states' (الأحوال) held by al-Baqillani and al-Juwayni, who maintain that states are fixed in themselves, neither existent nor non-existent, but the majority of the Ash'aris reject this view. The indivisible atom is affirmed as created, which is the Ash'ari position on atomism. The commentary also includes a remark that the earlier definition of 'existent' as 'fixed externally' is not the author's intended meaning; rather, it is a refutation of the Sophists, affirming the reality of concepts like 'human', 'animal', etc. The text is cut off at the end, but the core teaching is clear: existence is identical to essence, and atoms are created.

Al-Ash'ari teaches that existence is the essence itself and that the indivisible atom is created, refuting the idea that existence is an added attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Denial of Consensus and Permission of Forbidden Acts
Commentator defines denial of definitive consensus and its ruling, including the case of permitting forbidden acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rulings on denying definitive consensus and permitting a forbidden act, and the condition that the matter must be known by necessity from the religion for disbelief to apply.

Core Idea
Denial of consensus leads to disbelief only if the matter is known by necessity from the religion.

This lesson examines two related issues from the commentary: the denial of a definitive consensus and the permission of a unanimously forbidden act. The commentator clarifies the relationship between these two and highlights a key condition—that the matter must be known by necessity from the religion for disbelief to apply. Understanding this distinction is crucial for accurately applying takfir based on consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage from Sanusi's commentary once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: the commentator first defines denial of consensus, then permits a forbidden act.
The passage is a continuous explanation of two cases.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage addresses two scenarios: (1) denying a definitive consensus (nafy mujma' qat'i), and (2) believing a forbidden act is permissible (istibahat muharram). Note how the commentator connects them.
The second case is said to be included in the first.
3
Understand the condition 'known by necessity from the religion'
The commentator states that the preponderant view is that disbelief only follows if the matter is 'known by necessity from the religion' (ma'lum min al-din bi'l-darura). This is a key condition.
This condition distinguishes definitive consensus from other types.

Compare the poet's apparent wording with the preponderant view
The poet's wording suggests any denial of consensus is disbelief, but the commentator argues this is weak. The correct view is more restricted.
This shows the importance of scholarly interpretation.
5

The commentator mentions a Maturidi view that distinguishes between inherent and incidental prohibition. Consider how this relates to the main ruling.
This adds nuance to the discussion of istibaha.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وَمِثْلُ هَذَا مَنْ نَفَى لِمُجْمَعِ) أي: ومثلُ مَنْ جحد أمراً معلوماً من الدين بالضرورة، مَنْ نفى حُكماً مُجمَعاً عليه إجماعاً قطعيًّا، وهو ما اتفق المعتبرون على كونه إجماعاً، بخلاف الإجماع السكوتي ؛ فإنه ظني لا قطعي، وظاهر كلام الناظم أن من نفى مُجمَعاً عليه يكفر، وإن لم يكن معلوماً من الدين بالضرورة؛ كاستحقاق بنت الابن السدس مع بنت الصلب، وهو ضعيف، وإن جزم به الناظم، والراجح أنه لا يكفر من نفى المجمع عليه إلا إذا كان معلوماً من الدين بالضرورة. وقوله : (أَوِ اسْتَبَاحَ كالزِّنَا ) أي : أو اعتقد إباحة مُحرَّم مُجمع عليه، معلوم من الدين بالضرورة، ولو صغيرة؛ سواء كان تحريمه لعينه كالزنا وشرب الخمر أو لعارض كصوم يوم العيد، فإن تحريمه لعارض، وهو الإعراض عن ضيافة الله تعالى، خلافاً لبعض الماتريدية، حيث قال: من اعتقد حِلَّ مُحرَّم فإن كان تحريمه لعارض فلا يكفر، ولا يخفى أنه يلزم من استباحة المحرم المجمع عليه المعلوم من الدين بالضرورة أنه نفى مجمعاً عليه، فهو داخل فيما قبله، فما ذكره المصنف صريحاً إلا تبعاً للقوم، وتنصيصاً على أعيان المسائل وزيادة في الإيضاح. وقوله : (فَلتَسْمَع) تكملة.
His saying: 'And similar to this is one who denies a [matter of] consensus or permits [forbidden] like zina, so listen.' Meaning: And similar to one who denies a matter known by necessity from the religion is one who denies a ruling upon which there is a definitive consensus, which is what the reliable scholars have agreed upon as consensus, unlike silent consensus, for it is speculative not definitive. The apparent wording of the poet indicates that the one who denies a consensus-based [ruling] disbelieves, even if it is not known by necessity from the religion, like the entitlement of the granddaughter to one-sixth along with the daughter, but this is weak, even if the poet asserted it. The preponderant view is that one who denies a consensus-based [ruling] does not disbelieve unless it is known by necessity from the religion. And his saying: 'or permits [forbidden] like zina' means: or believes permissible a forbidden [act] upon which there is consensus, known by necessity from the religion, even if a minor sin, whether its prohibition is for its essence like zina and drinking wine, or due to an incidental reason like fasting on the day of Eid, for its prohibition is due to an incidental reason, which is turning away from the hospitality of God Almighty, contrary to some Maturidis, who said: whoever believes a forbidden [act] permissible, if its prohibition is for its essence, he disbelieves, otherwise not. And it is not hidden that permitting a forbidden act upon which there is consensus known by necessity from the religion entails denial of consensus, so it is included in what preceded. Thus the author mentioned it explicitly only following the scholars and specifying particular issues and increasing clarification. And his saying: 'so listen' is a completion.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on verse 129 of the poem, addressing the rulings on denying a consensus (mujma') and permitting a forbidden act that is unanimously forbidden. The commentator begins by defining what is meant by 'denying a consensus': it is denying a ruling that is definitively agreed upon by the reliable scholars, as opposed to silent consensus which is only speculative. He notes that the poet's wording suggests that denying any consensus-based ruling results in disbelief, even if the ruling is not known by necessity from the religion. However, the commentator argues that this view is weak; the preponderant opinion is that disbelief only follows if the matter is known by necessity (ma‘lum min al-din bi’l-darura). He then explains the second part of the verse, 'or permits like zina': this refers to someone who believes a forbidden act is permissible, provided the act is definitively forbidden by consensus and known by necessity. This applies even to minor sins, and whether the prohibition is inherent (as with zina) or incidental (like fasting on Eid). The commentator mentions a contrary view from some Maturidis, who distinguish based on whether the prohibition is inherent or incidental. He concludes that the example is included for clarification, though it logically falls under the first part. The final phrase 'so listen' is merely a poetic completion.

معلوم من الدين بالضرورة

The Rulings on Argumentation and Disputation
The author defines argumentation and disputation and states their rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of al-mira' and al-jadal, and how intention determines their moral ruling.

Core Idea
The moral value of debate depends on intention, not the act itself.

This lesson introduces two key terms in Islamic theological discourse: argumentation (al-mira') and disputation (al-jadal). The author clarifies their linguistic and technical definitions, and more importantly, establishes that their permissibility hinges on the intention behind them. Whether they are blameworthy or praiseworthy depends entirely on the purpose: to belittle others or to seek truth. The passage also provides prophetic traditions and an example from Imam al-Shafi'i to illustrate the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the content. Notice the structure: definitions first, then rulings.
2
Understand the definitions
Identify the linguistic and technical definitions of al-mira' and al-jadal. Note how al-jadal is initially distinguished from al-mira' but later considered synonymous.
3
Recognize the role of intention
Focus on the key point: both terms are blameworthy when done to belittle or corrupt, but praiseworthy when done to establish truth. This is the central teaching.
The hadith and Imam al-Shafi'i's statement support this.
4
Reflect on application
Consider how this principle applies to your own discussions and debates. Ask yourself what your intention is when you argue with others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَكَالمِرَاء) هو لغةٌ : الاستخراج يقال مارى فلان فلاناً، إذا استخرج ما عنده، وعر فاً : منازعة الغير فيما يدعي صوابه ، ومحل كونه مذموماً إذا كان لتحقير غيرك، وإظهار مزيتك عليه. وقد ورد في الحديث: «هلك المتنطعونَ ... ثلاثاً (۱)؛ أي: المتعمقون في البحث . وأخرج الطبراني عن ثوبان مرفوعاً: سيكون في أمتي أقوامٌ يُغَلِّطُون فقهاءَهم بعُضَل المسائل - بضم العين وفتح الضاد؛ أي صعابها - أولئك شرارُ أمتي (٢) ، وأما إذا كان لإحقاق حق، وإبطال باطل ؛ أي: لإظهار حقية الحق، وإظهار بطلان الباطل، فممدوح شرعاً ولو من ولد لوالده؛ فيكون عقوقاً محموداً. قوله : (وَالجَدَلْ) بسكون آخره للوزن : وهو دفعُ الشخص خصمه عن إفساد قوله بحجة قاصداً به تصحیح كلامه، كذا عرفه الشارح وعليه فالفرق بينه وبين المراء : أن الجدال يكون من قبل صاحب القول يدفع عن قوله الإفساد والمراة يكون من قبل الخصم ؛ وإذا حققت النظر وجدتهما بمعنى واحد وحينئذ فنقول في تعريفهما : مقابلة الحجة بالحجة. ومحل حرمته إذا كان لإفساد قول الغير، بخلاف ما إذا كان لإحقاق حق أو إبطال باطل. قال الإمام الشافعي: ما ذاكرْتُ أحداً وقصدت إفحامه، وإنما أذاكره لإظهار الحقِّ، من حيث هو حق .
His statement: 'and argumentation (al-mira')' — linguistically it is extraction; one says 'so-and-so argued with so-and-so' when he extracts what he has. Conventionally: disputing with another regarding what he claims to be correct. Its being blameworthy occurs when it is to belittle the other and show one's superiority over him. It has come in the hadith: 'Perished are the nitpickers...' three times (1); meaning those who go deep in research. And al-Tabarani narrated from Thawban as a marfu' hadith: 'There will be in my nation people who will cause their jurists to err by difficult issues — with damm of 'ayn and fath of dad, i.e., their difficulties — those are the worst of my nation.' (2) But if it is for the purpose of establishing truth and refuting falsehood — i.e., to manifest the truth of the truth and manifest the falsehood of falsehood — then it is praiseworthy by law, even if from a child to his parent; so it is commendable disobedience. His statement: 'and disputation (al-jadal)' — with sukun of its last letter for meter — and it is: a person repelling his opponent from corrupting his statement by an argument, intending thereby to correct his speech. Thus defined by the commentator. Accordingly, the difference between it and argumentation is that disputation comes from the owner of the statement who repels corruption from his statement, while argumentation comes from the opponent. And if you investigate the matter, you will find them to be one in meaning. Then we say in their definition: confronting evidence with evidence. Its being forbidden occurs when it is for corrupting the statement of another, contrary to when it is for establishing truth or refuting falsehood. Imam al-Shafi'i said: 'I never debated anyone intending to silence him; rather I debate him to manifest the truth, simply because it is the truth.'
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage provides definitions and rulings for two related concepts: al-mira' (argumentation) and al-jadal (disputation). The author first defines al-mira' linguistically as 'extraction' and conventionally as 'disputing another's claim of correctness.' He then states that it is blameworthy when done to belittle someone or show superiority, citing two hadiths that condemn excessive argumentation and those who cause difficulty for scholars. However, he adds that if argumentation aims at establishing truth and refuting falsehood, it is praiseworthy, even if it involves a child disobeying a parent (commendable disobedience). Then he defines al-jadal as repelling an opponent's attempt to corrupt one's statement, noting that the commentator distinguishes it from al-mira' based on who initiates it. But upon scrutiny, the author considers them essentially the same, defining both as 'confronting evidence with evidence.' The ruling parallels that of al-mira': forbidden if intended to corrupt another's statement, praiseworthy if for truth. He concludes with Imam al-Shafi'i's statement that his debates were always aimed at manifesting truth, not silencing opponents. The lesson thus emphasizes intention as the determinant of moral value.

Key Terms
المراء
الجدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All forms of argumentation and debate are prohibited in Islam.
Correction: The passage clarifies that argumentation is only blameworthy when done to belittle others or corrupt truth; it is praiseworthy when done to establish truth and refute falsehood.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is argumentation (al-mira') considered praiseworthy?
A) When it belittles the opponent
B) When it is for establishing truth and refuting falsehood
C) When it is done from a child to a parent
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The moral status of debate in Islam depends on the intention: if for truth, it is praiseworthy; if for showing superiority or corrupting truth, it is blameworthy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Recall the last time you won an argument. Did you feel a sense of victory over the other person? In that moment, were you seeking truth or self-affirmation? What would it mean to you to repent from arguing for the sake of ego?
?
The author says that a child debating his parent for truth is 'commendable disobedience'. Are you willing to accept truth from someone younger, less educated, or lower in status than you? What pride prevents you from listening to truth regardless of the source?
?
Imam al-Shafi'i never debated to silence anyone. Think of a topic you feel strongly about. If you were to debate it today, could you sincerely say your only aim is to manifest the truth, even if it means you are proven wrong? What would change in your life if you adopted this attitude in every discussion?

Jadal and Mira': Definitions, Distinction, and Ruling
Defines jadal and mira', distinguishes them, and states their ruling based on intent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of jadal and mira', the subtle distinction between them, and that their ruling depends on intention.

Core Idea
Argumentation is judged by its intent: either to corrupt truth or to establish it.

This lesson unpacks two key terms in Islamic dialectic: jadal and mira'. The author defines them, shows they are essentially the same act, and then clarifies when such argumentation is permissible and when it is forbidden. The lesson also includes a profound statement from Imam al-Shafi'i about his own method of debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Let the author's definitions sink in. Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the overall meaning.
2
Distinguish jadal from mira'
Note the shift: jadal comes from the speaker defending his statement from corruption; mira' comes from the opponent attacking.
Both involve arguments, but the initiator differs.
3
See the deeper unity
The author says they are one in meaning: both are confronting an argument with an argument.
4
Understand the ruling
The key is intention. Forbidden if aimed at corrupting truth; permissible if aimed at establishing truth or refuting falsehood.
5
Reflect on the Shafi'i quote
Imam al-Shafi'i states he never debated to defeat a person, only to manifest truth. This exemplifies the proper intention.
This is a practical model for students.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَالجَدَلْ) بسكون آخره للوزن : وهو دفعُ الشخص خصمه عن إفساد قوله بحجة قاصداً به تصحیح كلامه، كذا عرفه الشارح وعليه فالفرق بينه وبين المراء : أن الجدال يكون من قبل صاحب القول يدفع عن قوله الإفساد والمراء يكون من قبل الخصم ؛ وإذا حققت النظر وجدتهما بمعنى واحد وحينئذ فنقول في تعريفهما : مقابلة الحجة بالحجة. ومحل حرمته إذا كان لإفساد قول الغير، بخلاف ما إذا كان لإحقاق حق أو إبطال باطل. قال الإمام الشافعي: ما ذاكرْتُ أحداً وقصدت إفحامه، وإنما أذاكره لإظهار الحقِّ، من حيث هو حق .
His statement: (وَالجَدَلْ) with its final letter silent for meter: it is a person repelling his opponent from corrupting his statement with an argument intending thereby to correct his speech. This is how the commentator defined it, and accordingly the difference between it and al-mira' is that al-jadal comes from the owner of the statement repelling corruption of his statement, and al-mira' comes from the opponent. And if you investigate the matter, you find them to be one in meaning. Then we say in their definition: confronting an argument with an argument. The place of its prohibition is when it is for corrupting the statement of another, contrary to when it is for establishing truth or invalidating falsehood. Al-Imam al-Shafi'i said: I never debated anyone intending to defeat him; rather I debate to manifest truth, insofar as it is truth.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-jadal' (argumentation) and distinguishes it from 'al-mira'' (disputation). The author starts by explaining the word 'al-jadal' in the poem, noting its pronunciation. He then gives the definition as per the commentator: it is a person repelling his opponent from corrupting his statement by means of an argument, with the intention of correcting his own statement. The key distinction is that al-jadal originates from the owner of the statement defending it, while al-mira' originates from the opponent attacking it. However, upon deeper investigation, they are essentially the same: both are a confrontation of argument with argument. The ruling depends on intention: if one engages in jadal to corrupt another's truth, it is forbidden; but if it is to establish truth or refute falsehood, it is permissible. The passage ends with a quote from Imam al-Shafi'i, who states that he never debated with the intention of defeating someone, but only to manifest truth.

Key Terms
الجدل
المراء
مقابلة الحجة بالحجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think all forms of jadal (argumentation) are forbidden in Islam.
Correction: Jadal is only forbidden when done with the intention of corrupting truth; it is permissible—even praiseworthy—when done to establish truth or refute falsehood.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is jadal (argumentation) prohibited?
A) Always, regardless of intention.
B) When it is for establishing truth.
C) When it is for corrupting another's statement (i.e., to defend falsehood or attack truth).
D) When it is between two scholars.

Key Takeaway
Jadal is judged by intention: forbidden when aimed at corrupting truth, permissible when aimed at establishing truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in discussions about religion, do you examine your intention: are you seeking truth or trying to prove yourself right? What would change if you truly intended only to manifest truth, as Imam al-Shafi'i did?
?
Think of a recent argument about faith where you felt defensive. Was your goal to correct your own statement (jadal) or to attack the other's (mira')? How can you shift your approach to align with the permissible purpose of establishing truth?
?
If you honestly assessed your debates, would you find any that were for 'ifsād' (corrupting truth) rather than 'iḥqāq' (establishing truth)? What step can you take today to repent from that and adopt Shafi'i's model?

Who Is 'The Best of Creation'?
Commentator explains the phrase 'as was the best of creation' and expands its reference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple interpretations of 'the best of creation' in the poem and how the commentary expands the reference to include various righteous figures.

Core Idea
The best of creation includes not only the Prophet but all who embody goodness.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the phrase 'as was the best of creation' from the poem. He explores possible grammatical functions of the kaf and identifies who exactly is meant by 'the best of creation.' The discussion moves from a specific reference to the Prophet to a broader inclusive understanding that encompasses prophets, scholars, saints, and others. This lesson will guide you through the exegetical reasoning and the preferred interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a sense of the flow. Notice how the commentator begins by explaining the kaf.
2
Identify the interpretations
Look for the different possibilities presented: the kaf as similitude or as ba; 'best of creation' as the Prophet, then as prophets, then as all who have goodness.
The commentator uses 'يُحتمل' to introduce possibilities.
3
Understand the preferred view
The commentator states that the preferred view (الأولى) is that it includes everyone for whom goodness is established, relative to others. This is inclusive of many righteous categories.
Note the list: prophets, scholars, martyrs, saints, ascetics, worshippers.
4
Reflect on the practical implication
The passage ends with a saying about learning from a spiritual master being more beneficial. Consider how this connects to the inclusive interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : كَمَا كَانَ خِيارُ الخَلْقِ أي كن متصفاً بأخلاق مثل الأخلاق التي كان عليها خيار الخلق، فالكاف للتمثيل والتشبيه، ويحتمل أن تكون بمعنى الباء؛ أي: كُنْ متصفاً بالأخلاق التي كان عليها خيار الخلق، والمراد من خيار الخلق نبينا ؛ لأنه ما تفرق في غيره من الخصال الحميدة، فهو الخيار المطلق. ويحتمل أن المراد به الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام؛ لأنهم خيار الخلق. جمع والأولى أن يُراد به كل من ثبتت له الخيرية، ولو بالنسبة لمن دونه، فيشمله ، ويشمل الأنبياء والعلماء والشهداء والأولياء والزهاد والعباد، ويكون الكلامُ مُوزّعاً باعتبار الأشخاص وأنواع الخير ; فمن الناس من له قدرة على صورة مجاهدته ، ومنهم من له قدرة على صورة مجاهدة غيره من الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام، ومنهم له قدرة على مجاهدة العلماء، وهلم جرا ; وإذا كانت المجاهدة على يد شيخ من العارفين كانت أنفع لقولهم : (حال) رجل في ألف رجلٍ أنفعُ من وَغظ ألفِ رجلٍ من
His statement: as was the best of creation: i.e., be characterized with morals like the morals that the best of creation had. So the kaf is for representation and similitude, and it is possible that it is in the sense of the ba (by), i.e., be characterized with the morals that the best of creation had. And what is meant by 'best of creation' is our Prophet, because good qualities are not dispersed in other than him, so he is the absolute best. And it is possible that what is meant is the prophets, peace be upon them, because they are the best of creation. Summary: and the preferred view is that it means everyone for whom goodness is established, even relative to those below him. Thus it includes him (the Prophet) and includes the prophets, scholars, martyrs, saints, ascetics, and worshippers. And the statement is distributed according to persons and types of good. So among people are those who have capacity for the form of his (the Prophet's) struggle, and among them are those who have capacity for the form of the struggle of others from the prophets, peace be upon them, and among them are those who have capacity for the struggle of the scholars, and so on. And if the struggle is under the hand of a master of the knowers, it is more beneficial, because of their statement: The state of one man among a thousand men is more beneficial than the exhortation of a thousand men of...
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage provides an exegetical commentary on the line 'wa kun kama kana khiyar al-khalq' (and be as was the best of creation). The commentator explains the function of the kaf (like): it is for similitude (tashbih) and also possible in the sense of the ba (by), meaning 'be characterized with the morals of the best of creation.' The initial interpretation identifies 'the best of creation' as the Prophet Muhammad, as he possesses all good qualities absolutely. However, the commentator presents another possibility: it could refer to all prophets, as they are the best of creation. Then a preferred view is given: it includes everyone for whom goodness is established, even relative to those below him. This encompasses prophets, scholars, martyrs, saints, ascetics, and worshippers. The phrase is thus distributed: different people have different capacities to emulate the struggle (mujahada) of the Prophet, other prophets, or scholars. The passage concludes with a saying that learning from a spiritual master (shaykh of the knowers) is more beneficial than general exhortation, exemplified by a man's state influencing a thousand men more than words.

Key Terms
خيار الخلق
الكاف للتمثيل
الخيار المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think 'the best of creation' refers only to the Prophet Muhammad.
Correction: The preferred interpretation in this commentary is that it includes everyone for whom goodness is established, such as prophets, scholars, saints, etc.

Checkpoint
What is the preferred interpretation of 'the best of creation' in this commentary?
A) Only the Prophet Muhammad.
B) Only the prophets.
C) Everyone who has goodness, including prophets, scholars, and saints.
D) Only the companions of the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
The commentary expands the reference of 'the best of creation' to include all who embody goodness, making the emulation of their moral struggles accessible to everyone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says the state of one righteous man among a thousand is more beneficial than the exhortation of a thousand. Think of someone in your life—a teacher, a friend, an elder—whose silent presence inspires you more than any words. Have you been truly learning from their state, or only from their words? What is one habit of theirs you will consciously adopt this week?
?
The commentary expands 'the best of creation' to include everyone for whom goodness is established, even if only relative to those below them. Look at yourself: who is 'below' you in some aspect of goodness? Are you a 'most of creation' to them? If you saw yourself as a role model, what would you change today?
?
You are called to be 'as was the best of creation'—not as a copy, but according to your capacity. Where in your life are you excusing yourself from striving because you think the ideal is too high? Name one specific area where you know you can improve, and commit to the first step before you close this lesson.

Defining Hawa and Distinguishing It from Hawa'
The commentator defines hawa and hawa' with linguistic and poetic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and moral definition of hawa (desire), its typical usage in sacred texts, and how it differs from hawa' (air) both in meaning and consequence.

Core Idea
Hawa (desire) and hawa' (air) are distinct but both can lead to ruin.

This lesson clarifies two important terms that often get confused due to similar pronunciation: hawa and hawa'. While hawa refers to the soul's inclination toward desires—often leading away from truth—hawa' simply means air or wind. The commentator provides definitions, Quranic and hadith evidence, and a poetic illustration to highlight their distinct meanings and moral implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and etymology of hawa
Start by reading the first part of the passage where hawa is defined as the inclination of the soul to what it desires, even to destruction, and note the etymology linking it to 'falling into the Fire'.
The term 'hawa' is from the root h-w-y, meaning to fall or descend.
2
See the examples of hawa in sacred texts
Read the Quranic and hadith examples that show hawa used both for inclination contrary to truth and, in a rare case, for inclination to truth. Understand that the default usage is negative.
The hadith from Aisha shows that even the Prophet's desire is fulfilled by Allah, indicating a positive use.
3
Contrast with hawa' and the poem
Read the definition of hawa' (air) and then the poem that combines both words. The poet uses 'hawa'' (long) and 'hawa' (short) to show how attachment to comfort (air) and following desire (hawa) lead to disappointment and death.
The poem illustrates the destructive consequences of both, though hawa' here represents love of ease.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَالهَوَى) أي : وأرجو الله في الخلاص من الهوى، وهو بالقصر : ميل النفس إلى مرغوبها ، ولو كان فيه هلاكُها، وإذا أُطلق انصرف إلى الميل إلى خلافِ الحقِّ غالباً، نحو: ﴿وَلَا نَتَّبِعِ الْهَوَى [ص: ٢٦]، وقد يُستعمل في الميل للحقِّ، كما في قول السيدة عائشة رضي الله تعالى عنها : لا أرى ربَّك إلا يُسارع في هواك (١) ، تخاطبه الله لما نزل قوله تعالى : وتُرْجي مَن نَشَاءُ ..) [الأحزاب: ٥١] الآية . وسُمِّي الأول هوى؛ لأنه يهوي بصاحبه إلى النار. وأما الهواء - بالمد - فهو ما بين السماء والأرض مِنَ الريح الذي تسير به قال الشاعر : [من الكامل] جُمِعَ الهَوَاهُ مَعَ الهَوَى فِي أَضْلُعِي فَتَكَا مَلَتْ فِي مُهْجَتِيْ نَارَانِ فَقَصُرْتُ بِالمَمَدُودِ عَنْ نَيْلِ المُنَى وَدَرَجْتُ بِالمَقْصُوْرِ فِي أَكْفَانِي و معنى كلامه أنه اجتمع فيه الممدود والمقصور، فبالممدود قصر عن نيل مُناه؛ لكونه ألف الريح ،اللينة، وأحب الراحة ففاته خير كثير، وبالمقصور مات ودَرَج في أكفانه ؛ لأنه تَبعَ هوى نفسه، فتمكّن منه العِشْقُ فقتله.
His saying: (and hawa) i.e., I hope for Allah to be saved from hawa, and it (with qasr) is the inclination of the soul to what it desires, even if its destruction is in it. When it is used absolutely, it usually turns to inclination contrary to truth, as in: 'And do not follow hawa' [Sad: 26]. And it may be used for inclination to truth, as in the saying of Lady Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her: 'I do not see your Lord except that He hastens to fulfill your hawa.' She addressed Allah when the verse was revealed: 'You can postpone whom you wish...' [al-Ahzab: 51]. The first is called hawa because it causes its companion to fall into the Fire. As for al-hawa' (with madd), it is the wind between heaven and earth by which ships sail. The poet said: [al-kamil] 'Hawa' and hawa have gathered in my ribs, thus two fires have become complete in my lifeblood. So with the long one I fell short of attaining hopes, and with the short one I passed away and was wrapped in my shrouds.' And the meaning of his speech is that both the long and the short were combined in him; with the long he fell short of attaining his hope because he loved soft wind and loved comfort, so much good missed him; and with the short he died and was wrapped in his shrouds because he followed his own hawa, so love overcame him and killed him.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'hawa' (desire) and distinguishes it from 'hawa'' (air). The commentator explains that 'hawa' (with qasr, short vowel) refers to the soul's inclination toward what it desires, even if that leads to destruction. In its unqualified use, it usually implies inclination contrary to the truth, as in the Quranic verse 'And do not follow hawa'. However, it can also be used for inclination to the truth, as shown in the hadith where Aisha says that Allah hastens to fulfill the Prophet's desire. The term 'hawa' is so called because it causes its follower to fall (yahwi) into the Fire. Then the commentator contrasts it with 'al-hawa'' (with madd, long vowel), which means the wind between heaven and earth that sails ships. A poetic evidence is quoted in which a poet speaks of both being combined in him: the long one (hawa') causes him to fall short of his hopes, and the short one (hawa) leads to his death and shrouding. The poem illustrates the destructive effect of desire and the softness of wind that leads to laziness. The explanation of the poem makes clear the moral: trusting desire leads to ruin.

Key Terms
الهوى
الهواء
القصر
المد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That hawa and hawa' mean the same thing because they sound similar.
Correction: Hawa (short) means desire, while hawa' (long) means air. They are distinct terms with different roots and meanings, as the commentator clearly distinguishes with linguistic and poetic evidence.

Checkpoint
What is the primary meaning of 'hawa' (with qasr) according to the passage?
A) The air between heaven and earth
B) The soul's inclination to what it desires
C) A type of wind
D) A poetic meter

Key Takeaway
Hawa (desire) is the soul's inclination to what it wishes, usually leading away from truth, while hawa' (air) is the wind; both can cause ruin if indulged excessively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of the 'soft wind' (hawa') in your life—a comfort or ease you cling to. What good have you missed out on because you prioritized that comfort over obedience to Allah? Will you sacrifice that comfort tomorrow for something that pleases Him?
?
When was the last time you followed your hawa (desire) knowing it was against the truth? What did that cost you spiritually? How will you arm yourself against a similar situation tonight?
?
The poet died because his hawa consumed him. Which desire is currently consuming you—pride, lust, greed, or something else? Name it now, and commit to one concrete step to weaken its hold on you before you sleep.

Meanings of the Prophet's Family, Progeny, and Followers
Al-Bijuri defines three terms and argues for the universality of the prayer's recipients.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meanings of the terms for the Prophet's family, progeny, and followers as used in the prayer supplication, and why the author emphasizes generality over exclusivity.

Core Idea
The prayer for the Prophet includes all who follow his path, encompassing believers past and future.

In this passage, al-Bijuri unpacks three key phrases from the poem—'his family', 'his progeny', and 'a follower of his path'. He clarifies that the prayer covers not only the immediate household but every believer who follows the Prophet's way, even if sinful. He then addresses a potential misunderstanding about the phrase 'from his nation', ensuring that its scope is universal, not limited to a specific era.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument structure come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Bijuri is commenting on three specific phrases from the poem.
2
Identify the three terms and their definitions
Find the definitions for Āl, 'Itrah, and Tābi' li-Nahjihi. Note the alternative opinions for 'Itrah and the inclusive scope of Tābi'.
The author includes even sinful believers in the category of followers.
3
Examine the discussion on 'min Ummatihi'
Pay attention to the author's clarification that 'from his nation' is descriptive, not restrictive. He anticipates an objection about Jesus and answers it.
The objection is that someone could follow the law without being from the nation, but the author argues that Jesus will be from his nation at his descent.
4
Reflect on the purpose of generality
Consider why the author emphasises that the supplication includes all followers. How does this relate to the universality of the Prophet's mission?
The generality makes the supplication more excellent.

قوله : (وَآلِهِ) أي : والصلاة والسلام الدائم على آله، وقد تقدم الكلام على الآل في أول هذه الكتابة (١). وقوله : (وَعِتْرَتِة) بالمثناة الفوقية ؛ وهم أهل بيته (۲) ، وقيل : زوجاته، وقيل : نَسْلُه ورَهْطُهُ الأَدْنَون. وقوله : (وَتَابِعِ لِنَهْجِه) أي : وكل مُتَّبع الطريقته ، ولو في الإيمان فقط، فدخل عصاة المؤمنين والقصد بهذا التعميمُ في الدعاء؛ لأنه أفضل. وقوله : (مِنْ أُمَّتِه) أي : أمة إجابته ، وهذا القيد لبيان الواقع لا للاحتراز عن المتبع الطريقته وليس من أمته ؛ لأن المُتَّبعَ لشريعته لا يكون إلا من أمته لعموم بعثته، لا يُقال : قد يكون المُتَّبِعُ لشريعته الله من غير أمته ؛ كما في سيدنا عيسى حين ينزل آخر الزمان؛ لأنَّا نقول : هو حينئذ من أمته وفائدة القيد المذكور التنصيص : . على العموم، لئلا يتوهم إرادة خصوص القرون الثلاثة، نظير ما قالوه في قوله تعالى : هو ومَا مِن دَابَهُ فِي الْأَرْضِ وَلَا طَارٍ يَطِيرُ بِجَنَاحَيْهِ إِلَّا أُمَمٌ أَمْثَالُكُمْ مَا فَرَّطْنَا فِي الْكِتَابِ مِن شَيْءٍ [الأنعام: ۳۸] كما أفاده السعد، والله أعلم. وهذا آخر ما يسره الله تعالى من غير حشوِ ولا تعقيد على جوهرة التوحيد»، والله أسألُ، وبنبيه أتوسل أن يجعلَ هذه الكتابة خالصة لوجهه الكريم، وأن ينفع بها النفع العميم، والمرجو من صاحب العقل السليم والخلق القويم أن يُقِيلَ عثراتي، ويَسْتُرَ هفواتي، ولا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله العلي العظيم، وصلى الله وسلَّم وشرَّفَ وكَرَّمَ على النبي الرؤوف الرحيم وعلى آله وصحبه أجمعين وسلامٌ على المرسلين، والحمد الله رب العالمين.
His saying: 'and his family (Ālihī)' means: and eternal prayer and peace upon his family, and the discussion of the family has preceded at the beginning of this writing (1). And his saying: 'and his progeny ('Itratihi)' with the above-dotted letter (tā') — they are his household (2), and it is said: his wives, and it is said: his offspring and his closest kin. And his saying: 'and a follower of his path (Tābi‘ li-Nahjihi)' means: and every follower of his way, even if only in faith, so includes sinful believers, and the intent by this is generality in supplication; because it is better. And his saying: 'from his nation (min Ummatihi)' means: the nation of his response, and this restriction is to indicate reality, not to exclude the follower of his path who is not from his nation; because the follower of his law cannot be except from his nation due to the universality of his mission. It cannot be said: there may be a follower of his law who is not from his nation, as in our master Jesus when he descends at the end of time; for we say: at that time he is from his nation. And the benefit of the mentioned restriction is specification upon generality, lest one imagine intending specifically the three centuries, analogous to what they said in His statement: 'And there is no creature on earth nor a bird flying with its wings except that they are nations like you. We have not neglected in the Book anything' (al-An‘ām 6:38) as al-Sa‘d said. And Allah knows best. And this is the end of what Allah facilitated without redundancy or complexity upon Jawharat al-Tawhid. And I ask Allah, and through His prophet I seek intercession, that He make this writing purely for His noble face, and that He benefit with it abundant benefit. And what is hoped from the possessor of sound intellect and upright character is that he overlook my stumbles and conceal my lapses. There is no power or strength except through Allah, the High, the Mighty. And may Allah bless, grant peace, honor, and venerate the compassionate, merciful Prophet, and his family and all his companions, and peace be upon the messengers, and praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on three phrases from the opening of the poem: 'his family' (Ālihī), 'his progeny' ('Itratihi), and 'a follower of his path' (Tābi‘ li-Nahjihi). The author first defines 'Ālihī' as those on whom eternal prayer and peace are sent, noting he previously discussed the family. For 'Itratihi', he explains it as the Prophet's household, offering alternative opinions: his wives or his offspring and close kin. For 'Tābi‘ li-Nahjihi', he expands that it includes any follower of the Prophet's way, even if only by faith—thus including sinful believers. He notes the purpose of this generality is to encompass all in the supplication, as it is superior. Next, he analyzes the phrase 'from his nation' (min Ummatihi), stating it refers to the 'nation of response' (those who accept his call). He emphasizes that this restriction is for clarification, not to exclude genuine followers who might not seem part of his nation, because any follower of his law is necessarily from his nation due to the universality of his mission. He then addresses a potential objection: could someone follow his law without being from his nation, like Jesus when he descends? The author answers that at that time Jesus will be from his nation. The benefit is to affirm generality, preventing the mistaken idea that only the first three centuries are meant, drawing an analogy with Quran 6:38. He concludes the commentary, offering supplications for acceptance and forgiveness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'from his nation' limits the prayer to only those who are from the Prophet's nation in a restrictive sense, excluding followers like Jesus who are not originally from his nation.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'from his nation' is descriptive of the reality (those who respond to his call) and not restrictive. Any follower of his law is from his nation due to the universality of his mission, and Jesus at the end of time will be from his nation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the intended scope of 'follower of his path' (تابع لنهجه)?
A) Only the righteous and sinless believers
B) Only the Prophet's family and companions
C) Any follower of the Prophet's way, including sinful believers
D) Only those from the first three generations of Islam

Key Takeaway
The prayer for the Prophet includes all who follow his path, encompassing believers from all times, as affirmed by the universal scope of his mission and the descriptive nature of 'from his nation'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that the Prophet's prayer covers you even when you sin and fall short? How does this affect your hope and motivation to return to His path?
?
The author includes Jesus as part of this ummah in the future. Is there someone you consider outside the ummah that you should now include in your good thoughts and prayers?
?
Are you a follower of his path in action, or do you only claim faith? What is one concrete change you can make today to align your life with his way?
